THE ACTS OF THE SESSION
(FIRST SESSION: THIRD PERIOD)
Air Navigation (Charges) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 123 of 1981)An Act to amend the Air Navigation (Charges) Act 1952, and for related purposes.
Apple and Pear Export Charge Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 147 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Apple and Pear Export Ch~rge Act 1976.
Apple and Pear Levy Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 1460f 1981)- An Act to amend the Apple and Pear Levy Act 1976.
Appropriation Act (No.1) 1981-82 (Act No. 1640f 1981)An Act to appropriate certain sums out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund for the service of the year ending on 30 June 1982.
Appropriation Act (No.2) 1981-82 (Act No. 165 of 1981)An Act to appropriate a sum out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund for certain expenditure in respect of the year endingon 30June 1981.
Australian Apple and Pear Corporation Amendment Act (No.2) 1981 (Act No. 145 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Apple and Pear Corporation Act 1973.
Australian Capital Territory Stamp Duty Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 124 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Stamp Duty Act 1969.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Cheques) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 126 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Tax (Cheques) Act 1969.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Insurance Business) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 125 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Tax (Insurance Business) Act 1969.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Insurance Business) Amendment Act (No.2) 1981 (Act No. 130 of 1981)- An Act to amend section 6 of the Australian Capital Territory Tax (lnsurance Bus;ness) Act 1969.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Life Insurance Business) Act 1981 (Act No. 128 of 1981)An Act relating to the imposition of tax in respect of certain life insurance business having a connection with the Australian Capital Territory.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Vehicle Registration) Act 1981 (Act No. 129 of 1981)An Act relating to the imposition of tax in respect of the registration of certain vehicles in the Australian Capital Territory.
Australian CapitalTerritory Taxation (Administration) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 127 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Taxation (Administration) Act 1969.
Australian Meat and Live-stock Corporation Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 150 of 1981)- An Act to amend section 50 of the Austrillian Meat and Live-stock Corporation Act 1977.
Australian National University Amendment Act (No.2) 1981 (Act No. 1660f 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Natiollal University Act 1946.
Broadcasting Stations Licence Fees Act 1981 (Act No. 168 of 1981)An Act to amend the Broadcasting Stations Licence Fees Act 1964, and for related purposes.
Census and Statistics Amendment Act (No.2) 1981 (Act No. 177 of 1981)An Act to amend the Census and Statistics Act 1905, and for related purposes.
Commerce (Trade Descriptions) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 151 of 1981)An Act to amend the Commerce (Trade Descriptions) Act 1905 in relation to the exportation of food.
Commonwealth Grants Commission Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 173 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Commonwealth Grants Commission Act 1973.
Companies and Securities Legislation (Miscellaneous Amendments) Act 1981 (Act No. 153 of 1981)- An Act to amend laws relating to companies and securities and for related purposes.
Continental Shelf (Living Natural Resources) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 182 of 1981)An Act to amend the Continental Shelf(Living Natural Resources) Act 1968 to facilitate the collection of levy imposed by the Foreign Fishing Boats Levy Act 1981 and of amounts payable under certain agreements, and for certain other purposes.
Crimes (Currency) Aat 1981 (Act No. 1220f 1981)An Act to make provision with respect to offences connected with the counterfeiting of money or certain kinds of securities and other activities injurious to Australian currency, and for related purposes.
Customs (Unlawful Exportation of Food) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 152 of 1981)- An Act ~to amend the Cllstoms Act 1901 in relation to the exportation of food.

Thursday, 27 August 1981
Mr SPEAKER (Rt Hon. Sir Billy Snedden) took the chair at 10 a.m., and read prayers.
PETITIONS
The Clerk-Petitions have been lodged for presentation as follows and copies will be referred to the appropriate Ministers:
Diabetics: Insulin
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled, the petition of the undersigned Citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
That in view of the recent standardisation of insulin and insulin syringes, having the effect of increasing the cost of both items to the Diabetic in general, and as every insulindependent Diabetic must use the same, daily or twice daily according to medication.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that syringes be made available on the NHS and that insulin prescriptions revert to 10 bottles as previously supplied.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Charles, Mrs Child, Mr Duffy, Mr Hawke, Mr Holding, Dr Jenkins, Mr Barry Jones, Mr Kent, Mr Milton, Dr Theophanous and Mr Willis.
Petitions received.
Trans Australia Airlines
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble Petition of undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
Many Australians depend almost exclusively upon Trans-Australia Airlines for the provision of essential air services.
The Federal Liberal Party policy objective of selling Trans-Australia Airlines within 4 years is contrary to the national interest.
Sale of Trans-Australia Airlines would inevitably result in:
(i) the creation of a private monopoly of major airline services in Australia;
(ii) higher domestic air fares.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the House of Representatives will reject outright any proposal to sell Trans-Australia Airlines.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mrs Child, Mrs Darling, Dr Everingham, Mr Mountford and Mr Wallis..
Petitions received.
Health Care Services
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
That many Australians will be financially disadvantaged by the health changes proposed by the Federal Government, especially the withdrawal of Commonwealth medical benefits

for the uninsured, and the introduction of a means test for free public hospital care;
That the health insurance changes promote inequity, inefficiency and lack of universal coverage;
That the public hospital system and community health programes will be financially disadvantaged by the abdication of Commonwealth responsibility and funding.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the House of Representatives will take all steps necessary to reintroduce a system of health care based on ability to pay, and access to health care based on medical need.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Howe, Mr Les Johnson and Mr Mountford.
Petitions received.
Family Allowances
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in the Parliament Assembled. The humble Petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
I. Because of an anomaly in tax rates a single income familypays nearly $IOa week more tax than a dual income family earniflg the same total amount.
2. Family allowances established in 1976 have not been increased since then, yet over the same period living costs have increased by nearly 60 per cent.
Your petitioners most humbly pray that:
The Parliament should act-
I. To restore the lost value of family allowances and to index them for future price rises.
2. To increase the spouce rebates from $830 to $1,342 per year.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Hodgman and Mr Shipton.

And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.

844 REPRESENTATIVES 27 August 1981

Petitions

Tertiary Education
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in the Parliament Assembled. This Petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth that:
I. The proposal to introduce fees for higher and second degrees will mean that access to post-graduate and recurrent education will be limited to the rich; Australian research and ability to cope with technological change will suffer; this proposal must be seen as a step in the direction of the introduction of fees for all tertiary students.
2. The proposed introduction of a loans scheme for students will mean graduating with a heavy debt or being denied a tertiary education if loans are refused because a student is seen as a "poor risk" (particularly affecting women and those doing non-vocational courses); only the rich will be able to get a tertiary education without course restrictions and the burden of indebtedness.
3. In regard to the proposal to restrict eligibility for TEAS and the foreshadowing of reviewing TEAS levels only in the light of the availability of loans, it should be noted that any further downgrading of TEAS, which is already inadequate, can only further limit access to education.
4. The proposed restriction of Adult Secondary Assistance to those needing qualifications for employment will severely restrict access to a full high school education and necessary bridging education to tertiary education for those who left school early.
5. The proposal not to fund 30 Colleges of Advanced Education if they are not rationalised is an attack on teacher education. This attack will create teacher shortages in the mid I980s, restrict access to education now and endanger the future quality of teaching available to Australian children.
Your petitioners therefore pray that:
(i) post-secondary tuition fees not be introduced for any courses or for any category of students.
(ii) that the TEAS scheme be extended so as to more fully meet the real financial needs of students.
(iii) that a comprehensive loans scheme not be introduced. (iv) that there be no restriction of Adult Secondary
Assistance. (v) that the proposal not to fund 30 CAEs unless they are
rationalised be rescinded.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mrs Child.
Petition received.
Toxic Shock Syndrome
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned showeth that your petitioners are gravely concerned by the threats to women's health and lives posed by Toxic Shock Syndrome, and urge the following steps to be taken as a matter of urgency:
(I) Tampons, sanitary napkins, vaginal deodorants and similar sanitary products to be re-c1assified under Therapeutic Agents (not under "Cosmetics" as at present), in order to bring them under the scrutiny of the Australian Drug Evaluation Committee. We believe manufacture must be under the strictest hygienic conditions.
(2) After re-c1assification, we urge an immediate government-funded inquiry into all aspects of manufacture and content of these products. This must lead to: (i) contents of product and instructions for correct usage to be shown on packaging; (ii) removal of synthetic fibres, chemicals or ingredients that are potentially or actually harmful to users.

(3) Allocation of extra funding for ongoing research by the National Health and Medical Research Council into these products, results of this research to be made public to ensure all health workers and potential users are fully informed. This campaign of public education would necessitate multilingual distribution of the information where appropriate.
Your petitioners, therefore, humbly request that Parliament take immediate and positive action to avert possible threats posed to women's health by implementation of the above measures.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mrs Child.
Petition received.

Rights of Disabled
To the Honourable The Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in'the Parliament Assembled. The humble Petition of undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
I. The failure of the Federal Government to seek information from the census re disabled people-i.e. partial. or total disability-to enable for adequate planning of, and for, types of services needed by disabled people to be undertaken.
2. The failure of the Federal Government to lead the way in employment of disabled people by employing a greater number of such people within the Commonwealth Public Service-note the small number currently employed within the Service.
3. The wastage of the Federal Government funds perpetuating large, bureaucratic institutions and organisations where there are often few disabled people on their boards and little emphasis is placed on consumer participation in decision making.
4. The failure of the Federal Government to encourage independence of disabled people, or to develop 'normalised' support systems-attendant care.
5. The failure of the Federal Government to provide 'noninstitutionalised' specialised accommodation for those people who would not be able to benefit from, or cope with attendant care.
6. Failure by the Federal Government to alter, or create a 'disability' allowance means test free for all disabled peoplethis would assist with necessary equipment and mobility costs.
7. Failure to provide a national compensation scheme thus saving time and enabling people to be automatically covered in the event of a disability.
8. Failure by the Federal Government to abolish tariffs on all equipment available to the disabled-e.g., Optacon.
9. Mismanagement of FederaII.Y.D.P. Funds-e.g., nonreceipt by some States of funds through unresolved negotiations.
Your Petitioners therefore humbly pray that immediate action be taken to remedy the above stated and that the Federal Government recognise that disabled people are citizens who should have equal rights.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Mountford.
Petition received.

And yourpetitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Sinclair.

FOOD PURITY STANDARDS: SALMONELLA POISONING
Notice of Motion
Mr JOHN BROWN (Parramatta)-I give notice that, on the next day of sitting, I shall move:
That this House(I) notes with alarm that 350 cases of salmonella poison-
ing have been reported in Australia over, the last month, including most regrettably the death of a seven-year-old child;

(2) condemns the failure of the Government to implement the recommendations of the National Health and Medical Research Council for a national code of food purity standards with legislative power to demand recall of dangerous products, and
(3) demands that the Minister for Business and Consumer Affairs use his undoubted powers under section 62 of the Trade Practices Act to implement legislation immediately to minimise any similar outbreaks of such disease.
QUESTIONS WITHOUT NOTICE
SALES TAX: SEAT BELTS AND OTHER CAR SAFETY DEVICES
Dr KLUGMAN-My question is directed to the Minister for Transport. I ask him, when answering it, to use the knowledge that he gained when he was Minister for Health. How can a responsible government impose a 17+ per cent sales tax on seat belts and, other car safety devices for children and adults, but not increase excise duty on alcoholic beverages and tobacco products?
Mr HOWARD-The question clearly relates to revenue matters in the' last Budget. The honourable gentleman completely neglects to acknowledge the undoubted fact that already in this country extremely high levels of excise duty are imposed on alcohol and tobacco. His question implies that in some way, as a result of the Budget, the items that he mentioned are more heavily taxed than are beer or cigarettes. That is a palpable nonsense. The level of taxation through the excise on those items is already extremely heavy. Whenever there is a broadening of the taxation base, it is inevitable that taxation will apply to items in respect of which in isolation a case can be made for no taxation at all to be applied. Many components which make up the safety features of a motor vehicle are already subject to the general rate of taxation which has now been increased from 15 per cent to 17t per cent. In that respect the analogy with the seat belts is entirely relevant.

HEALTH FUNDING ARRANGEMENTS
Mr LIONEL BOWEN-I wish to ask the Minister for Health a supplementary question arising from the answer just given. Is it a fact that his Government has altered the cost sharing arrangements in respect of health so that, whereas previously the Commonwealth was prepared to pay half the costs, on this occasion it has decided that New South Wales will have to accept a block grant? Is it not a fact that if the Commonwealth Government had honoured its commitments to New South Wales in accordance with previous arrangements New South Wales would have received $lOOm more in this financial year for health alone?
Mr MacKELLAR-The specific answer is that the Commonwealth's contributions are not being made available in block grants. They are part of the recurrent tax sharing arrangements. They are being specified for the health area but they are part of the general tax sharing arrangements. As I have pointed out to the House on a number of occasions, the basis for the funding is last year's expenditure, escalated by 10 per cent, minus a proportion of the increased revenue which will become available to the States resulting from the imposition of charges agreed to and put in place by the New South Wales Government.

NUCLEAR WEAPONS: ISRAEL
Mr HOWE-Is the Minister for Foreign Affairs aware of a report in the Jerusalem Post of 19 to 25 July this year to the effect that the United Nations Secretary General, Dr Waldheim, is expected to release soon a report on a study by an expert panel which concludes that Israel can make nuclear weapons? Is he aware that the report apparently concludes that Israel may already have enough weapons-grade material to make several bombs comparable with the one dropped on Nagasaki in 1945 and that the Dimona reactor alone could have produced enough weaponsgrade plutonium for 10 to 15 weapons? Is he aware that Israel has refused to ratify the Nuclear Non-proliferation Treaty and has refused to allow international inspection of its facilities, including inspection by the International Atomic Energy Agency? In view of the. extraordinarily grave implications these circumstances create for the Middle East and, indeed, for international stability, can the Foreign Minister state whether the Government has undertaken or will undertake initiatives aimed at encouraging Israel to ratify the Nuclear Non-proliferation Treaty and allow appropriate international agency inspection of its nuclear facilities?
Mr STREET-I am not aware of the particular report to which the honourable gentleman has referred but, as is well known, Australia is a

strong supporter of the Nuclear Nonproliferation Treaty and urges all countries to ratify it and observe its provisions. I agree that the spread of nuclear weapons, whether to Israel or any other country in the region, has grave implications for the future of peace in the Middle East. Australia would certainly urge all countries in the area to become signatories to the NPT.
SALES TAX
Mr IAN CAMERON-I ask the Treasurer why he and his Department have held until I January 1982 the levelling of the 2t per cent sales tax on those items that have not previously been taxed, yet imposed on the night of 18 August an increase to 17t per cent on grain trucks, some mining vehicles, aircraft for private use and oils and greases which were not taxed previously, without any notice to people engaged in the purchase of these items. I ask whether he will allow that section which is asked to pay the 17t per cent sales tax to wait until 1 January 1982 before it is levied.
Mr SPEAKER-I call the Treasurer.
Mr Ian Cameron-Mr Speaker, I also extend an invitation to honourable members of the Labor Opposition to attend the Birdsville races to allow them to cool down.
Mr SPEAKER-The honourable member for Maranoa will resume his seat.
Mr HOWARD-As I think I have explained to the House on a couple of occasions, three categories of sales tax changes arise out of the Budget. The first category of sales tax changes involves an increase of 2t per cent in the three previous rates, the second batch of changes involves the removal of a series of anomalies and inconsistencies in the application of the sales tax law and the third category involves a broadening of the sales tax base to bring to sales tax for the first time a wide range of items. All these matters will be the subject of debate in this House later today. I simply say to the honourable gentleman that I have had some research carried out by the Australian Taxation Office as to the practice in these areas. I can assure him and the House that on every previous occasion-I refer in particular to 1967 and 1968 when a significant number of anomaly changes of a similar kind to the ones to which the honourable gentleman refers were undertaken-those changes were imposed with effect from the night of the announcement. It has been the long-standing practice when increases in rates occur for those increases to date from the time the increase is announced. That has been the invariable practice with incre~es in excise. It has been the invariable practice with variations in the

854 REPRESENTATIVES 27 August 1981

Questions Without'Notice

rate of sales tax. Equally, if we are changing the sales tax law to bring to taxation for the first time items which are similar in character to other items that are subject to sales tax, it is not unreasonable that they also operate from the date of announcement.
In respect of the particular example to which the honourable gentleman referred, people who are at present not paying taxation on trucks used for the particular purposes, as I think the honourable gentleman will acknowledge, enjoy an advantage over people who have to pay the 17t per cent and have always had to pay it since tax has applied to those trucks which are used for other purposes. It is because of that inconsistency, that advantage-that privilege perhaps-that the Government decided to make the change. The methodology adopted has been the methodology adopted by successive governments and successive Treasurers of both political persuasions in announcing and implementing sales taxes and indirect tax changes.
TAX AVOIDANCE
Mr MOUNTFORD-I draw the Treasurer's attention to an advertisement which has appeared in recent issues of the Australian Financial Review and in which Mr Peter Clyne invites readers to purchase six tape cassettes at a cost of $150: Such cassettes explain to people how they can dodge new laws on tax avoidance, how not to pay tax until 1995, how to cheat legally, et cetera. I ask the Treasurer whether he will approve of the Commissioner of Taxation acquiring a set of the cassettes so as to learn more of the ineffectiveness of the Government's tax avoidance measures and then to be in a better position to advise the Government on effective counter-measures. Alternatively, will the Treasurer agree to the commissioning of a taxation consultant to advise the Commissioner of Taxation as to the best means of ensuring that future legislation is effective so that it places an equal obligation on all Australians to pay a fair share of tax?
Mr HOWARD-I do not believe the Commissioner of Taxation needs either my authority or my encouragement to obtain a copy of those tapes or, indeed, otherwise find out what advice Mr Clyne and others are giving in this area. I will make two other comments. Firstly, the Government has already-I think it is important that this is said in response to the honourable gentleman's question-sought the advice of people beyond the Australian Taxation Office in drafting tax avoidance legislation. In fact, the new section 260 of the Income Tax Assessment Act was the result of the

combined efforts of the Taxation Office, the Commonwealth Atttorney-General's Department, two extremely competent and highly regarded private legal counsel who are expert in taxation matters as well as the Government's own taxation advisory committee which is chaired by the managing partner of one of the mostrep~table accounting firms in Australia and includes a number of lawyers and accountants who. are highly regarded throughout Australia for their technical competence in the taxation area.
I am very pleased to say to the honourable member for Banks that in every respect his suggestion about the Commissioner of Taxation engaging people in the private sector who are expert in these matters has already been anticipated and taken up. I believe that the new taxation laws will be effective. That is a view which is shared very widely throughout legal and accounting circles. I believe the new section 260 or Part IVA will be effective. I share totally the view of the honourable member for Banks that it is desirable and in the interests of tax equity that the section be effective. That is why the Government went to such extraordinary pains to work out an effective section and why we consulted and engaged a wide variety of views, not just views within the Australian Taxation Office.
NATIONAL AUSTRALIA DAY COMMITTEE
Mr HODGES-My question is directed to the Minister for Home Affairs and Environment. Is it a fact that former world champion runner Herb Elliott faces dismissal by the Government from his job as chairman of the National Australia Day Comrriittee as was widely reported in the Press this week? Is there any truth in the report that Mr Elliott's dismissal is connected with the selection of Professor Manning Clark as Australian of the Year and that Professor Clark was regarded by Liberal conservatives-as an unsuitable choice? Is it a fact that the National Australia Day Committee does not even choose the Australian of the Year, nor does it have a representative ort the panel which makes that selection? Has not the Government shown its confidence in Mr Elliott by recently reappointing him as a commissioner of the Australian Broadcasting Commission?
Mr WILSON-I have seen the recent Press reports alleging that Herb Elliott was to be sacked as chairman of the National Australia Day Committee. The reports to which the honourable member refers are thoroughly mischievous, they are without foundation, there is no truth in them and I refute them utterly. The National Australia Day Committee was established some two years

Questions Without Notice

27 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 855

ago. Herb Elliott, who is a distinguished and great Australian, was appointed as its first chairman. He has served the Committee with distinction and his work on the Committee and the work of the Committee have encouraged a greater awareness about Australia's national day. The National Australia Day Committee comeS under my ministerial responsibility. As a result, I have been in regular communication with Herb-Elliott.. Some time ago Herb Elliott drew to my attention the fact that he had recently been appointed as general manager of a sports store in Melbourne and that as a result of his new responsibilities he would not be able, following the expiration of his present appointment, to continue as chairman of the National Australia Day Committee. I was very disappointed to receive this information, but I accepted the situation. The Government has been most appreciative of the leadership that Herb Elliott has shown, and it is thoroughly mischievous of people to put about rumours to try to denigrate people such as Herb Elliott who have served their country well in many ways. I want to put on record the appreciation of the Government for the work Herb Elliott has done on that Committee.
Mr John Brown-Mr Speaker, I raise a point . of order. I wonder whether the Minister will table

Mr WILSON-I make two points in conclusion. The first point is that the Australian-of the Year is not selected by the National Australia Day Committee. That person is selected by a group of independent Australians representing a wide cross section of the community. Secondly, I say that the Government has demonstrated its confidence in Herb Elliott. He was recently appointed, as the member for Petrie mentioned in his question,as a member of the Australian Broadcasting Commission.

PERSONAL EXPLANATION
Mr SPEAKER-The honourable member for Barker has indicated tome that he wishes to make a personal explanation.
Mr PORTER (Barker)-I claim to have been misrepresented.
Mr SPEAKER-If the honourable gentleman wishes to proceed with a personal explanation he may do so.
Mr PORTER-I refer to a report on page 32 of yesterday's Advertiser headed 'Sack Rodda over prawn control, ALP demands'.Th~ article ~eports a debate in the South Australian Parliament where the Australian Labor Party Opposition moved a motion of no confidence in the State Minister of Fisheries, such motion being defeated on the floor of the House. In the course of the debate, as reported in the Advertiser, .improper motives were attributed to me. The subject of the motion involved a move'earlier this year by the Gulf of St Vincent prawn fishermen to force a closure of the neighbouring Investigator Strait prawn fishery. This move was based on the belief that the financial returns to the Gulf prawn fishermen would increase if the Investigator Strait prawn fishermen were thrown out of their own area.
The Gulf fishery is a State fishery whilst the Investigator Strait fishery is under Commo~wealth control and is fished by some of my constituents. For many reasons I believed it was unfair to force a closure of the Investigator Strait fishery, th~reby directly affecting the livelihood of fishermen in my area without any action being taken by the Gulf fishermen. During the debate in the South Australian parliament, Mr Keneally, an ALP State member, is reported to have alleged that my action was not based on a concern for my constituents but rather because a member of the Kangaroo Island community had made contributions to the Liberal Party of Australia. Such a suggestion is utterly false. I have no idea who from Kangaroo Island contributed to Liberal party funds. In fact, I conscientiously avoid having anything to do with the handling of party funds. The action I took on behalf of the island fishermen and their community was because I believed they were being unfairly treated, and the allegations <:>f this poor little Keneally man are totally wIthout foundation.

AUSTRALIAN INSTITUTE OF ABORIGINAL STUDIES
Mr VINER (Stirling-Minister for Industrial Relations)-For the information of honourable members, I present the report of .the Australian Institute of Aboriginal Studies to the Minister for Aboriginal Affairs (Senator Peter Baume) on the social impact of uranium mining on the Aborigines of the Northern Territory-for the period I October 1981 to 31 March 1981-together with the text of a statement by the Mi~ister for Aboriginal Affairs relating to the report.
Motion (by Mr Sinclair) proposed:
That the House take note of the papers.
Mr HOLDING (Melbourne Ports) (11.l3) -In speaking very quickly to the motion and the

860 REPRESENTATIVES 27 August 1981

Institute ofAboriginal Studies

accompanying statement of the Minister for Aboriginal Affairs (Senator Peter Baume). I would like to draw the attention of the House to the importance of the Government having in the East AlIigator Rivers region a monitoring team which is capable of working with Aboriginal communities and making ongoing reports and assessments, not merely to the Minister but also to the House, as to the impact of uranium mining and mining generally in that region on Aboriginal communities. The Minister's statement indicates a number of points on which he felt the report showed some very positive achievements. I had the benefit of being in this region for some three and a half weeks during the recess. I would like to pay a tribute to the people involved for and on behalf of the Australian Institute of Aboriginal Studies for the work they are doing in the field. I was able to observe at first hand their relationship with the Aboriginal community and with the mining community. I believe they are fulfilling a very important job, under often very difficult and isolated circumstances, for Aboriginal residents in that area.
There are a number of aspects, however, which I believe the report does not cover at this stage and which ought to be drawn to the attention of the House. Firstly, there is genuine concern amongst traditional Aboriginal owners and affected Aboriginal communities at the increasing pressure which is being placed on those communities by the various meetings being called in respect of the Pancontinental Mining Ltd project and the Koongarra project. Some Aboriginal leaders spend more time at meetings than the average parliamentarian. I think this is producing many problems for them and is placing them under considerable stress.
If one looks at the recommendations of the Fox .report one sees that Fox was adamant that if mining were to continue in this region it ought to take place slowly and in a way which minimised disruption to the lives of traditional Aboriginal communities. If we look at the intensity at which these negotiations are proceeding, we will see clearly that the actual recommendations of the Fox report and the spirit of that report are not being honoured. I believe the Government and the Minister have a responsibility to see that, in considering the alternatives that are being put to them, the Aboriginal community in the east AlIigator Rivers region has an opportunity to consider these matters in a traditional Aboriginal way and that it is not jammed, as it is at the moment, into making decisions.
Fox found also that traditional owners, if they had their choice, would choose to have no

uranium mining at all, but he pointed out that they felt genuinely that they would be unable to prevent it. My observations in the region confirm that view. I think it is still their view. It is a matter of concern to me that the Minister says-I believe genuinely-that traditional owners do have an alternative, they do have a right to say that they will have no mining at all in the region. That is not the position as it is taking place on the ground. The negotiations that have taken place and the advice that has been given to traditional owners has been given in such a way that they do not believe, and they are not being told, that one of their very real options is a no mining option. I believe that if the Minister seeks the views of those who are living in the community and perhaps seeks a further interim report from the team from the Australian Institute of Aboriginal Studies he would find that the statements that I have made would be confirmed.
Grave problems are confronting Aboriginal communities for the future. Some of those problems have been highlighted by this report. There have been a number of developments in recent times, the most recent one being the leak at the Nabarlek mine which must give concern to the Government. Often all sorts of promises are made by mining companies, but when it comes to fulfiIling those promises they are unable to do so. I think the report also notes, in fairness to Nabarlek, that certainly its promise to restrict the sale and supply of alcohol in the region has been honoured. From my observation that is very much the case. I believe that Nabarlek in this regard has made all attempts that could be made in order not to impinge upon the traditional life style of Aboriginal communities in the region.
One other matter that is worthy of comment is that the report highlights again that, although mining has taken place, very few employment opportunities are being offered to-Aboriginal communities; That does not mean that the mining companies are not concerned to make those opportunities available; it is just that the nature of the work opportunities that are available cannot always be filled satisfactorily by Aboriginal people. However, it is interesting to note, as the Minister notes, that very real progress has been made by the Australian National Parks and Wildlife Service in its employment of Aboriginals. That is a very positive feature. I believe that Service and its Director are to be commended for the way in which they have involved Aboriginals, particularly younger Aboriginals, in training schemes which are giving them a very useful and positive role and satisfactory employment within their locality.

Institute ofAboriginal Studies

27 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 861

I believe one aspect of that ought to be drawn to the attention of the Government as a matter of some seriousness. This Government entered into contractural relations with the traditional owners when the. K~adu National Park was established. The Di_wL" of the National Parks and Wildlife Service ~gned leases for and on behalf of the Commonwealth of Australia under which the Commonwealth undertook very substantial obligations. Not the least of those obligations-this is a matter of law-was that the Commonwealth would not employ anyone from the Northern Territory Service without the approval of the Northern Land Council and traditional owners.
The razor gang has recommended the handing over of the administration of the park to the Northern Territory Administration. That decision was taken in isolation. It was taken without any reference at all to the Northern Land Council or to the traditional owners. The Public Service Board is now endeavouring to implement those recommedations. The net effect of that is simply this: If the Public Service Board is allowed to proceed in that way it will mean that the Commonwealth is not honouring its contractual obligations under the lease and the undertakings that have been made and signed as a legal agreement by the Director of the National Parks and Wildlife Service are not being honoured. I think it is very important that when a senior public servant enters into legal arrangements with any group in the community the Commonwealth stand behind both the spirit and the letter of those agreements.
What is now occurring is that the Director of National Parks, having been made party to legally binding leases and the terms of those leases, is now being obliged, by Public Service Board decisions to evade obligations which were undertaken clearly and willingly for and on behalf of the Commonwealth. Such action is bad enough if it occurs in.a normal commercial relationship but it is even worse when it occurs when we are dealing with traditional Aboriginal land owners. They are entitled to believe that the Government, its public servants and the Minister will honour contracts and leases which have been freely entered into for and on behalf of the Commonwealth. I thank the House for its courtesy.
Debate (on motion by Dr Edwards) adjourned.

INTER-PARLIAMENTARY UNION
Report
Dr EDWARDS (Berowra)-by leave-I present the report of the Australian Delegation to the Inter-Parliamentary Union Conferences in 1980.
Ordered that the report be printed.
Dr EDWARDS-I seek leave to make a short statement in connection with the report.
Leave granted.
Dr EDWARDS-The Inter-Parliamentary Union dates back to 1889. It is an international organisation based on national groups established in the 'parliaments' of the member countries, of which in October 1980 there were some eightynine. The headquarters of the InterParliamentary Union are in Geneva. The membership includes most countries of the West and, wait for it, the Soviet Union and the Eastern Bloc, Japan, the Third World, and newly-emergent countries. The aims of the Union are the promotion of personal contact between members of all parliaments, the advancement of the work of international peace and co-operation and the strengthening of parliamentary institutions. Perhaps the best shorthand description of the InterParliamentary Union is that it is the parliamentary counterpart of the United Nations. The United Nations is the world-wide organisation of governments and commands resources and world attention accordingly. The Inter-Parliamentary Union is the world-wide organisation of parliaments and parliamentarians and while less well known is a significant world body which should

862 REPRESENTATIVES 27 August 1981

Institute ofAboriginal Studies

receive the interest and support of all honourable members of this Parliament.
Mr Speaker, some people would dismiss the Inter-Parliamentary Union ,as largely a talk-fest. In fact, the Union is widely regarded as a significant forum for the exchange of views in a number of areas and particularly for the exchange of views and some meeting of minds on the central matter of East-West relations-that is, relations between the super-powers and the central issues of the strategic balance, arms control and dIsarmament negotiations. As one instance, it is a matter of record that the Inter-Parliamentary Union made a notable contribution, springing from a resolution of the 59th Conference in Paris in September 1971, to the formulation and acceptance by governments of the Helsinki Agreements to facilitate the rapprochement and cooperation between European countries with different political and economic systems. Many of the Inter-Parliamentary Union suggestions were included in the Helsinki Final Act which was adopted in 1975. The Inter-Parliamentary Union has been active in its implementation since then.
I stress that sort of achievement as a positive restilt springing from the wide and divergent memberships of this Inter-Parliamentary, so-called, Union. As I put it in my Foreword to the report, this is the counterpoise so to speak, to the difficulties for many of us in approaching this organisation from a Western standpoint where there is a measure of unreality and incongruity about an organisation of 'parliaments' which, as I have said, includes most of the countries of the Eastern Bloc. Indeed, it includes just about all of them with the sort of competition that has been going on in recent years to recruit new members ~o the organisation supportive of their particular ideological standpoint. With the new admissions at the Manila spring meeting this year the membership is now over 90 countries. There is something unreal or incongruous from a Western standpoint about an organisation of 'parliaments' which includes all the countries of the Eastern Bloc as well as other states where there is no multi-party parliamentary democracy as we know it. Perhaps about 30 countries would have a democratically elected parliament and government, as we know it.
lt is still-indeed, all the more so-important for Australia to participate in providing general support for the Western presence since the Soviet Union exploits the organisation to the full as an instrument of foreign policy and for the dissemination of its propaganda. I further state in my foreword that the Inter-Parliamentary Union is also a context for the furtherance of the so-called North-South dialogue, Third World issues. I say

'so called' because the 'North' means the West and Japan and not the also relatively rich Soviet Union and its allies. In this context Australia as a rich but commodity exporting country, and thus having interests in common with both developed and developing countries, has a key role to play. I take this opportunity of identifying myself with and supporting unreservedly the initiatives of the Prime Minister (Mr Malcom Fraser) and the Government in the North-South context.
I add only this comment, Mr Speaker: The interrelationship of East-West and North-South issues is starkly brought out when the two are debated together, as at the 1980 InterParliamentary Union meetings., The massive spending on arms stands in stark, sombre and dramatic contrast to the want and poverty of more than two-thirds of the world's population. The United Nations, the Inter-Parliamentary Union and, more recently and authoritatively, the Brandt Commission and other authorities join in urging a massive increase in assistance to developing countries on humanitarian grounds, yes,. but of course and equally compelling, because it is in our long term political and economic self interest.
As well as the consideration of such major world issues, the Inter-Parliamentary Union also makes a continuing contribution in more specific areas. The most important of these is its work in pursuit of the human rights of parliamentarians, and I particularly commend to members that part of the report dealing with that issue, at page 78 of this present printing.
I report to the Parliament that at the East Berlin meeting last September, I was honoured, and the then and previous Australian delegations and Australia were honoured, by my election to the lOman Executive Committee of. the InterParliamentary Union. I also became Chairman of the 'Ten-plus' group within the Union-that is, the 'west European and other' group, which effectively is the Western caucus within the IPU-for the 1Z month period to the next conference in Havana. In that capacity I was gratified, with your. support and concurrence, Mr Speaker, to extend an invitation to the representatives of the 'Ten-plus' countries to hold their meeting here in Canberra last April prior to this year's IPU spring meeting in Manila. The occasion was counted as a considerable success-with the effect, among others, that the 'Ten-plus' co-ordinated effectively and managed to secure the election of all its candidates for committee positions, including taking one position from the Eastern bloc.

Institute ofAboriginal Studies

27 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 863

I express my thanks to all members of the delegation for their work and support, and in particular the deputy leader, the honourable Charles Jones, the member for Newcastle. Notwithstanding our difference of political persuasion I venture to say that we made a pretty good team, and over the three years of our leadership of the Australian IPU delegation ably assisted--
Mr Les McMahon-That is the end of Charlie.
Dr EDWARDS-Ably assisted by the various members of the delegations from time to time We made the Australian presence strongly felt.
Mr SPEAKER-I think the honourable member for Newcastle insists on a withdrawal.
Dr EDWARDS-Mr Speaker, I refuse. I hope you do not put it to the test. I particularly appreciated his support and advice in the matter of my election to the Executive and also the conduct of my duties as chairman of the lO-plus because that involved a great deal of head counting and tactical election know-how of which he has considerable experience, gained, I would say, in a rather rougher context than the IPU. On behalf of all members of the delegation, I also express our appreciation for the very high standard of advice and assistance received from Miss Sue Boyd, the adviser to the delegation, and Mr H. C. Nicholls, the secretary. Miss Boyd is about to leave the job with the IPU delegation to take up a posting in New York. We wish her well and every success in that role.
I conclude by saying that I thank the Parliament for the privilege of serving as leader of the Inter-Parliamentary Union delegation during the thirty-first Parliament. My term as leader expired at the election last October and I wish my successor, my colleague the honourable Ian Robinson, an equally rewarding term of office as he takes over for the term of the present Parliament.
Mr CHARLES JONES (Newcastle)-by leave-At this point I congratulate the leader of the delegation, the honourable member for Berowra (Dr Edwards) on his election as the Australian representative on the executive on the Inter-Parliamentary Union. It is a position which I believe the country, not the person, holds; at the same time the honourable member for Berowra did a good job as the leader of the delegation in the conduct of affairs of the IPU over the last three years. I believe it bears out the point that I have been making, both in the Parliament when reports have been dealt with and in the executive of the IPU, that there has to be some form of continuity of representation, at the IPU, both from the Government and from the Opposition, it

should not just be a hit and miss arrangement. We should do what a lot of other countries are doing: They have almost permanent delegates there who establish credibility to represent their countries. The honourable member for Berowra has very rightly drawn attention to the fact that the IPU is the second largest international organisation of its type and, as far as parliamentary representation is concerned, it is the largest organisation of its type. Representatives from different countries meet, talk, debate, argue and put forward different points of view concerning the issues confronting the world today.
No longer do the delegates regard the meetings as a jaunt. If they go they should go prepared and should put forward a certain point of view. The Australian delegation is probably unique in the sense that the honourable member for Berowra and other members from the Government represent their party and we represent the Opposition in this place and we put forward our point of view on certain issues. There is nothing more positive than the case of the resolutions regarding East Timor which I moved expressing my point of view and the point of view of the party of which I ,am a member. As a result of putting that point of view-it may sound a little strange-the decolonisation bloc put me on the drafting committee. I believe I was instrumental in stirring up the Portuguese delegation to the point of openly and positively supporting the argument for the right of independence and self determination of the people of East Timor. The Government had a contrary point of view. This is what astounded the delegates. I spoke and put forward a positive point of view for the decolonisation and the right of self determination. The representative of the Government then put the Government's point of view. This is what they could not understand. I think one of the things that is lacking from the IPU is the right of members of one delegation to put forward contrary points of view. It is interesting to note that the United Nations is still putting pressure on Indonesia togrant the right of self determination to the people of Timor. That is in line with the point of view that I have beim putting. I also note in the delegation's report to the United Nations on the Western Sahara Polisario Front that this is a matter in regard to which the leader of the delegation and I put forward different points of view. I draw the attention of the honourable member for Berowra to the fact that his Government is now supporting the Polisario Front in its right to self determination in the Western Sahara dispute.

Institute ofAboriginal Studies

This conference enables members of the same delegation attending the meeting to express differing points of view. I think it should be continued. I
would hate like hell ever to think that this Parliament would restrict the right of an individual to put a certain point of view at the conference. I found the conferences most interesting. It afforded members the opportunity to talk to other people and hear other points of view, For example, the Irish delegation used to present a point of view on the situation in Northern Ireland and the British delegation used to defend what had been going on.
This type of matter is raised from time to time as a side issue. I asked that a matter about Socialist International be included in the report; I note that it was not. My party is a member of SocialistInternational, which used to have at every conference a dinner or a luncheon at which prominent members of Socialist International would be in attendance and in which a welcome would be extended, particularly by the home State, to all the visiting delegates. It is interesting to note the situation in the German Democratic Republic, as it calls itself, when permission was sought-I was one of the delegates who sought permission-to hold a meeting of Socialist International in the Parliament building in East Berlin in which the
conference was being held. In all the other countries in which we had met these meetings had been allowed. Permission was refused on this occasion..
In straight-out defiance of this refusal we met. Admittedly this meeting had to take place in a public place-in a tea room that was normally available to members. We let it be. known loud and clear what we were going to do. We met there and talked about things concerning the various democratic socialist countries and unanimously carried a resolution condemning the actions of the South Korean Government in its persecution of Kim Dae Jung, the socialist Opposition leader in that country. These sorts of things go on when delegates are permitted to attend conferences such as the IPU and to mix with parliamentarians from other places. This gets results, as was shown,jn the case of Singapore and the persecution of people who claimed to be members of Parliament.
I support the contents of the report and again congratulate the leader of the delegation for his election to the executive. As he said, it was a team effort to achieve that. I thank you, Mr Speaker, for permitting the delegation to extend an invitation to the body known as the nine-plus~it is now the 1O-plus-to hold its pre-spring meeting in Australia. Even though we have internal differences of opinion on minor matters, we.are in fairly close agreement on the overall issues. It is

important for a body such as the IO-plus to continue to meet. The delegates who attend such meetings have a job to do in the preparation and planning of such meetings. We know what happened on one occasion when the meeting did not take place. Quite frankly, we were a rabble until such time as the nine-plus got together and started to talk about tactics to counter the Russian bloc, which is the only way to describe it. The Russians certainly dominate it. Whatever they want is what the others fall in line with. I thank you, .Mr Speaker, on behalf of the delegation, for extending the invitation to the meeting in Canberra. Admittedly, the previous delegation of which I was a member extended the invitation, but at least the meeting was held here this year.
DIAMOND MARKETING POLICY
Discussion of MaUer of Public Importance
Mr SPEAKER-I have received a letterfrom the honourable member for Blaxland (Mr Keating) proposing that a definite matter of public importance be submitted to the House for discussion, namely:
The Fraser Government's railure to develop a diamond marketing policy ror Australia, particularly with respect to the Ashton Joint Venture.
I call upon those members who approve of the proposed discussion to rise in their places.
, More than the number ofmembers required by the Standing Orders having risen in their places-
Mr KEATING (Blaxland) (11.44)-One would have hoped that, with the proposing Of such a serious topic, the Government would have had the decency to have it answered by a minister, notably the Oeputy Prime Minister and Minister -for Trade and Resources (Mr Anthony). I appreciate that he is Acting Prime Minister at the moment, but that should not deter him from meeting his responsibilities in the House and answering on a matter like this. Whenever such a matter was. proposed by the right honourable gentleman when in Opposition he was always answered by the Minister for Minerals and Energy, Mr Connor. The discourtesy which is shown tq the Opposition by the Government in this J-Iouse is really beyond the pale when matters of ministerial accountability are in question. I will not stay for the reply of the honourable member for O'Connor (Mr Tuckey) becauseI just do not regard any contribution he may make as being a serious representation of Government policy. I am at least in the position of being able to put the view of the Opposition.

Diamond Marketing Policy

27 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 865

f Australia has the potential of becoming the largest diamond producer in the world. The Ashton Joint Venture at Argyle will be the first in what may be a number of world ranking diamond projects. There are 50 other known kimberlite :pipes in the same region of Western Australia. Ashton will be a significant new source ofdiamond supply in the world market. As such its size and potential is threatening to other world producers, such as South Africa, Zaire and the Soviet Union. The Oppenheimer group, which controls the diamond syndicate companies of De Beers and Anglo American, is particularly concerned at the emergence of a large low cost producer outside its sphere of influence. De Beers controls over 50 per cent of the world diamond trade and, until recently, controlled almost 80 per cent.
With Australia's newest mineral industry emerging as a potent world force almost at bIrth, the question must be asked: What is the Australian Government doing to develop a diamond marketing strategy? The simple answer. is nothing. What is even more obvious is that the Acting Prime Minister cannot even come intothe House and offer some explanation as to the Government's policy. The negligence of this Government in not involving itself in the marketing options of a new industry of this significance defies explanation. The Acting Prime Minister told the Parliament yesterday that he was not familiar with contractual terms currently being discussed between-the Ashton joint venturers and De Beers and went on to say, in an answer to my colleague the honourable member for Hawker (Mr Jacobi), that the Government would leave it to the joint venture companies to maximise their. returns through their own corporate strategies. He added as a rider that cognisance should be taken of national inter~t considerations.
What is obvious is that the Government has left this most significantdiscovery at Argylean.d the marketing of its produce to the whim of individual companies and the caprice of the De Beers Central Selling Organisation, known as theCSO. One would have thought that the Commonwealth Government would seek to maximise the national wealth from diamonds in the same way as South Africa has with De Beers. But the Fraser Government has not even taken the trouble to gather vital information on the nature of the AK l' diamond pipe and the true market value of the diamonds therein. No survey of the pipe has-been done by either the Western Australian Government or the Commonwealth Government. The Commonwealth Bureau of Mineral Resources, Geology and Geophysics apparently lacks the wherewithal to evaluate the deposit.

Without an adequate information base on the deposit, and the world diamond market, the Commonwealth would be at a complete loss to resist a joint venture contractual marketing proposal, let alone propose some arrangement perceived to be more advantageous to Australia. The Ashton project is expected to come on stream in 1985 and contractual arrangements are now being considered. The De Beers Central Selling Organisation is believed to have offered a long term low price contract to the Ashton joint venturers which is to be concluded by October or November of this year. Such' a contract hastily concluded in a government policy vacuum could have serious long. term implications for the industry and shackle it to South Africa before it has had any opportunity to grow independently. The CSO looms large in any diamond marketing strategy for Australia. An intelligent, well-informed de.- cision has to be made as to whether Australia should commit a substantial proportion of Ashton production to the CSO. Recently the world's largest producer, Zaire, has broken its marketing link with the CSO, as has Angola. The Soviet Union is also moving some of its diamond produce outside the CSO orbit.
At a time when the CSO is declining as a world diamond force, should Australia's fledgling diamond industry be enmeshed in its operations? That is per.tinent. There is no doubt that the CSO offers stability, outlets and guaranteed prices if Australia joins the CSO, but it makes it very much stronger at a time when its own diamond reserves are-declining. Such a decision would virtually commit Ashton's future to De Beers policy. This would be an imprudent course for Australia, though it may be possible to make an arrangement with the CSO which preserves Australian autonomy. The alternative is to take the Zaire course, but this would involve the establishment of a formidable Australian marketing force in the world, and one which would need to be established with expedition. An independent Australian marketing effort has access, competition and time frame problems which may inhibit Australian diamond development. No doubt De Beers would counter such a move with stiff competition designed to bring Australia quickly to its knees. This would be difficult given the low cost nature of our deposits when compared with the high and escalating costs of the African deposits.
The linkage question with the CSO is a fundamental decision which has to be made. The Acting Prime Minister made it obvious yesterday that .neither he nor the Government have even considered this question let alone arrived at any determination. Associated with the CSO decision is

866 REPRESENTATIVES 27 August 1981

Diamond Marketing Policy

the matter of a domestic cutting and polishing industry. The real profits in diamonds are in the processing rather than the mining.
There is no reason why Australia with an abundant indigenous source of diamond should not become one of the major cutting and polishing centres in the world. Australia is forever seeking processing opportunities related to minerals. Diamonds would provide a perfect opportunity. It would be a tragedy for Australia to emerge as a significant force in raw diamond supply and see the real profits of its produce taken offshore with foreign processing. It takes three to five years to trai~ a gem cutter. Within a relatively short space of tIme Australia could have a significant processing industry. Does anyone seriously contend that the Fraser Government has .considered this matter let alone arrived at a policy approach. Like everything else this will be left to market forces, even when the market in this case is manipulated by a monopoly.
~hat I have said so far relating to marketing polIcy presumes that everything is above board and that it would be a matter for decision by the joint venturers and the Commonwealth Government. Well everything in the Ashton project is far from above board. De Beers already have important links with the project. For a start Ashton Mining, which has 38.2 per cent of the project, is dominated by the Malaysian Government and De Beers. The Malaysian Government is itself vulnerable to De Beer policy by virtue of the fact that the CSO has established a large processing facility in Malaysia. There is no possible way that Ashton Mining will respond to anything other than De Beer policy. To reinforce the Opposition's contention that the Fraser Government is negligent in respect of the development issues surrounding Ashton, the Government granted Ashton Mining naturalising status in February. That is, the Government has given this company the status of Australian so that in future it may enjoy all the privileges of a majority-owned Australian company. The effective control aspect was completely ignored. This decision by the Treasurer (Mr Howard) was a travesty of the spirit and intent of the naturalising policy.
Currently 14,000 small Australian shareholders hold 40 per cent of the Ashton stock. Even with another II per cent of Ashton's shareholding in Australian hands how could the Government possibly believe that 14,000 small Australian shareholders could arrive at a boardroom policy which would predominate over that of De Beers and the Malaysian Government sitting at the board with a determined strategy and 49 per cent of the stock. What is the majority 2 per cent of the

equity ~orth in terms of control when 14,000 AustralIan shareholders are unable to formulate and exercise a policy at board level? If the 51 per cent were in the hands of say, the Broken Hill Proprietary Co. Ltd or CSR Ltd with a specific policy then it would be a different matter. But as things stand the Treasurer's decision to grant De Beers and Malaysian Mining naturalising status is a disgrace. How could anyone seriously expect these two interests to respond to the Australian national interest.
The little manoeuvre by Ashton Mining, with the Treasurer's compliance, allows a 60 per cent foreign-owned company to be regarded as 51 per cent Australian. This then allows the project to proceed with what is deemed to be the mandatory 51 per cent Australian equity. So much for Ashton Mining.
The dominant corporate force in the project is Conzinc Riotinto of Australia with a 56.8 per cent interest. It will also manage the project. CRA is a naturalising company which has, to date, met the spirit and the letter of its obligations to the Commonwealth Government in respect of its naturalising status, which includes an all Australian board. CRA is currently 58 per cent owned by Riotinto Zinc Corporation Ltd. It has been alleged that Oppenheimer interests have the largest holding of RTZ stock and as such would have a substantial influence over RTZ and in turn CRA. I have no doubt that the CRA board is motivated by the best interests of its shareholders and Australia. These interests may at times, though, be mutually exclusive. RTZ is still the major CRA shareholder. Nevertheless, one may reasonably expect that the spirit of the RTZ naturalising agreement with the Commonwealth Government will be complied with. One aspect of these agreements was the appointment of an all Australian board. The purpose and intent of having an all Australian board was that when a possible conflict of interest arose, the national sentiments of the Australians would take precedence over the majority RTZ shareholder interest. The Opposition would like to be assured that this position would obtain in relation to the Ashton project. It should also be understood that without naturalising status for CRA, the project on a second count, would fail to comply with the mandatory 51 per cent Australian equity requirement.
Another important element in the corp~rate arrangements of the project is the 5 per cent interest held by Northern Mining Corporation. This is an all Australian company without any De Beers connection. Northern has played a role beyond its small minority interest and has been in dispute

has done but have they discovered any of these things? No. Their spokesman has given us a little story about diamond mining, one which has been in the Press for the last fortnight.
