It is one of the normal practices in a sharemarket battle for a lot of statements to be made. At this time, insufficient credibility can be attached to those statements. It is not unusual in this place to find members of the Opposition complaining bitterly about company directors and others overstating the possibilities of their particular venture-they would have them hung if they possibly could-but because there is a degree of conservatism among the partners, members of the Opposition feel that there is something wrong because it suits them to on this occasion. The State Minister told me that he is keeping the Commonwealth informed and that, in his view, the Commonwealth has quite properly left the negotiations to the States at this time. I agree with him entirely. That is why there are no massive announcements coming out of this Parliament at this time on where we are heading. It is a State issue at this time. It is being handled by a very competent Minister in Peter Jones.
Let us look at some of the other issues. I have mentioned the role of the Commonwealth, in particular in regard to foreign investment. We had quite an interesting comment from the honourable member for Blaxland in that regard when he mentioned a lot of problems relating to the Ashton group. He said that that group owns 38.2 per cent of the joint venture and that Conzinc Riotinto of Australia-a company of which he apparently approves, because he said some nice things about it and its all-Australian board-has 56.8 per cent and is listed as the manager of the venture. In other words, at present a fully Australian board is the manager of the venture. What is so wrong about that? Let us consider diamonds anyway as we know all about them. We have control of export if we choose to use it. The honourable member for Blaxland has told us that we have no such control. At present we are not in the business of exporting diamonds. When the agreement which the Western Australian Government is negotiating, quite correctly and at the proper time, is reached what action this Government

should take will be a lot clearer to it. We are all well and truly aware of how little time it would take us to reinstitute the necessary export control. It is ridiculous to say that what we do not need now is a failure to the Australian public.
I now refer to the De Beers organisation. There is an old statement about diamonds being a girl's best friend. If it were not for De Beers they would not be worth very much. It is no secret that the world has an over-supply of diamonds. They could be dug out at a more rapid rate. It has been pointed out that the Russians choose to sell diamonds through a scheme which must be completely anathema to their politics. They do so because they know that, if they do not, the price will fall because of over-supply. It is interesting to note what the De Beers organisation said in its 1980 report to its shareholders. I do not think it was trying to fool the Opposition. the report stated:
In the middle of 1980 the boom conditions experienced during the late 1970's came to an end. Since the end of 1980 the market in cutting centres has further weakened and it has become necessary for the CSO in accordance with established policy to reduce the offerings to the market substantially in order to maintain stability. Stocks of rough diamonds in the trade are not excessive but in the view of the large stocks of polished diamonds the CSO as I have mentioned has undertaken its traditional function of reducing the quantity of goods offered to its customers in order to assist the market to recover its balance.
Let us remember that De Beers is a selling cartel. This country knows a fair bit about buying cartels. Where might we be if CRA and other companies did not go to the big man first? Let me stress that there is no agreement. There will be no agreement. The first agreement will be with Western Australia. I am sure that our Minister and his Department know what is going on and that we will have the necessary controls when we need them. If the Opposition has some criticism, it should come up with a solution. That was completely absent in the words of the honourable member for Blaxland. God save us if he is the man who one day is in charge as he seems to think that all one has to do to be able to run a government is criticise.
Mr BEAZLEY (Swan) (12.14)-1 was going to reply in some detail to the comments of the honourable member for O'Connor (Mr Tuckey) on this matter, but the utter silliness of the bulk of his speech defies reply. Suffice to say that, as a Western Australian member of parliament, I h~; observed discussion in the Western Australian: "''''. Press and the-Western Australian Parliament over the last six months. What has been absolutely evident throughout that discussion is that the Western Australian Government has demonstrated its complete incompetence to defend the interest of

870 REPRESENTATIVES 27 August 1981

Diamond Marketing Policy

the Western Australian people, let alone the Australian people, in matters related to mining. The Grants Commission report has revealed that persistently there has been a pattern in Western Australian royalties payments way below what the Western Australian Government could reasonably be expected to obtain on behalf of its people. The honourable member for O'Connor now expects us to accept the bona fides of the Western Australian Minister for Mines who has perpetrated the bulk of this situation. The honourable member for O'Connor says: 'Do not worry. He has it all under control. He has visited the project. He therefore knows all about it and there are no problems. He sent a man to discuss with De Beers the problem of marketing. Therefore, he knows all about it.'
What sort of laughable institution does the honourable member for O'Connor think this is when he brings in this sort of nonsense as a serious argument? We must not be too surprised at this. The Minister for Trade and Recources (Mr Anthony) would not debate this matter because he is in ignorance of it. The honourable member for O'Connor is in ignornace of it as well. How can he possibly expect the Western Australian Government to be responsible for or to have any substantial influence on the marketing arrangements of this product when it goes overseas? It is properly a Commonwealth responsibility and it was exercised under the Whitlam Labor Government in consultation with the States. No consultation is being carried out in this case because the Commonwealth has abnegated its responsibility. It did so again yesterday when the Minister replied to a question by the honourable member for Hawker (Mr Jacobi).
The Government's failure to defend the national interest on mining ventures of major importance to the economic future of this country stands once more exposed. Its failure is based on a deliberate determination to remain in ignorance of the national interest. In this case the Government has on its hands probably the richest single diamond mine in the world, a project which has been under evaluation for some years now, the dimensions of which have been to a considerable degree known in mining circles. Yet when the honourable member for Hawker asked a pertinent question yesterday on marketing arrangements and their impact on the national interest the Minister was obliged to reveal ignorance and a determination that he would not investigate or interfere with the venturers' claims and marketing arrangements, despite what the honourable member for O'Connor might think of some future action on his part.

If one wanted to devise a sample case which justified everything the Labor Party has said about the requirements of effective minerals policy one could not go past this one. In the first instance the public revelation of the value of the Ashton Joint Venturer's Lake Argyle project has come as a result of the leaking of a document produced for the junior partner in the group, Northern Mining, which is the only part of the project wholly Australian owned. Judging by the article by Jan Roberts which appeared in the Age and which was repeated in the Daily News of yesterday, the commentaries of a number of Northern Mining employees reveal an effort. by the company to defend our rights to a share of the world market commensurate with the significance of the project, to a price which ensures a return to the Australian community and to participate in the profitable secondary processing of the mineral-in this case through diamond cutting. The company does not argue its case because of big-heartedness but because it has a clear economic interest. Its circumstances indicate clearly that national capital and multinational capital are likely to have very differing interests in marketing arrangements. This is because multinational capital's interest in an enterprise is likely to be associated, as it is in this case, with a vertically integrated international operation with profit opportunities at all stages of the processing and marketing of the mineral. National capital, however, will not have such access and will therefore seek, firstly, the best possible price at the extractive stage and, secondly, processing opportunities locally and a say in international marketing arrangements. It has none of that automatically.
That is why Opposition members have said over and over again in this place that significant Australian components should be a feature of every resources project in this country. Yet at every stage this Government has thwarted the operation of the policy guidelines established during the Whitlam Government until they have come through in this case with totally ridiculous naturalisation methods that were amply dealt with by the member for Blaxland (Mr Keating) and commented upon pathetically by the honourable member for O'Connor. This Government has deliberately placed itself on the question of diamond marketing and mining in a position where it can be of no assistance to Northern Mining. It did this firstly in October 1976 by removing diamonds from the operation of export control arrangements established during our time. It did it secondly by permitting a situation in which the Bureau of Mineral Resources has no resources for or

Diamond Marketing Policy

27 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 871

rights to an independent survey of the value of the field.
There is a pathetic little piece in the Jan Roberts article which relates the comments of a spokesman from the BMR pointing out that his Bureau was limited to newspaper reports and to whatever companies chose to tell it. The Australian public and the Australian Public Service, as a direct result of the anti-national policies of this Government are compelled to scrabble about like theives in the night for information on the national interest. This is not to say that Northern Mining's reluctance to be involved with the central selling organisation is necessarily the best policy. The honourable member for Blaxland did not suggest it was. With a project so new and with that organisation's capacity to damage hold-outs, co-operation might be the most useful means of defending our national interest. But it is the terms of co-operation which are important. Obviously Australia's bargaining power is very great. In the Age today it was pointed out that production costs at Argyle will be about $6 a carat versus De Beers' South African average of $32 a carat. Argyle kimberlite pipe one-AKI-has a capacity to yield, again on estimates which are constantly being valued upwards, around 25 million carats a year, 20 times as high as the average Southern African mine and three times higher than last year's South African production. This colossal competitive advantage is being frittered away by the failures of this Government which cannot define, let alone defend, the national interest.
If Northern Mining were assisted by the Government or if it were itself involved, certain features of the bargaining process would obviously have emerged. Firstly, there. would have been an official counter to the efforts of other partners in the project to downgrade the value of the field. These partners have pointed out that in gemstone quality Ashton is somewhat lower than its South African equivalents. While the percentages are lower th!ln the South African percentage at the quality end, the volumes of production at Argyle will be so huge as to render this irrelevant. In case, near gemstone quality is what is available in most jewellery stores, and around 40 per cent of the amount which would be produced from the field would be of that quality.
Secondly, there would be an insistence that the development of the field would be accompanied by a cutting industry in Australia. There is no reason why Perth should not be the centre for a cutting operation for the whole of Australia. We now face the ludicrous possibility, if we do not have that cutting industry, that we will have to

continue to import the $54m-worth of cut diamonds we currently import despite the fact that these diamonds will now originate from Australia. The cut diamonds which come back into Australia will be many times the value of the raw product which goes out. Northern Mining, according to the article, would receiveonly $600,000 for the 60,000 carats of gems needed to supply the Australian market. The Central Selling Organisation would then be able to sell the stones back for $54m to the Australian trade. Thirdly, if the Government took an interest in bargaining with CSO or in establishing its own Australiansupported marketing operation, the project would now be offered a price for the product which is not 80 per cent below its real value, which, according to independent valuation, the CSO offer is. Fourthly, if the Government took an interest in bargaining we would be in a better position to beat down the monstrous commission which the CSO now insists upon.
The Government's position continues to leave the Australian population in ignorance of the possibilities of the venture. The Government's position continues to deny the possibility that the Australian population is going to benefit significantly from this project. The Government is worthy of considerable criticism on this matter. It is sigriificant that it has sought not to answer this criticism in the House itself, but to put up two of its most junior members to do so. That is a disgrace and a discourtesy which was never shown the present Government when it was in Opposition by our side of the House and it is a discourtesy which we would not show it, were we to be in office again. But it would not be bringing forward matters of public importance on this subject because it would be shamed by the effectiveness of our capacity to defend the national interest with regard to this matter and would prefer to maintain silence in much the same way as the Minister has maintained silence on this matter in the House today.

Much has been said today by the Opposition about the influence of the De Beers company's international operaticn in this project. I think we should, for a moment, look at the .criticism that the joint venture party is going into a market operation with De Beers and that De Beers is extending its influence on the joint venture. I believe this shows a distorted insight, both by the reporter who wrote the article and the Opposition, into the world marketing situation of diamonds. This is a damaging allegation and is so typical of many that come from the other side in relation to our resource projects. Again, it is based on hearsay and leaked documents.
Surely it is natural that a company which mines diamonds and which is endeavouring to form marketing operations for diamonds in Australia wiiI talk to De Beers, the world's largest diamond selling organisation. But it is also talking to many of the other companies involved. The honourable member for Blaxland (Mr Keating) attempted to mislead this House by stating that De Beers has a very major shareholding in the joint venture. My understanding is that De Beers has less than a 3 per cent holding in this venture, and this can hardly be termed a substantial holding. It is also a fact that the joint venture partners have spoken with not only De Beers but also General Electric-a major world producer of synthetic and industrial diamonds. The partners have also looked at the Zale Corporation, which is the world's largest marketer of diamonds through its chain of retail stores.
From the Government's point of view I emphasise that iUs not necessary at this time to develop a marketing policy. But the Government does have a clear policy in relation to minerals. Our policy is to ensure that there is a reasonable return for exports and that the development of projects takes place in the national interest. Therefore this matter of public importance raised today by the Opposition is an exercise in futility. It displays an ignorance of this particular venture, and I believe it should be totally disregarded by this Parliament. The process of evaluating a resource is a

long exercise and the marketing aspects particularly as they relate to diamonds will involve discussions with hundreds of companies throughout the world.
This Government will look carefully at whether a diamond marketing policy will include further processing, such as sorting and cutting, on the basis of its economic viability. The question of deciding the best form of marketing to adopt in these circumstances is clearly complicated and difficult. It is essentially a decision to be made on the basis of the commercial judgments of the companies conerned. They are aware of the Government's desire that Australia should obtain the appropriate benefit from mining projects of this kind and that processing and upgrading should take place in Australia where this is feasible.

UPGRADING OF AIRPORT FACILITIES FOR WIDE-BODY AIRCRAFT
Reference of Work: Public Works Committee Act,
Mr McVEIGH (Darling Downs-Minister for Housing and Construction) (12.36)-1 m.ove:

874 REPRESENTATIVES 27 August 1981

Upgrading ofAirport Facilities

That, in accordance with the provisions of the Public Works Committee Act 1969, the following proposed work be referred to the Parliamentary Standing Committee on Public Works for consideration and report: Upgrading of airport facilities for the introduction of domestic wide-body aircraft.
This proposal is for aircraft works associated with the introduction of wide-body jet aircraft by the domestic airlines and the consequential redeployment of elements of the existing fleet. In addition to these works, the airlines will be providing infrastructure works of their own at many airports. The works to be provided by the Commonwealth will include, where necessary, alteration and expansion of terminal facilities, strengthening and extension of runway, taxiway and apron pavements, and associated engineering works. The airports at which the works will be carried out include Adelaide, Coolangatta, Hobart, Melbourne, Perth and Sydney. The estimated cost of the proposed works is $23.3m at August 1981 prices. I table plans of the proposed works.

I hope a great number of submissions will come from all interested parties so that the Public Works Committee can thoroughly investigate the matters referred to it.
Question resolved in the affirmative.

SALES TAX (EXEMPTIONS AND CLASSIFICATIONS) AMENDMENT
BILL 1981
Second Reading
Debate resumed from 18 August, on motion by MrMoore:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
Mr WILSON (Sturt-Minister for Home Affairs and Environment)-Mr Deputy Speaker, may I have your indulgence to suggest that the House have a general debate covering this Bill, the Sales Tax Amendment Bills (Nos 1 to 9) and the Sales Tax Amendment Bills (Nos lA to 9A) as they are associated measures? Separate questions will, of course, be put at the conclusion of the debate.
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER-Is it the wish of the House to have a general debate covering these measures? There being no objection, I will allow that course to be followed.
Mr WILLIS (Gellibrand) (12.45)-These sales tax Bills give effect to three sets of decisions relating to sales tax that were announced in the Budget. Those three sets of decisions were: Firstly, to raise by 2t per cent the rates of sales tax on items previously taxed; secondly, to eliminate various alleged anomalies and inconsistencies in relation to sales tax; and, thirdly, to apply sales tax at the rate of 2t per cent to a whole range of items which were previously exempt from such tax. I wish to deal in more detail with what is proposed in the legislation. The first of those three sets of decisions is to raise the rate of sales tax by 2t per cent. At present there are three sets of sales tax. Prior to the Budget announcement the rates were 2t per cent, 15 per cent and 27+ per cent.
The decision to raise by 2t per cent the existing rate of 2t per cent means, of course, that there will be a doubling of sales tax on items in that category. When we talk about a 2t per cent increase it should be born in mind that we are in effect talking of a doubling of the tax on all these items. The items involved in this category are

876 REPRESENTATIVES 27 August 1981

Sales Tax Amendment Bill

everyday items such as furniture, crockery, glassware, cutlery, washing machines, vacuum cleaners, radiators, toasters, kettles, brooms, garbage cans, floor coverings, blinds, bedding, light fittings and candles. They are clearly items which are present in every household and which are important in people's daily expenditure patterns. Therefore an increase in the tax on those items is something which affects every person in the community. I think it is perhaps interesting to note also in this regard that, as everyone is aware, we have already had an increase in interest rates over the last year and a quarter. The cost of buying a home has increased because of that significant increase in interest rates.
This legislation in respect of the set of decisions applies sales tax to building materials, which increases the cost of building a home. The increase in sales tax on furniture and the other items I have just mentioned means that it costs far more to furnish a home, so home buyers are being hit in every possible way. The second rate of sales tax was pre-
viously 15 per cent. That is being raised to 1n per
cent-an increase of some 17 per cent in the amount of tax payable. That means that the cost of a car, which is the main item in this category will be significantly increased. In broad terms, apart from motor cars, the items that are covered by this category are: Commercial motor vehicles, soft drinks, stationery, potable spirits, boats and yachts. There are many other items but they are the main items listed.
The third category was previously taxed at 27t per cent. This increases to 30 per cent-a rise of 9 per cent in the amount of tax payable. The items affected here are jewellery, field glasses, fountain and ball point pens, watches, clocks-apart from alarm clocks-furs, hair oils, cosmetics, body creams, shaving goods, hair brushes and combs, cameras and films, radios, tape recorders, records and television sets. These items are all taxed at a high rate because at some time in the past they were regarded as luxuries. I find it difficult to conceive of shaving goods and combs and brushes as luxuries and many people would dispute today that a radio or television set was a luxury. Nevertheless they are taxed as such and the rate of tax now increases from 27t per cent to 30 per cent.
I refer now to the second category of decisions relating to the elimination of various alleged anomalies and inconsistencies. There are 34 of these items altogether, of which 29 represent increases in tax either by increasing the existing rates or by applying tax to previously exempt items. These increases are quite substantial. For instance, on television picture tubes the tax is increased from zero to 30 per cent, which will

mean a great increase in the cost of television sets. The tax on manufactured pet food, which I understand now also includes fresh pet foods, will go from zero to 17t per cent; the tax on car safety belts and windscreens will go from zero to 17-1: per cent; alarm clocks will go from 15 per cent to 30 per cent, household insecticides from zero to 17t per cent and so on. The tax increases are quite considerable and will have more than a minor impact on the expenditure patterns of people and on their living standards.
There are some reductions in bringing about the elimination of the so-called anomalies and inconsistencies. I should mention that there are some reductions in tax or eliminations of tax. They are mainly on items which are quite inconsequential, such as coffee whiteners and parts of movable toilet pans. In effect, we are looking at an array of increases under the disguise of eliminating anomalies. This considerably increases tax on a wide range of goods, some of which are particularly important. For example, I refer to the decision to tax colour television picture tubes which were previously exempt. It was the case that the tax on a television set applied to the whole of the set except for the tubes. The picture tube is a highly important part of a television set and the application of a 30 per cent tax instead of a zero tax to the tube means that the cost of a television set, as I understand it, will increase in the order of $80 or more. Altogether this so-called elimination of anomalies and inconsistencies will add $117m to tax revenue this year and $142m in a full year. In our view, the Government is achieving a broadening of the tax base under the guise of tidying up the legislation.
The third category of goods subject to tax covers those goods which were previously exempt but which are now being taxed at 2-1: per cent. All that was mentioned in the Budget was the broad categories of items which would be so taxable. The broad categories mentioned were clothing, footwear, drapery, soft furnishings, manchester, et cetera, building materials, books, magazines, newspapers, packaging and wrapping materials and tools and a range of other goods, not including food, medicine or wine. When we see what is being taxed we find that although. what was said indicates a fairly broad range of goods in fact, many items are now to be subject to tax which one would not,' on the face of it, think would be subject to tax under those headings. This will now be the case.
The legislation was brought in less than 48 hours ago by the Government to apply the tax to these previously exempt items. There was nothing in the second reading speech 01 the Treasurer (Mr

Sales Tax Amendment Bill

27 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 877

Howard) to throw any light on the matter I am about to raise but if one goes back to the legis- ~ation an? to the schedule of previously exempt Items whIch are now to have their exemption removed, one finds that these kinds of items are ?eing covered: Books including .leaflets, periodIcals, magazines and even printed music. It also includes maps and road and tourist maps. We also find that greaseproof paper, vegetable parchment paper and wax paper are to be taxed. Under the heading of 'building materials' all of the expected items are there-bricks, plaster, wallpaper and builders' hardware. The heading covers also nails and tacks, nuts and bolts, paints and all of the other normal building items. It also includes cooking stoves and ranges which were previously tax free but which will now be taxed at 2t per cent. It includes electrical fittings and accessories such as chandeliers and pendants-even light globes come under the heading of 'building materials'. These items are all taxable at 2t per cent. Included also are baths, shower screens, toilets and hot water services. It is a very broad interpretation of what comes under the heading of 'building materials'. We also find that paper bags, rubber. ba~ds and sticky tape are to be subject to tax, whIch IS really getting down to pretty minor items. Garden tools are also to be taxed.
Under the heading 'clothing' we find that not only all the obvious clothing is to be taxed, but also baby napkins and like items for babies. Also to be taxed are aprons, sanitary napkins and like items. It is a very broad interpretation of what is meant by 'clothing'. Without going through the list of thousands of items we find that we are facing a very substantial broadening of the tax base. It is not as though the Government is just simply extending the tax to a few additional categories. In effect, it is extending sales tax to all goods
ewxicnee.ptInfoerffethcrt,eethocsaet eagroe rtihees -ofnoloy d~oomdesdwichinicehaanrde
now to be free of sales tax.
The effect of these measures will be to raise altogether some $550m this year or $892m in a full year. This represents an increase in sales tax this year of 39 per cent and, therefore, a considerable increase in indirect taxation and in total tax revenue. As has been made absolutely clear by ~tatements outside this House, the Opposition is Implacably oppo&ed to these measures. It is opposed to them because they represent a repudiation of a pledge made only five months ago not to broaden the indirect tax base, they will have an adverse effect on the living standards of Australian families, they will affect the equity of the tax system in a quite regressive way and they will

most certainly add to the inflation rate. Therefore, the measures are totally the opposite to the Government's alleged policy of fighting inflation.
. In regard to the first of those reasons for opposmg these measures, I note that on 12 March this year the Treasurer announced that after reviewing the possibility of broadening the indirect tax base either by a value added tax, retail sales tax or by extending the wholesale sales tax, the Government had decided not to undertake any such measures. That was a clear and major statement from the Treasurer on 12 March in this House on taxation. A little over five months later the Government is, in fact, considerably broadening the indirect tax base. This is the complete opposite. of what it said at that time. If it was not approprIate five months ago to undertake this considerable broadening of the indirect tax base and to increase the incidence of indirect taxation generally, why is it now appropriate? We have been given no explanation for this at all.
All the Treasurer has been able to say is that he had something much more horrific in mind in cond~cting the investigation into broadening the indlfect tax base late last year and early this year. He wanted to raise indirect taxes-he made this clear in his statement on 12 March-by $3,OOOm. Now he says that because he is only raising them by a little under $l,OOOm the same arguments do not apply-that is, the arguments which he used in March of this year to say that the Government would not be going ahead with the idea of extending indirect taxation. The main reason he did not proceed last March was because of the inflati.onary impact; at least, that was the publicly gIven reason. There may be other reasons, but the reason he gave to this House was that to increase indirect taxes in the way he was proposing would have been too inflationary to bear. He estimated that a $3,OOOm increase in indirect taxes would increase the inflation rate by 6 per cent. Broadly speaking, he reached that calculation by saying that a $500m increase in indirect taxes increases the consumer price index directly and indirectly by one per cent.
Sitting suspended from 1 to 2 p.m.

Leave granted; debate adjourned.
REMUNERATION AND ALLOWANCES AMENDMENT BILL 1981
Bill-by leave~presented by Mr Newman, and read a first time.

878 REPRESENTATIVES 27 August 1981

Remuneration Amendment Bill 1981

Second Reading
Mr NEWMA~ (Bass-Minister for Administrative Services) (2.1 )-1 move:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
Earlier today I tabled the Remuneration Tribunal's 1981 Review which was presented to me on 18 June 1981. The Government has accepted the Tribunal's report on judges' remuneration and the following determinations: Allowances for members, Ministers and office-holders of the Parliament, and remuneration payable to holders of offices in the First Division of the Australian Public Service and holders of public offices. The Government accepts the merit in the Tribunal's decisions with regard to the increases in salaries for Ministers, members and office-holders in Parliament.
The decisions recognised the real decline in Parliamentary salaries that have already occurred in comparison with the wages earned by the average Australian. It is important to note that between March 1975 and December 1980 average weekly earnings rose by 84 per cent while the salaries of members of the Federal Parliament rose by 50 per cent. It should be remembered that members of parliament did not automatically receive the cost of living increases which flowed to most wage and salary earners as a result of national wage case decisions. It is also significant to look at the restraint that has already been exercised by the Government on its own Ministerial salaries in the interests of setting a national example.
Salary increases recommended for Ministers by an independent inquiry in 1971 were not implemented until 1973. Increases recommended by the Remuneration Tribunal in 1974 were not implemented. Increases in Ministerial salaries recommended in 1975, 1978 and 1979 were not implemented. In 1978 the Tribunal recommended that Ministers' salaries be increased by 4.9 per cent in line with national wage increases over the previous 12 months, but the Government did not accept this recommendation and no increases were given. In 1979 the Tribunal recommended increases of 9.1 per cent but the Government approved a rise of7.3 per cent.
Mr Deputy Speaker, the Government believes that the inflationary threats posed by excessive wage increases are so serious that an example of restraint must once again be set. Thus, as a lead to the rest of the community the Government has decided that the salary rise recommended by. th~ Remuneration Tribunal for all senators, members, office-holders and Ministers will not be taken at once, but spread over two years. I express

the hope that the community will take full note of this example being set by the Government and the Parliament.
The Remuneration and Allowances Amendment Bill gives effect to these views where action is required to implement or modify the Tribunal's decisions. Part II provides that members and office-holders of the Parliament will receive in 1981-82 salary or additional salary increases amounting to half those determined by the Tribunal. The rates which appear in the Bill reflect the total amounts which will be received in 1981-82. Part III deals with the salaries of Ministers and provides for the sum appropriated under the Ministers of State Act to be increased to provide for half the increases in the Tribunal's report. The Tribunal's report on the remuneration of judges is implemented by Part IV of the Act. Finally, Part V provides that the power of disallowances in the Remuneration Tribunals Act will not apply to the Tribunal's 1981 determinations, including those proposed to be modified by this Bill. I commend the Bill to the House.
Debate (on motion by Mr Lionel Bowen) adjourned.
SALES TAX (EXEMPTIONS AND CLASSIFICATIONS) AMENDMENT BILL
1981
Second Reading
Debate resumed.
Mr WILLIS (Gellibrand) (2.5)-Before the suspension of the sitting, I was making the point that the impact of these sales tax measures would be to add considerably to inflation, and that on the basis of the calculations giv~n by the Treasurer in his taxation statement on 12 March the impact would be in the order of 2 per cent. In that statement of 12 March, the Treasurer said thllt a one per cent increase in the consumer price index would occur for an increase in revenue from sales from indirect taxes of some $500m. I shall turn to his statement at that time. He said:
As a general rule, each additional $500m raised through indirect taxes results in direct and indirect additions to the consumer price index of almost 1 per cent. Furthermore, there are additional How-on consequences for the consumer price index through the wage system which are hard to quantify.
So he therefore was quoting at least one per cent, and maybe more. Now is he saying that the inflationary impact of sales tax rises of almost $1,OOOm-that is, about one-third of what he was talking about in March of-this year-would be only 0.75 per cent. Clearly, there is a great inconsistency between what he i~ saying now and what he was saying then. The only way that that can be explained at all, in my view, is that he is talking

Sales Tax Amendment Bill 1981

27 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 879

about the impact of those sales tax measures on the consumer price index for 1981-82 as against 1980-81. Of course, for only half that year we had the impact of the measures whereby previously exempt items are to be taxed. It would seem to us that the real figure is in the order of two per cent,
and maybe even more with the flow-on introduced to wages, as he said. This, of course, is an extremely important point. These increases in sales tax will not be some minor factor; they will have a notable impact on the inflation rate.
What the Treasurer seems to be saying is that, although the inflation impact in March of this year was something which concerned him, inflation now does not concern him that much. One would ask: 'Has there been some great change in the level of inflation since March of this year?' Of course there has not. There has been a slight reduction in the CPI over that time, but the forecasts are for a notable increase at least in the next year. One reason for that notable increase is the measures now before the House. One must ask: 'Whatever happened to the so-called "inflation first" policy?' If the policy is to fight inflationthat is what the Government keeps saying is its policy-why is it introducing measures such as this one which clearly add directly to inflation? On the basis of the Treasurer's own figures, these measures will add approximately 2 per cent to the inflation rate. How is this possibly fighting inflation? What it is in fact is the continuance of a process which defies reason but which has been pursued by the Government during its time in office, that is, the process of fighting inflation by increasing prices. This is perhaps one of the more blatant examples of it, but there are many examples in the earlier Budgets of the Fraser Government.
We are concerned about the inflationary impact of these measures and the reason why we oppose them is important. We think it is absurd to be adding directly to the inflation rate in this way. It obviously not only will add to the inflation rate directly but also will feed the inflationary expectations about which the Treasurer says he is concerned, more than any other factor because it adds to the inflation rate. That in itself is a major cause of increased inflationary expectations, and certainly outweighs any reduction in inflationary expectations which may come from reducing the deficit by the increased revenue Which comes from measures such as this. I think the Government and the Treasurer are totally misled in believing that by undertaking measures like this they are acting in some anti-inflationary way. It is quite obvious, on the face of the measures, that they are inflationary, and by their impact on inflationary expectations they certainly add to the inflation

rate in the future. I also note what the Treasurer said in March of this year when he said that he would not broaden the incidence of indirect taxation. At that time he said quite unambiguously that any move to widen indirect taxes should be accompanied by reduced income tax. In his statement of 12 March 1981 he said:
Throughout its-consideration of this issue, the Government remained firmly of the view that if a shift towards greater emphasis on indirect taxation were to occur, it should be accompanied by a noticeable reduction in personal income tax.
Where are the reductions'in personal income tax? Despite this firm view of the Government in March of this year that any widening of the impact of indirect taxation should be accompanied by compensating reductions in income tax, we have the opposite. Instead of a firm pledge to reduce income tax, we now have a firm commitment to increase it, along with the increases in indirect taxation. Of course, there can be no denying that this Budget includes a substantial increase in the incIdence of income tax. Pay-as-youearn taxpayers will pay 22 per cent more tax this year, although the estimated increase in the wages bill is 1St per cent. That clearly spells out an increase in the incidence of income tax for wage and salary earners-the majority of income tax payers.
It is quite clear that this firm promise of March of this year that if at any future time there were to be some expansion of the incidence of indirect taxation it would be accompanied by reduced income tax has been totally broken. Indeed, not only has it been broken in the sense that there are no income tax cuts but also, in fact, there have been considerable income tax increases. That, of course, is a further reason for the Opposition strongly to oppose these measures. We also oppose them because what the Government is doing now clearly represents the not so thin edge of the wedge to a further expansion of indirect tax at some future. time. In particular, with the new category of 2t per cent-that is, the items which were previously exempt but which are now taxable at 2t per cent-it would be very easy to raise these rates to the 5 per cent level, doubtless claiming that this was necessary to eliminate an anomaly, as the Government has done in respect of other items in this year's Budget. It is highly likely, therefore, that this is just the first step towards an even further extension of the indirect taxation that the Treasurer and the Treasury clearly desire. I note in this regard that the previously exempt items now to be taxed were all suggested by the Treasury in Taxation Paper No. 5 of 1974 and, of course, were not adopted by the then Labor Government, but they are now being

880 REPRESENTATIVES 27 August 1981

Sales Tax Amendment Bill 1981

adopted by t~is Government. It was also suggested then that the rates could be raised as another means of extending indirect taxation. That has also been done in this Budget. The Treasury also suggested in that paper that we could have a tax on services. That has not been done at this time. Clearly, this could 'be a future step in the process of increasing indirect taxes on which this Government now seems to have embarked.
Doubtless the Government will claim that this is not its intention. But the Opposition is not the only body which believes that the Government's intention is to go much further than it has to the present stage in extending the impact of indirect taxation. The morning after the Budget was delivered last week the Australian Financial Review had an editorial which spoke of the sales tax move as 'a major step towards a broadening of the tax base'. The editorial went on to state:
But of course the future of the new sales taxes is quite predictable. Over time they will creep up, while the much more noticeable income taxes will when electorally convenient, be reduced.
But the sales tax on a broader base is now a fait accompli. It will not be nearly as difficult once it is in operation to extend it to nearly all the categories of retailing.
Clearly, it is a wider perception than just that of the Opposition that this is the not so thin edge of the wedge for a much more significant extension of indirect taxation. As we made clear when the Treasurer made his statement in March of this year, we deplore any attempt to increase the incidence of indirect taxation. This is partly because of its inflationary impact, which I have spoken of, and because it undeniably increases the inequity of the tax system. Obviously, that inequity arises because of the way in which tax is levied. It applies in a way which has no regard for the capacity of individuals to pay. Instead of being a progressive tax, as income tax is, it is a regressive tax; that is, the tax applies at the same amount regardless of income and therefore bears more heavily as a proportion of income on those persons with lower incomes. It is regressive rather than progressive taxation, not only having no regard for the capacity of people to pay but also bearing on people in reverse of their capacity to pay. We oppose any extension of the incidence of indirect taxation. We have been consistent about that in the past with other indirect tax measures and similarly we oppose these measures because of what is involved in that regard.
In regard to the matter of the inequity of indirect taxation, I point to a couple of surveys which bear out what I am saying in this regard.

Firstly, the household expenditure survey of 1976 conducted by the Australian Bureau of Statistics-if one updates the tables contained in that survey-clearly shows that households on lower incomes spend a greater proportion of their incomes than do their higher income counterparts. Therefore, if one taxes most of their expenditure it will weigh more heavily on them than on higher paid persons. Households on lower and middle incomes spend a greater proportion of their incomes on housing, fuel and power, food, household equipment and operation, and medical care-areas which are hard hit by the Commonwealth Government's charges and indirect taxes and by State charges. These State charges are likely to be increased in forthcoming budgets.
In 1976 Mr Neil Warren of the University of New South Wales looked at the incidence of all federal indirect taxes on different income levels for households. This study showed a declining trend as income rises. These finding have been updated by the increase in average weekly earnings since that time. The results show that a household with an income around average weekly earnings-that is, about $320 a week-pays 10.2 per cent of that income in indirect taxes, compared to 7.1 per cent for a household with an income double average weekly earnings and 14.9 per cent for a household earning less than $4,000. What comes clearly from that analysis of the impact of indirect taxation on households with differing incomes is that for lower income households those indirect taxes weigh far more heavily. This is not simply a matter of assertion; it is a matter of detailed, empirical analysis by Mr Neil Warren of the University of New South Wales. I believe that in those circumstances there can be no denying whatever that a move towards more indirect taxation, as is involved in these measures, means a move to a more regressive tax system and that if the Government persists in this direction clearly it will be forcing the lower income section of the community to bear a greater and greater proportion of the total taxation burden.
We oppose these measures also because of their overall effect on family living standards. As we have said over the past week or so outside this House and, I think, inside it, the impact will be in the order of $4 a week for a family. That calculation conies simply from dividing the total annual tax revenue by the number of people in Australia, which gives a total tax levy per person of a little over $60 a year-that is, more than $1 a week. For a family of four that means an amount of about $4 a week. In fact, it comes to about $4.70. Because part of the sales tax burden will bear on mining companies and some other bodies

Sales Tax Amendment Bill 1981

27 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 881

which will not be able to pass the cost on to consumers, we can just round that figure off to about $4. This is probably a very generous rounding-off. It would seem to us that there will be an increase of at least $4 a week for a family of four. I would like to know how the Treasurer gets a figure of about $1.50 to $2, which is the figure that he keeps using. It seems to us to be a figure which is totally unjustifiable on the basis of the amount of revenue which he says his tax will raise-almost $900m in a full year. That must mean a burden per head of population over $1 a week. Clearly, that will impinge on family living standards to a significant degree. It is not some minor element, as the Treasurer tries to make out.
Also, of course, in looking at that effect we are looking at the impact of the tax revenue. We are not there looking at the total impact on consumers which will more than likely be greater than the tax revenue because wholesalers will add the tax to their price. But the retailer will then place mark-ups on the price from the wholesaler which will include the wholesale increased tax. So the mark-up by the retailer to the consumer will mean that where there is a 2t per cent rise we will more than likely see an increase well above that. Those increases are likely to mean that for an increase of 2t per cent with a 20 per cent mark-up, one will see a 3 per cent effective tax rate. For a 50 per cent mark-up it will be 3.75 per cent rather than 2t per cent as the Treasurer suggests.
For all of the reasons I have given we are strongly and implacably opposed to these measures. In asserting that opposition we are being totally consistent with the view which we have taken in the past in respect of indirect taxation measures in general. Also we say that we have a right, as an opposition, to oppose tax measures. That is in no way inconsistent with the statement which we have made many times that we do not believe that the Opposition ought to block Supply. This is not blocking Supply. We are not suggesting a blockage of the Appropriation Bills and therefore putting the Government in jeopardy. All that we are saying is that there ought to be a blockage of these tax Bills because they are not in the best interest of this country. It is consistent with the fact that we have opposed various tax Bills in the past.

Mr GILES (Wakefield) (2.22)- The honourable member for GeJlibrand (Mr Willis) has given, as is usual, an objective and moderate summing-up of the economic situation revolving

around these sales tax Bills. I want to do as he has done and that is touch on the generalities before I speak on the Sales Tax (Exemptions and Classifications) Amendment Bill, the Sales Tax Amendment Bills (Nos 1 to 9) and the Sales Tax Amendment Bills (Nos lA to 9A). I will do this very briefly. What intrigues me is the sort of credibility gap that is left once we have listened carefully to the speeches by the honourable member for Gellibrand or, indeed, the Leader of the Opposition (Mr Hayden).
The community knows and understands very well that the Opposition, in government, has a role of higher expenditure than the current Government, although it tries, from time to time, to reverse the argument. It does not take a lot of horse sense to know that, if one adds up the costs of many of the Australian Labor Party's bills, we need more revenue to finance them: Yet, what do we see? The Opposition has been against the razor gang reports by and large. It has been against the health package which the Government has brought down and which has been the biggest single saving on government expenditure of this Budget. It objects to the run down that this Government is pleased to have been able to take a part in by taking account of redundancy in trying to cut back on the number of civil servants that perform their functions for the community properly and well. Now it objects to the sales tax measures. So, there is an enormous credibility gap over what the Opposition will do under similar situations. I repeat that, in government, it would need more revenue than this Government to finance it schemes. Yet it objects to every cutback in government expenditure that the Fraser Government is bringing about to try to achieve a better balance on behalf of the consumers and the people of Australia. I think that point needs to be taken into account.
The honourable member for Gellibrand says he opposes these Bills. He looks on them as being regressive legislation. That is a simplistic argument and one that can be readily believed out in the consumer marketplace. This is not necessarily true at all. Really what these sales tax Bills do is to put a higher tax on people who spend up. Whether they come from one section of society or another they really are the losers. But there is another side to it. If one looks at the overall Budget strategy and sees that there is a downward thrust of some form of expenditure, particularly on luxuries, then, indeed, it does not even necessarily become highly inflationary. Certainly, there is a check on the bare direct figures in an indirect fashion.

27 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 885

retreated. In this Budget sales taxes were dramatically increased as part of a move which is very much in line with the move that the Treasurer was contemplating some months ago.
It is important to emphasise that this is not part of any overall program of policy reform. Whilst the Asprey Committee, for example, recommended the introduction of VAT and canvassed the possibility of increases in sales taxes, it did so within the context of a number of proposals which would have led to sweeping reforms in the tax system if they had been adopted. The Committee saw the need to look at the question of equity in consumer taxes, and in an overall program of reform, to provide for a system of tax credits for lower income families to compensate for the obvious inequitable character of an increase in sales taxes. It also recognised the need at the other end of the scale to introduce capital and capital gains taxes to assist in providing a level of equity to the overall system of taxation. I do not think it would be possible for the Treasurer to argue that what he is about is in any sense an equitable system of taxation. Yet equity is one of the fundamental principles, though not the only principle, by which a system of taxation ought to be judge<l.
No doubt the measures which have been introduced by the Government will result in a further shaw increase in the degree of inequity which is being built into the taxation system as Ii whole. The increases in sales tax will collect another $150 to $200 a head from the Australian population, a very sharp increase in the total burden of taxation. This is being done without compensating effect to cushion the impact of those changes on lower income families. Alongside these proposals there is nothing from the Government in terms of either capital taxes or the resource rent taxes so strongly favoured by the Treasury. Nor on the other side is the Government concerned to bring about any dramatic change in tht< social security system along the lines of a negative income or a guaranteed minimum income as has been canvassed, for example, by Professor Henderson in the poverty inquiry. Furthermore, the imposition of indirect taxation in this case and in the earlier cases I mentioned seems to run directly counter to the Government's professed strategy. The Government is introdcing taxation which must be inflationary. In the Government's own policies it hardly seems to be concerned about inflationary impacts. Inflation has been built into the system as a result of its oil pricing and taxation policies, the health insurance changes this year, and now the imposition of a heavy indirect taX.

I think we can say quite confidently that, with these sales tax measures alone, another one per cent will be added to the consumer price index. We can look at examples of where this sales tax is going to fall. As the shadow Treasurer has indicated, the impact will be very much on the ordinary family. For example, as a result of sales taxes imposed on building materials, $500 or $600 is likely to be added to the price of a house costing in the range of $35,000. It is a very severe impost in a situation where, because of the rise in interest rates, it is becoming increasingly difficult for ordinary people to purchase their own homes, The Government says that it is on about home ownership. When we look at the Government's record we see that it is on about reducing the opportunities of people to own their own homes.
We could also look at the impact of sales tax on transport. We are talking about very basic areas of people's expenditure-housing and transport. As a result of the increased imposition contained in these measures, the prices of cars are likely to rise considerably. For example, a Holden Gemini will cost $116 more, a 6-cylinder Commodore will cost $168 more and a Datsun Bluebird will cost $130 more. At a time when the vehicle industry is not exactly in a very healthy condition we find that the Government is moving to make it more difficult for people to purchase cars. I would have thought we ought to be stimulating the market, if anything, and not depressing it at a time when there is so much uncertainty about the future of the industry.
We can look at increases in sales tax on a whole range of consumer goods, which I will come back .. to in a moment. These increases will represent a massive difference to the cost of living for the normal family. It has been estimated that they will cost a four-person family an additional $245. These unfair increases will swallow up any increases in family allowances, which, of course, are not being provided for what is regarded as the average family in Australia-a man, his wife and two children. They have to be looked at in the context of a Budget which will allow income tax to rise by 21 per cent-well above the expected rate of increase in incomes this year. This is also happening in the context of a government which, through its changes to medical insurance, will add $10 a week to the costs for the average Australian family.
It has been said, but it needs to be said again, that this Government is entirely insensitive to the effects of its policies on ordinary families. The principal impact of these changes, it cannot be emphasised enough, is not going to hit the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) or those people

27 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 887

that the Government was interested only in attacking industry whereas the Leader of the Opposition said only the other night that the Government was not interested in people but was interested only in industry. I wish members of the Opposition would make up their minds about what they really believe.
These Bills are to implement three Government decisions outlined in the Budget. The first group of Bills is to increase by 2t per cent the existing rates of sales tax as they apply to sales taxable items. The new rates of sales tax will be effective as from Budget night. The second group of Bills is to implement the Budget decision to correct a number of anomalies and inconsistencies in the sales tax law. This will mean that a number of items previously sales tax exempt will now be taxed at the rate equal to that applied to other light goods in their class. It will also mean that some goods will now be exempt from sales tax. This is consistent with the exemption applied to other like goods in their class. I shall dal later with one of those items in this category. Finally, the third set of Bills relates to the imposition of a 2t per cent sales tax on a number of items previously not subject to sales tax.
The Treasurer (Mr Howard), when announcing the new sales taxes, said that there is advantage in some change in the mix of direct and indirect taxes to increase the potential for desirable reductions in direct taxes and particularly personal income tax as economic and budgetary conditions permit. Honourable members will recall that the Government has on two occasions reviewed the nature and source of the Commonwealth Government's revenue, particularly as it relates to the composition of taxation as between direct and indirect taxes, On both occasions, for a number of reasons, the Government did not decide to introduce a new broadly based indirect form of taxation. Whilst these new sales tax initiatives involve a slight shift in emphasis they do not constitute a new, broadly based tax because major areas such as the service industries are not subject to these new or increased tax rates. However, it is a small step towards reducing our dependence on personal tax as a source of revenue and relying a little more heavily on indirect tax whilst maintaining the tax free status of basic food items.
If we look to the proportions of revenue raised by personal income tax, company tax, customs, excise and sales tax and the crude oil levy w~ see that over the years the proportion of the Commonwealth Government's revenue raised from personal income tax increased from 34 per cent in 1958-59 to a peak of nearly 57 per cent in

1977-78. Personal income tax now provides more than half the total tax revenue for the Commonwealth Budget. Over the same period customs, excise and sales tax as a proportion of the total Budget have fallen from around 40 per cent to just over 20 per cent. Clearly, we now rely on income tax as the major source of our revenue and indirect taxes make a much smaller contribution to our total income. As time goes on this reliance on personal income tax is likely to increase because the income from one of the forms of indirect tax, that is the crude oil levy, will directly decline in future years as Australia uses up its old oil resources and becomes more dependent on the more recent oil discoveries which are not subject to the crude oil levy.
Therefore, whilst our overall tax burden is lower than the average of the industrialised world and whilst our level of personal tax is lower than the Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development average, over time we have become more reliant on personal income tax and less reliant on indirect taxes. Also our indirect taxation tends to be concentrated on a limited number of items unlike, for example, most European countries. In my view there are a number of advantages in redressing this shift toward a greater reliance on personal income tax. Also, bearing in mind as I have said that revenue from the crude oil levy is likely to grow less rapidly in the years ahead, this would have further aggravated the imbalance. I therefore welcome the Government's initiative to increase our reliance on indirect taxation rather than direct taxation and also to spread the base of that indirect taxation in order to include goods which were not previously taxed.
The arguments for a change in emphasis in our tax raising mechanisms are numerous. Firstly, personal income tax, being an extremely visible form of taxation, can become a disincentive for people to increase their work effort and income if the tax rate is such that they believe their marginal return is not worth the effort. I am firmly of the view that lower, rather than higher, income tax rates will increase the incentive for people to earn extra income and therefore increase our standard of living through increased production and increased. productivity. Secondly, indirect taxation has the advantage of being somewhat harder to evade and avoid than personal income tax. Of course the disadvantage is that it is likely to contribute to an increase in the consumer price index.. However, as the Treasurer has mentioned, the increase in the CPI, and so the inflationary impact of the imposition of these taxes, is likely to be small.

888 REPRESENTATIVES 27 August 1981

Sales Tax Amendment Bill 1981

It could also be argued that a heavy reliance on indirect taxation such as sales tax can be regressive depending on the rate of tax and the items which are included in the tax base. Clearly, this current change will not result in a heavy reliance on sales tax. I believe one would be hard put to sustain this argument in the present circumstances. Having explained that I believe the Government's initiative in undertaking this move to increase indirect taxation is a good one as it constitutes a small step to redress what I believe is becoming a fundamental imbalance in the source of Commonwealth revenue, the question then arises as to why the Government did not reduce income tax at the same time as it increased sales tax.
The revenue which is expected to be raised from these sales tax moves is, in this financial year, around $553m. That revenue could have been used to increase the domestic surplus, as has been done in the Budget or for personal income tax deductions. For example, that money would have enabled us to increase the tax threshold by about $250. That is a reduction in tax of about $4.80 a weekfor the ordinary taxpayer. The Government decided against this course and instead will use the money to reduce the amount the Government would otherwise have had to borrow. The Government believes the Australian community will be best served by a reduction in the Government's borrowing requirements, and this move seeks to take the steam out of interest rate increases. Last financial year the Commonwealth, State and local governmens borrowed in excess of $4,OOOm. The amount to be borrowed this year can be reduced by a domestic surplus, such as predicted in this Budget, and thereby interest rates will be reduced below what they would otherwise be.
The . question then for the Treasurer was whether to use the sales tax revenue for tax reductions o.r to try to take the steam out of interest rate incr~asesby using the revenue to lower our loan requirements. On, my calculations Ii. one per cent increase in interest rates on a $30,000 home loan would more than wipe out the $4.80 tax benefit which could have arisen if the sales tax revenue had been used to reduce income taxes. Therefore, if the Government had used the sales tax revenue to reduce income tax rather than try to reduce interest rates the general, Australian public could well have been worse off because the tax benefit may have been-absorbed by increased interest rate 'payments. The Government has taken the sound course of bringing pressure to bear on the interest rate problem, and this will benefit all Australians. Clearly once interest rates

are reduced and become more stable the Government will be in a better position to reduce income taxes and so move towards the restoration of a reasonable balance in our reliance on indirect and direct sources of taxation.
I now refer to the Sales Tax (Exemptions and Classifications) Amendment Bill which alters the sales tax classification of certain goods. Over time a number of anomalies have become evident in the sales tax treatment of a number of goods, and this Bill seeks to remedy some of those. One particular anomaly which seriously affected my electorate relates to the timber industry. I first raised this matter with the Treasurer in 1978. As far as I am aware, all equipment used in felling and removing timber from the forest to the roadside is tax exempt, except log forwarders. When introducing this Bill the Treasurer stated:
The expansion of logging of soFtwood Forests has led to the development of speCialised equipment called log Forwarders, to haul the Felled logs From the Felling point in the Forest to roadside pick-up points.
I am delighted that the Government has accepted that in the interests of equity and consistency a sales tax exemption will now be provided for log forwarders. The sales tax involves between $10,000 and $20,000 per log forwarder and it may well be more now, depending on the price of log forwarders. A number of these pieces of equipment are in use in the pine forests in the south-east of South Australia, serving the mills in the Mount Gambier, Millicent, Nangwarry, Kalangadoo, Mount Burr, Penola and other areas. The Government has now removed the restriction placed by the imposition of this tax on the use of this technogically advanced equipment. I am sure that not only the people using the log forwarders but also the whole of the timber industry will benefit from this rational decision. I might add that I also welcome the Government's decision to exempt from sales tax protective clothing of a kind used for sport or recreation such as helmets, guards, gloves and mittens.
One area on which I believe the Government must keep a close eye is the administration of the collection of the new taxes. We must continually review the administrative procedures in order to ensure that we minimise the inconvenience to the public and especially to small businesses, which in some cases will have to administer the collection or the exemption claims arising from the imposition of these new taxes. That matter must be the subject of continual review to ensure that the burden, especially that imposed on the small businessman, is not unreasonable. I conclude by supporting the Bills and welcoming the Government's initiative in moving away from an even

Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Hon. J. D. M. Dobie)-I will now take the opportunity to mention to all honourable members that speeches, up to this point, have been heard in silence. It is clear from the Australian Government Statistician study that something like 26 per cent of the purchases of lower income groups are for food alone. That fact, which is available from the official statistics, has never been mentioned by the whinging honourable members opposite. It was not mentioned by the great editorial writer in the Sydney Morning Herald. It is extraordinary that a newspaper like this, which itself is to suffer a burden as a result of increased sales tax, is prepared to run a campaign against sales tax in the Budget on a basis which seems to me to be slightly lacking in honesty.
It seems to me impossible, iIi an editorial looking at this subject in a genuine way, to ignore the fact that food is totally exempted from sales tax when that editorial is protesting that this Budget attacks viciously the lower income groups. I want to stress the position of the pensioner population of Australia. Fortunately, as a result of this Government's decisions, pensioners receive full indexation for cost of living rises. Yet the bulk of the cost of living rises which will be brought about by the sales tax increases will not affect pensioners one bit.
