Mr MOUNTFORD-In relation to table 5, that paper states:
The figures in the third colum of Table 5 give some indication of the weight of tax on specific expenditure categories. (These latter figures, incidentally, do not give a precise measure of the extent to which the imposition of the taxes would have affected the final prices of the goods concerned.) Although receipts from revenue taxes on consumer commodities in 1970-71 represented only 6.3 per cent of total personal consumption expenditure, the ratio of tax to various categories of expenditure varied a great deal.
This ratio is very low in the case of food because commodity taxes are imposed on very few food items. In addition, it is apparent that about one third of personal consumption expenditure . . . bears no Commonwealth taxation of this kind. This consists predominantly of expenditure on various services-for example, rent, education, entertainment, fares, etc.
Expenditure onservices is, of course, a growing component of personal consumption. Such expenditure will therefore doubtless continue to increase faster than expenditure on consumption goods. Moreover, by comparison with many goods subject to tax, some of these services are very much in the discretionary spending class, e.g. the. services provided by restaurants, hotels and guest houses, cinemas, theatres, etc. This prompts the question as to whether at least some of these areas of expenditure should be brought within the taxation field.

The paper went on to state that personal consumption and services areas which are not presently taxed could be looked at with the purpose of taxing possibilities. What we see, therefore, in these measures which are covered in the legislation before. us at present is that they largely follow the suggestions put forward by the 1974 Treasury Paper, which incidently were not proceeded with by the Government of the day-a Labor Government. The Department of the Treasury and the Treasurer obviously are concerned only with measures which will increase income without giving any consideration to the effect they have on the Australian community. What these increases mean is that the Government is seeking to balance its Budget by taxing the Australian community a further.$81Im this financial year. These increases will mean that a typical family will payout between $3.50 and $4 a week more for their basic weekly requirements because of the impost of additional sales tax. For those families with a number of children going to school, it will mean even higher increases because of the clothing content involved.

894 REPRESENTATIVES 27 August 1981

Sales Tax Amendment Bill 1981

What we have is yet another one of the Government's broken promises. The Treasurer stated on 12 March this year, when he announced the Government's decision not to proceed with the introduction of a new broadly based indirect tax, that the Government was anxious to 'minimise the inflationary consequences of collecting additional revenue' and that it did not wish to 'move towards greater reliance on indirect tax without
providing a noticeable offset in personal tax reductions'. Quite contrary to these promises, taxpayers will now be slugged with an extra $892m increase in sales tax in a full year which, the Government admits, will add at least 0.75 per cent to the inflation rate. Increased sales taxes are completely reprehensible as they hit hardest at those on the lowest incomes. Many articles essential to the family budget will be hit. Sales tax on furniture, floor coverings, whitegoods, crockery and bedding is to be doubled, from 2t per cent to 5 per cent. Not only will increased interest rates mean a higher cost of buying one's home but also will it now be even more expensive to furnsih it and live in it. Clothing, building materials, shoes, books and newspapers will all be subject to the new 2t per cent tax, with the tax on school books costing parents an extra $6m a year. The increased family allowances for large families will quickly be eaten up by the higher cost of keeping children in clothes and shoes. The Housing Industry Association estimates that the tax on building materials is likely to add $1,000 to the cost of building and furnishing a new home. The National Roads and Motorists Association has calculated that the cost of a new 6-cylinder Holden Commodore will rise by $178 and the cost of a 4-cylinder Datsun will rise by $150. Even the oil to run the car will cost 17.5 per cent more. The net result on the family budget of all these increases will be over $4 a week, as I have said. This will have a disastrous impact on family living standards which have already been battered by rising interest rates and more expensive health costs since the last election. Added to the increase in 'income tax of $11 a week for a family with a
breadwinner on average weekly earnings, this means a massive tax increase of $15 a week.
As an indirect tax, these sales tax increases will hurt those families on lower incomes. Whether one earns $10,000 or $40,000 a year, the same costs will need to be borne on items essential to family living. Rather than increasing indirect taxes, the Government should be reducing them so as to ease the pressure on lower income groups in our community. This year increases in sales tax will give the Government a further $892m in a full year. I believe that this legislation is only the

beginning, with future years bringing further increases, including increases in the food and services areas. As personal consumption expenditure on food is almost double that of the clothing and manchester area and as servicing expenditure is similar to the clothing area, we can get some idea of the additional sales tax revenue which could be raised by the Government in future years.
The honourable member for Macarthur (Mr Baume) stated just a few moments ago that 26 per cent of the household expenditure of lower income groups is spent on food. We can see, therefore, the effect that possible further sales tax increases would have on those people in the lower income bracket, which includes many millions of our fellow Australians. The sales tax scheme is one whereby the Government obtains additional wealth by stealth. The present legislation is another broken promise by the Government. The Government has no electoral mandate for these matters. For that reason and because the Australian Labor Party has a policy to reduce indirect taxation owing to its inequitable sharing amongst high, middle and low income earners, the Opposition opposes the legislation.
Mr SHIPTON (Higgins) (3.41)-1 am pleased to be speaking in this debate in support of the Government's sales tax proposals. As the House knows, the Government has decided in the Budget to increase by 2t per cent each of the existing rates of sales tax effective in relation to goods passing the taxing point after Budget night. Additionally, the Government has legislation before the House to apply tax at the rate of 2t per cent to a range of goods that were exempt, including clothing, footwear, drapery, soft furnishings, manchester, building materials, books, magazines and newspapers, packaging and wrapping materials, and hand tools but not including medicines, food or wine. At this stage, I think it is important that I react to what the honourable member for Banks (Mr Mountford) just said. I think he tried to use scaremongering tactics to frighten the people in the community by saying that sales tax would be extended to food. That is not so. Traditionally, if one looks at sales taxes and retail taxes around the world, food is exempt. I can assure the House and the Australian community that, in fact, food is not included in this sales tax legislation. I think the honourable member should withdraw that comment.
Let us look at sales tax because it is an important tax and it has an impact on all people in the community. Under the existing legislation, manufacturers and wholesalers are charged with lodging returns and paying that tax. In 1975 the

Sales Tax Amendment Bill 1981

27 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 895

Asprey Taxation Review Committee recommended that there be a broadly based tax on goods and services. In fact, the Committee recommended that there be a value added tax. I totally reject the imposition of a value added tax and I am pleased that the Government has, in fact, also done that. I think it adds to great costs in the community. There is in place a sales tax system and I think the Government has shown good sense in introducing this new legislation. Sales tax is nothing new. When the Romans conquered Egypt they imposed a general sales tax. Sales tax was first introduced in Australia in 1930.
I think an important aspect of the measure that we are introducing is that we are taxing for the first time a large number of goods in the community where the cash economy exists. In other words, we now have in force a group of sales tax Acts that, broadly speaking, cover at various rates of tax a whole range of goods, but not services, in the community. That means that in the long run the emphasis on personal income tax can be relieved. I hope that in the long run the heavy reliance on personal income tax will be relieved. My view is that no person should pay more than 50 per cent of his or her income in taxation. Similarly, I hope that in the long run we will increase the tax threshold-the so-called amount before tax is paid-by relying on sales tax of this kind. It is worth reiterating that for the first time the cash economy is in fact being taxed in part.
I would like us to look at the impact of sales tax in a constructive way so that we cansee whether it should be changed in the future, whether changes in the method of collection need to be made in the long run. I support the legislation before the House; but we do not live in a perfect world, so let us just look at what we might do in the long run. It is very hard for a Treasurer when introducing a Budget to maintain secrecy and, in fact, still make broad inquiries as to how changes should come into effect because the more people he consults, the more risk there is of breaching secrecy.
The present method of sales tax collection involves manufacturers and wholesalers. They are, in fact, playing the role of tax collectors. They are, in fact, collecting a tax which is actually borne by the consumer at the end of the chain. The manufacturer or wholesaler has to pay this tax to the authorities within 21 days. This period of 21 days was obviously put into the Sales Tax Act in the days when people paid their accounts in 21 days. Things have changed and will change, especially when interest rates have gone up. I hope that the effect of the domestic surplus proposed in the Budget will moderate them. I am confident about that. It is standard practice in the

community today for people to take terms of 60 days or 90 days for payment. In fact, the manufacturers and wholesalers will have to finance the payment of this tax. I have written to the Treasurer (Mr Howard) about extending the time of payment from 21 days. I know that he will look at that. I know that there is a cost to revenue. But there is a difficulty for those people who, in fact, have to collect the tax. It affects their liquidity and cash flow. I think that has some effect on the small manufacturer in Australia who has the capacity to expand by way of increased employment. That person now has to worry about paying sales tax within that 2I days and in the terms that I have mentioned. I ask the Treasurer to look at this matter and try to come up with some imaginative alternative that relieves the burden on these people. A large amount of time is spent by small businesses on administration and this can discour-
age them. It also creates the liquidity problems to which I have referred.
I am surprised iil many ways that the manufacturer organisations have not made the point that I have made today. I have long been an advocate of extension of the time for payment beyond 21 days. The sales tax method of collection is one that, as I have said, has been around for a long time. In the longer term, I would like to see the point of collection of sales tax extended to the retail level.
Let me give an example. Let us take an item priced at $1 and apply a fictional rate of 30 per cent sales tax. The wholesale price is $1.30. Retail mark-ups are, in fact, 100 per cent, so that the retail price of the good is $2.60. The government
gets 30c in tax and the cost to the consumer is $2.60. One could extend the tax payment point to the retail level. The item could still cost $I, to which would be added a retail mark-up of 100 per cent, bringing the cost to $2 for the article. The sales tax could be at the rate of 15 per cent so as to get the 30c for the Government and one would have a price of $2.30. This would mean a lower price to the consumer and the Government would still have its 30c. Such a prospect is not before us today; it is something for the future.
I was pleased to hear the honourable member for Macarthur (Mr Baume) quote the Managing Director of David Jones (Australia) Pty Ltd or a senior executive of that organisation-I apologise if I am wrong in my details-as saying that, in fact, retailers would not pass on fully the cost of this tax. I think this provides an opportunity andchallenge for retailers not to add to the level of inflation, not to increase their mark up, because of the mechanism by which sales tax is collected. I ask retailers to act responsibly in the present climate and to look at the mark-ups and margins

896 REPRESENTATIVES 27 August 1981

Sales Tax Amendment Bill 1981

and, in fact, as a whole, perhaps reduce those margins. I hope that the Retail Traders Association will make some statement in that regard to reassure the community.
Finally, in support of this legislation which is before the House and in a constructive waybecause, as I said, we do not live in a perfect world and the time is limited before a Budget is brought down-I want to look at the various rates of sales tax and at some of the anomalies and inconsistencies which have been corrected by the Treasurer in the Budget. These are included in Budget Paper No.4. Under the sales tax legislation previously exempted goods will be taxed at rates of 2t per cent. There are now four basic rates of sales tax. There is a new rate of 2t per cent on previously exempt goods and a rate of 5 per cent which generally applies to household goods. There is a general rate of 17t per cent on other goods and a rate of 30 per cent on luxuries. That is an average of 13 per cent as a rate but, of course, not 13 per cent across the board in terms of contribution to revenue. Different items 'contribute different amounts to revenue.
If one looks at sales tax anomalies and inconsistencies, one item that does stand out is nonreturnable containers, such as bottles and cartons used in the marketing of soft drink. I ask the Treasurer to have a look at this item because I think anomalies still exist even though some have been corrected. What has happened is that by taxing non-returnable containers such as bottles, the soft drink industry will have added costs and the price of lemonade in milk bars and supermarkets will go up. In fact, an anomaly still exists because fruit juice and flavoured milk are exempt from sales tax. In my view the tax on these containers should be reduced to a rate of 2t per cent or fruit juice and flavoured milk should be taxed at the rate of 17t per cent as are the non-returnable containers. Finally, I want to look at some of the confectionary items, such "as the traditional ones that are very popular in many households. I have a weakness for one or two myself. Some of these are Picnic, Chomp, Kit Kat, Polywailles and Chokito.

Mr SHIPTON-Seriously, they are taxed at 17t per cent. The honourable member for Lalor points to the clock. I am cognisant of the time. Perhaps the Treasurer might look at those items in relation to chocolate coated biscuits. I support the sales tax legislation. I will be pleased and

Mr WEST (Cunningham) (3.54)-1 oppose these iniquitous increases in sales tax. Sales tax is a form of indirect tax. It is most unfair. It is regressive and it bears down disproportionately on low income earners and pensioners as, indeed, all indirect taxation does. What is more, sales tax is inflationary. I draw the attention of the Hous~ to the remarks made by the Treasurer (Mr How\lrd) on 12 March this year when, in renouncing the Government's intention to increase broadly-based consumer taxes, he made this very point. I will return to that matter later. However, it is an easy way for a government which eschews other forms of taxation such as a resource rent tax on nonrenewable mineral exports and increasing corporate taxes-a wealth tax-to generate income.
Instead of seeking by those means to increase revenue this Government in this Budget has increased sales taxes by $890m. The Government increased taxes on the current range of goods, which were not subject to sales tax, by 2t per cent. However there has not just been an increase of 2t per cent. Different items have been increased to 5 per cent, 17t per cent and 30 per cent. I will give the House and all the people of Australia some examples. Furniture, floor coverings, electrical goods such as toasters, jugs and mixing machines, et cetera, are taxed at a rate of 5 per cent. Goods taxed at a rate of 17t are motor cycles, caravans and motor vehicles parts. Industry does not escape. Computers are subject to 17t per cent sales tax. In the highest category, items taxed at a rate of 30 per cent include radio and television receivers, electric shavers, razors and so on. Those are goods 'which the honourable member for Macarthur (Mr Baume) and other honourable members were happy to call luxury goods.
In the second category there are those commodities which are now subject to higher taxation because the Treasurer chooses to say that anomalies exist. That range goes up from total exemption to 30 per cent and from 2+ per cent to 17t per cerit. Some goods in those ranges are television picture tubes and motor vehicle safety belts. Why the Government wants to tax motor vehicle safety belts is beyond me completely. Also included in that range are oil and greases for road vehicles. As if the Gov'ernment is not getting enough money already-$3.3 billion from that levy and it wantsio tax greases and oils for motor cars paid for by motorists. Irtdustry does not escape either because road vehicles used in the mining industry and forklift trucks used in

Sales Tax Amendment Bill 1981

27 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 897

stevedoring that are not carried as ships' equipment will be increased from being exempt to attracting a rate of 17t per cent. Of course, then 'there is a whole new range of goods which are subject to a tax of 2t per cent as was mentioned by the Treasurer in his Speech. These include clothi.ng, kiddies footwear, building materials, books, magazines, newspapers and so on.
The Government has acted in a peculiarly sadistic way against the, building industry. It has increased interest rates on housing loans by 2 per cent in the last four months which has resulted in a $50 per month average increase for an average housing loan of $30,000 over a period of 25 years. It has reduced the amount of Federal funds given to the States for public housing from $584m in real terms six years ago to $262m this year. It has now imposed a new and extra sales tax on the building industry. That must surely be regarded as the final coup de grace.
I want to give the lie to what the honourable member for Macarthur was saying. He said that a lot of increases in sales tax will not be passed on. He said that they will be rather insignificant. The Housing Industry Association calculates that a modest brick veneer cottage will cost an extra $500 to build. The Guild of Manufacturers estimates that furnishing a home will cost an extra $500. That is $1,000 extra to start with. These figures do not include basic electrical appliances such as refrigerators, stoves, vacuum cleaners, television sets and freezers. The price of a large television set will increase from between $80 to $150. The honourable member for Macarthur must be mad to say that these are insignificant increases. Television picture tubes will increase by 30 per cent and will cost an extra $100 to $150. The National Roads and Motorists Association has calculated that the price of a 6-cylinder Holden Commodore will rise, as my colleague the honourable member for Banks (Mr Mountford) has said, by $175 at the very least.The cost of a 4cylinder Datsun will rise by $170.
This Government has viciously and carefully devised a taxation onslaught, not against corporations, but against wage and salary earners and pensioners. The figures on page 227 of Budget Paper No. I point out that total income taxes have risen by 22.2 per cent, total sales taxes have increased by a massive 38.6 per cent, but company tax has only increased by 10.1 per cent. I might add that although it is not as much this year, reveue from the iniquitous oil levy has risen, by 10.7 per cent to $3.35 billion, which is an enormous impost on the motorists of Australia. Of course that comes about by charging $A30.79 per barrel for Bass Strait oil that costs a dollar per

barrel to produce. From that $3.35 billion so raised, 50 per cent goes to the Government, less the $2.70 per barrel-the old Gorton charge-to the company, which is Esso-BHP, and the other 50 per cent goes to the company less the $3 per barrel levy to the Government. Not content with this super $3.35 billion ripoff, this Government is going to jack up the sales tax on oil and grease by 17t per cent. As my colleagues have said, we calculate that this will reduce living standards for an average family by approximately $4 a week.
To this can be added the further impost of personal income tax. Let me give an example: A wage and salary earner with a dependent spouse earning $15000 a year and receiving a 13.5 per cent salary increase during this financial year will see his income tax rise by almost $11 a week, on top of the $4 extra in sales tax. So that family is paying an extra $15 a week. This is the highest taxing government in the history of the Australian nation. We can go further and look at health insurance charges. Those who are not now in private health insurance funds, after I September will be faced with a $10 to $18 a week extra charge for health insurance right through the range for whichever rate they choose. Those who already have health insurance are faced with an increase of $1.60 a week. As a result of this Budget, this accumulating pile of extra charges on families can be added to that. Those who are paying off housing loans are faced with increasing interest rates which, as I said, have risen by 2 per cent in the last four months. That means an extra $50 a month on average for those paying off an average loan of $30 000. So if a family happened to fit into all these categories-that is, has a dependent spouse, was not previously paying health insurance and was paying off a modest housing loan-as a result of this Budget it would be liable for anything up to an extra $40 per week out of its disposable income.
The point I want to make is that this Government is a government of broken promises. As the Treasurer is sitting at the table, I refer him to his speech of 12 March this year which refers, firstly to inflation and secondly, broken promises. With regard to inflation, the Treasurer said:
As a general rule. each additional $SOOm.raised through indirect tax.es results in direct and indirect additions to the consumer price index. of almost one per cent.
That is, $500m equals a one per cent increase. Well, the Treasurer should do his own sums. In this case $890m equals 1.7 per cent over a year. He is prepared to jack up inflation in this country by 1.7 per cent. We heard it from his own mouth. I turn now to the broken promises. In that same speech the treasurer put all these arguments

about how repressive taxes were, about how inflationary they were, about how easy to impose they were and how much revenue they were creating. He then arrived at this startling conclusion:
Thererore. and despite obvious advantages rrom many other points or view. the Government came firmly to the conclusion that the inevitable squandering of the gains made and maintained in the fight against inflation in recent years was a price it was not prepared to pay.
The Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) said:
A great deal or hysterical nonsense has been reported about taxation. The Treasurer has said that he does not favour a Value Added Tax. I agree with. him.
So what does the Treasurer say about that? Inflation has risen by 1.6 per cent as a result of these increased taxes. That is another broken promise to add to the Government's litany. It is the history of other countries, especially the United Kingdom which resorted to high rates 6f indirect taxation, to have increasing unemployment. I might add that the United Kingdom has a 15 per cent value added tax as aresult of the 1979 Thatcher Budget. Since then, there have been record rises in unemployment in the United Kingdom, approaching three million at the present time. Unemployment has increased by 300,000 since the last Budget in the United Kingdom in April.
I conclude my remarks by reiterating our challenge to certain senators in the Upper House who might be having second thoughts about their attitudes-senators who were elected by the people of Australia on a campaign slogan of keeping governments honest. All I can say is: Why not keep them honest? This Government has no mandate for what it is doing here. Unless the Australian Democrats want to be known throughout Australia as the dithering and divided Democrats they will keep their campaign promise. These tax Bills are not supply Bills, they are money Bills, similar to Bills which have been voted against by Opposition parties in this place over many years, similar to income tax amendment Bills, oil excise duty Bills and similar to the Medibank levy legislation which this present Government rejected when we were in gqvernment. Therefore, it is idle for any member of the Australian Democrats to say either in the Senate or outside it that the people of Australia will be satisfied with an attitude that the Australian Democrats will send the Bills ba~k to the. House of Representatives for amendment and if that request is not met the Democrats will not oppose the Bills, because in that sense they will be removing the greatest lever they have, the one which the people of Australia entrusted them to use against this Government. We urge them to show strength and to join with Labor in opposing this iniquitous package of sales

tax Bills which this Government promised not to introduce.
Mr ROBERT BROWN (Hunter) (4.7)-1 think it is quite apparent from what has been said so far and from the record of the Australian Labor Party both in government and in opposition, that the ALP is uncompromising in its opposition to these proposals and will continue to resist what quite clearly amount to unjust, inequitable and indefensible attempts on the part of this Government to extract from ordinary Australian people and ordinary Australian families progressively increasing proportions of the total tax grab. We are uncompromising in our opposition and I endorse entirely the suggestion made by the honourable member for Cunningham (Mr West) in appealing to the Australian Democrats in the Senate to honour the undertaking they have given to the Australian people, the only reason they occupy those positions in the Senate at present.
These Bills are undoubtedly discriminatory fiscal measures which are designed simply to raise money. I know there has been some attempt on the part of the Government and on the part of the Treasurer (Mr Howard) to wrap up the policies in some type of economic jargon and some socially responsible sounding cliches. But in fact they remain simply provisions to obtain more money. In the process of doing so, the Government satisfies its ideological commitment to the most affluent sections of the Australian community.
Some of these attempts to clothe these and other taxing measures in respectable garb have included expressions-we are becoming bored with their continuous repetition-of this kind: The Government is attempting to adjust the tax mix; the Government is looking for more appropriate reliance on indirect taxes; the Government is seeking to allow the Australian people more opportunity to decide how they will spend their incomes. Of course, the implication of those attitudes is that if taxes are taken from people at the time they earn their income their capacity to decide how they will spend it will be reduced; but if the taxes are taken from them at the time when they spend their income, in some magical and mystical way, the capacity of these income earners to spend money is not in any way reduced. Of course that is not the case and no amount of cliche spruking on the part of Government spokesmen will make it the case.
This year, indirect taxes will provide the Commonwealth Government with a total of $1 1,273m-an increase over last year's figure of $9,820m. I said that our record both in opposition and in governmeritindicates that we will not in
,

Sales Tax Amendment Bill 1981

27 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 899

(
any way support the proposition that indirect taxes should provide a larger proportion of total taxation revenue. When we came into government, in 1972-73 the proportion of total Commonwealth revenue raised by indirect taxes was 30.1 per cent, which was far too high. The Labor Government set about to reduce that percentage. By the time we were unceremoniously dismissed from office, in our last Budget in 1975-76, we had reduced the percentage to 28.4. As we might expect, the Government which is now in office has maintained a constant, regular and continuing attempt to build up the percentage again. During this Government's term it has increased from 28.4 per cent to 29.8 per cent and it continues to rise. For example, since 1975-76 sales tax revenue has increased by 107 per cent. During the same time, the increase in the consumer price index has been 80 percent.
Government spokesmen have attempted to suggest that this adjustment from equitable and direct taxation to inequitable, unjust, indirect taxation should continue because the proportion of total taxation revenue in Australia which comes from indirect tax is lower than it is elsewhere. This is simply not the case. The Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development figures indicate that indirect taxes on consumption in Australia as a proportion of total taxation is 27.4 per cent. Let me compare that figure with some other OECD countries. In Canada, it is 24.5 per cent; a lower percentage. It is 14.9 per cent in Japan, 21.7 per cent in New Zealand, 24.6 per cent in the United Kingdom and 14.9 per cent in the United States of America. The OECD average is 26.9 per cent, which is lower than the percentage prevailing in Australia. How this Government can possibly justify this continuing change to indirect taxes as a proportion of total taxation which it knows is an inequitable, unjust, indefensible imposition on ordinary Australian families and income earners is completely incomprehensible.
Concern in connection with this action is not shared only by members of the Opposition. When the honourable member for Macarthur (Mr Baume) was speaking earlier, he was most concerned that a newspaper like the Sydney Morning Herald should editorialise against these proposals. It is not only the Sydney Morning Herald which did so. Less than a week ago, the Canberra Times, referred to indirect taxes as 'socially regressive taxes.' It also stated that 'the sales tax provisions are iniquitous'. That is not a soft term. It is a damning term from a responsible editorial in a responsible newspaper. It does not matter how cliche ridden statements from the Treasurer

and this Government become on matters of this kind; nothing will detract from the fact that these proposals are designed to rip off ordinary Australian families.
The Treasurer does not need to be told that if a pensioner-I am referring to another indirect tax-fiUs his petrol tank at a cost of $20, $11 of the money coming out of his pocket goes into the national coffers. If someone earning $100,000 a year fiUs his petrol tank at a cost of $20, $11 goes from his pocket to the national coffers. How inequitable! How unjust! How discriminatory! How sinful it is that a government should actively pursue with all the vigour at its disposal a continuous transfer of the responsibility of meeting national expenditure by increasingly placing this burden on the shoulders of those people in the Australian community who are least able to bear it. Examples have been given of some of the proposed changes. I said initiaUy that they are simply a means of raising revenue. It does not matter how much they are couched in jargon. How is it possible for example-this point has been made before by others but let me repeat it-for any government to justify an increase from no sales tax to 17t per cent sales tax on motor ve-. hicLe safety belts?
Mr West-It is ridiculous.
Mr ROBERT BROWN-It is ridiculous and absurd. It quite obviously results from the fact that most of the State governments-perhaps all of them-have now made it compulsory for the motor vehicle owner-not only the person earning $100,000 a year or more but also the pensioner-to equip his motor vehicle with safety belts. The Government and the Treasurer have attempted to take advantage of this requirement by putting a sales tax of 17+ per cent on motor vehicle safety belts. It was suggested-the details are given in Budget Paper No. I-that these changes were designed to overcome anomalies and inconsistencies. Were they indeed? Let me give an example. Certain protective equipment, not for use in the course of industrial operations, was changed from an exempt tax classification to a 17t per cent rate. Protective clothing-not equipment certainly-of a kind used for sport or recreation which was previously exempt will now be taxed at 15 per cent. A table designed to overcome anomalies and inconsistencies indeed!
This further confirms the point that I have made and on which I will finish. These provisions are designed simply to rip off ordinary Australian people to an increasing extent, to make sure that the ordinary families increasingly carry a bigger share of the burden as wen as those people who

900 REPRESENTATIVES 27 August 1981

Sales Tax Amendment Bill 1981

are finding it increasingly difficult to buy and furnish a home. We know that, as a result of these provisions, the cost of merely constructing a home will increase by between $750 and $1,000. The cost of furnishing the home will increase by about $500. The cost of providing all the electrical equipment and appliances within the home will take the price up further. Those 40,000 families on the Housing Commission list in New South Wales, and the 250,000 families in Australia living in caravans and all the people who are looking for an opportunity at some time in the future to provide themselves and their families with homes will have that opportunity taken further from their grasp, the Treasurer and the Government can take the credit for that situation.
Mr HOWARD (Bennelong-Treasurer) (4.19)-in reply-I thank those members who have participated in this debate. First of all, I remind the House that any taxation changes, unpopular as they inevitably are if they involve increases in taxation for any section of the community, have to be seen in their overall economic context as part of a Budget which seeks to achieve an overall economic objective. At this time, amongst other .things, the economic objective is to take pressure off interest rates which is a very important cause to all Australia's families, and the entire country. Therefore, it is important to see these changes within the context Of the Budget because they are clearly an integral part of the Budget and cannot be severed from it. It has been our view from the beginning that they are central to the Budget. That has been and will remain the view of the Government on this legislation.
It is also important to try to preserve a sense of perspective about what is involved in these changes. One of the things that intrigued me about the debate and about the contributions of members of the Opposition to the debate was that they could not quite make up their minds whether the Government was effecting a major change in the taxation system or whether it was not such a major change at all. They did not quite come to grips with that issue. They could not quite determine what their attitude was.
There can be no doubt that these measures represent a broadening of the existing wholesale taxation base. I do not dispute that. I do not argue that. I make no apology for that. I see virtue in improving the taxation system by achieving a better mix-I apologise to the honourable member for Hunter (Mr Robert Brown) for the use of the jargon-between direct and indirect taxation. But one ought not to lose one's sense of perspective or one's sense of proportion about it. One ought to

remember that when this anti-indirect tax party over there, the Australian Labor Party, was in office, sales tax increased at an annual rate of 19.1
per cent. Yet in the years that this Government has been in office, including this year with these sinful imposts, as the honourable member for Hunter calls them, sales tax increases will average 12.7 per cent annually. That is an example of what an anti-indirect tax party is. So even according to their own principles and their own criteria, they are not being really fair dinkum.

Mr West-Why was it wrong in March and

right in August?

.

Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Giles)~Order! The honourable member will resume his seat.

Mr West-Why was it wrong-

Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER-Order! The honourable member for Cunningham has made his speech. The Treasurer is entitled to express his opinion. I ask him to listen to it.

Mr HOWARD-Thank you, Mr Deputy Speaker. We have to preserve a sense of perspective. Naturally these changes represent a broaden-
ing of the wholesale tax base. As my statement to the House in March of this year has attracted so much attention in this debate-I am very grateful that the honourable member for Cunningham (Mr West) has read it carefully, as have a number of his colleagues-I would like to address a couple of the arguments that have been used by the Opposition arising out of that speech. The one that has been used most often is the one-I think I summarise it fairly-which says: 'In March you said that if there was a significant change in the indirect tax take you would have a compensating offset in personal taxation reductions'. That is the reference to my March speech which has been most frequently invoked by the Opposition.

It is true that that was said at the time and it is true that the Government said at the time that that could happen if there were a major change in the mixture of tax in the community. The sort of change under contemplation then included taxation of services and the possibility of a broad based retail turnover tax. For a short time it even included the possibility of a value added tax, but as everybody will know that was dismissed fairly early in the piece and remains an option that is
very unattractive to the present Government and, I would think, to most people who have experienced the operation of value added taxes in Europe. But there was nothing in what we said in March which constituted a promise that whenever there was an increase in indirect tax collections, that would be immediately matched by an offset in personal taxation cuts.

Sales Tax Amendment Bill 1981

27 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 901

It is true that, when the Government was putting the Budget together, the possibility of applying some of the proceeds of the increased indirect taxation this year towards a personal taxation cut was canvassed at some length. The decision was ultimately taken that the economic value of a lower deficit and, therefore, less pressure on interest rates was more important at present than a marginal personal tax adjustment. The honourable member for Hunter may dispute the Government's priorities in that regard, as he is entitled to do. He is entitled to say that it is more important for the average family to have a marginal tax cut or to have the possibility of interest rates coming down. That is a value judgment that any member of this House is entitled to make. But let there be no doubt that there was not in these measures a breach of commitment in any sense, shape or form on the part of the Government.
The second thing I would like to say something about is the question of living standards and disposable incomes. The Opposition made great play during the debate of the impact of these taxation changes on the living standards of ordinary Australians. That is obviously an important and proper concern to be raised in a debate of this kind. Last year, 1980-81, household disposable incomes in Australia rose by about 3.1 per cent. The best estimates available to me and to the Government indicate that real household disposable incomes in Australia in 1981-82 will rise at about the same rate. In other words, they will rise at about 3.1 per cent. The material to support that statement is to be found on page 55 of Statement No.2 of the Budget Papers.
True it is that there will be price rises-I will come to that in a moment-and true it is that a number of hems will be more expensive as a result of these sales tax increases. But once again in order to preserve a sense of perspective and a sense of proportion, we ought not to forget, as the honourable member for Macarthur (Mr Baume) reminds us, that indirect tax last year rose at a greater rate than it is expected to rise this year. We ought not to lose sight of that fact; nor should we lose sight of the fact that real household disposable incomes will be as high this year as they were last year.
I come then to the question of price effects. Of course there will be price effects and in a number of areas the price effects will be aggravated by retail mark-ups. I have not denied that and I know perfectly well that that occurs. But equally there will be competitive pressures on certain industries. The Australian newspaper, which has followed the sales tax rises with some interest and in some detail, as recently as today quoted this view:

There is a growing feeling among manufacturers and retailers that they may have to absorb much of the sales tal( in their margins . . . Mr Brian Walsh, general manager of David Jones . . . said in Sydney yesterday that regardless of the effects of the tal(, retailers would have to mark their goods at prices the market would accept.
I refer to those comments simply to illustrate that whereas there will be retail mark-ups on top of the sales tax increase, equally in other respects there will be cases where those increases are absorbed. Indeed, in some areas they ought to be absorbed. I think of those quota holders at present in Australia who, as a result of the appreciation of the Australian dollar, are having their positions significantly improved. Because consumer demand is still very much in excess of the. availability for sale of imported goods, and as a result of lower import prices, their profit position is significantly improved. I think people in that sort of situation might look at whether the consumer of their goods deserves a little more consideration in the light of the windfall they have gained from the appreciation of the Australian dollar.
They are general remarks about the context in which the sales tax increases have taken place. Of course, precise estimates of the price effects are very difficult to make. I simply remind the House that the price effects, as a result of oil price rises, were 1.3 per cent in 1978-79, I.5 per cent the following year and 0.8 per cent last year. It is expected that the price effects of these sales tax rises will be about the same order as they were last year. Therefore, they will have an effect of under or about one per cent during 1981-82.
The only other matters to which I will refer relate to the timing of the implementation of these changes and to the category covering inconsistencies and anomalies. The first thing I will say about the category called inconsistencies and anomalies is that we are dealing here with an area where for one reason or another over the years goods of a similar kind to those which have been included in the schedule to which the honourable member for Hunter referred have been taxed at some or other level of sales tax. It is because of this that it is fair to argue that a degree of inconsistency or anomaly exists. I will give one example. Before the Budget, alarm clocks were taxed at a lower rate than all other clocks and watches, and that the situation prevailed since 1946. I have not been able to discover precisely why that anomaly crept in in 1946. There is no logical reason why that should have been the case at that time, and that anomaly clearly cannot be supported in present circumstances.
On a couple of occasions in the House I have been reminded of the fact that a 17t per cent tax rate now applies for trucks that are used for grain

902 REPRESENTATIVES 27 August 1981

Sales Tax Amendment Bill 1981

haulage. That may sound unreasonable until it is recognised that trucks used for all other purposes have been subjected to the general rate of 15 per cent for many years. As a consequence people who have owned trucks and have used them for grain haulage purposes have for many years enjoyed an advantage over other haulers and users of trucks. In the circumstances those other people are entitled to ask why that competitive advantage should continue to be enjoyed. On the subjects of anomalies and the increases in the existing rate, I again remind the House, as I did this morning at Question Time, that precisely the same procedure has been followed in respect of these sales tax changes as has been followed in the past by governments of both political persuasions in this place when indirect taxation changes were made.
In 1967 a wide range of anomalies or inconsistencies were corrected. The tax rate on a number of items increased from 12t per cent to 25 per cent. Such items included tape recorders, tape players and similar appliances, parts and accessories, television slides, photographic equipment, certain musical instruments and sound tape recordings, and the increases were introduced over night. I also remind the house that in 1975 when the former Labor Government significantly increased indirect tax-the excise-on liquor and tobacco those increases also applied from Budget night. It has been the practice ever since the Commonwealth Government has levied indirect taxation in this country for changes in existing rates and changes that can be implemented immediately to apply from the date of announcement. We have followed that practice no more no less on this occasion. It is simply not practicable, from an administrative point of view, for changes to be implemented in advance of 1 January on items that are being made subject to sales tax for the first time. That is why a different procedure is being applied to them. I make it pluperfect clear that the practice we are following is no more disruptive and no less considerate of the convenience of the business community or Australian consumers than has been the practice in the past.
I have also been asked a question as to what is the impact of these changes upon existing contracts. Wholesale tax applies at the final point of wholesale, and that has been a provision of the sales tax law in this country since 1941. It will be found in section 70A of the Sales Tax Assessment Act (No.1) that unless there is a contrary provision in a contract, a sales tax increase will apply immediately and the effect of that increase will add to the price of the goods covered by the contract. In other words unless parties to a contract

have agreed in advance and excluded the operation of that section, it has always been part of the
law that the increase will be given effect to and passed on ultimately to the person who acquires the goods.

I conclude by repeating two things. These measures are integral to the Budget. They are part of an overall economic package. They contribute in a material way to achieving the overall Budget outcome which I believe and the Government believes in the immediate future will contribute to economic stability and will preserve the very significant economic gains that this country has made over the last 5t years. They repr.esent a modest but nonetheless significant contribution to an improvement in the mixture of taxation in the Australian community. They do, of course, significantly enhance the potential for personal tax relief in future years.

That the Bill be now read a second time.

COMMITTEE OF REFERENCE FOR DEFENCE FORCE PAY
. Ministerial Statement
Mr KILLEN (Moreton-Minister for Defence)-by leave-I am pleased to be able to announce that the Government has approved increases in pay and some allowances for members of the Defence Force, These increases stem from a report on work value change from the Committee of Reference for Defence Force Pay, which was presented to me on 20 August 1981. The Committee of Reference is an independent body under the chairmanship of Mr Justice p, A, Coldham, a Deputy President of the Conciliation

and Arbitration Commission, which advises the Minister for Defence (Mr Killen) on Services' pay, An interim report containing recommendations on changes in work value was presented to me on 31 October 1980 and subsequently implemented, The increases now recommended by the Committee on work value change result in total increases in Service salaries of something less than 7 per cent. In addition increases in allowances which have a 'remuneration' content in the context of work value have been recommended as well,
The examination of changes in work value stem from the principles of wage determination introduced by the Conciliation and Arbitration Commission in April 1975. It follows the recent spate of work value pay increases granted in the community and other areas of Commonwealth employment. The basis for the recommended salary increases is unaffected by the abandonment of indexation by the Commission in July 1981. Salaries for medical and dental officers in the ranks captain to colonel, army and equivalent, are derived from specific alignments with medical classifications in the Australian Public Service. The Committee of Reference has recommended that increases for these categories, which stem from work value change, should await completion of the review of work value cases within the jurisdiction of the Public Service Arbitrator, In assessing the quantum of salary increases, the Committee of Reference has adopted the 'averaging' approach which was a feature of the assessment of work value change in the general community.
Of the allowances affected, service allowance, which is paid principally as recompense for the non-payment of overtime and the need to work long and irregular hours, has been increased in concert with salary increases, The salary and service allowance increases resulting from work value change are to operate from 22 July 1980, that is, the date I referred the present reference to . the Committee of Reference. The revised rates recommended by the Committee have been further adjusted effective from 15 January 1981 and 7 May 1981 to reflect national wage decisions made effective from those dates, The rates of work value related allowances raised in the report are to be effective from 4 June 1981.
The Committee of Reference has recommended that the increases be extended to members of the Reserve Force from the same dates of effect. The second report completes the task of the Committee of Reference under its terms of reference with respect to work value changes, In this context, The Committee of Reference has advised:

Committee for Defence Force Pay

27 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 905

The assessment of work value change has been an extensive exercise involving a large and vibrant military industry which extends through a broad range of employments. To have short-circuited the task of assessing salary movement considered to be applicable would have been to stultify the application of wage fixing principles in the services.
Work value change resulting from new policies and advances in technology is extensively detailed in the report. Material on changes was obtained through inspections, information/data supplied by the Department of Defence and the service offices, exhaustive interviews with teams of service representatives, and over 1,600 individual submissions.
For my part, I acknowledge and thank the Committee for its many months of sustained effort. The current terms of reference require the Committee of reference to report further on a range of other matters. The report dealing with these matters is expected later this year.
I present the following paper:
Committee of Reference for Defence Force PayMinisterial statement, 27 August 1981.
Motion (by Mr Sinclair) proposed.
That the House take note of the paper.
Mr SCHOLES (Corio) (5.4)-The Opposition welcomes the statement of the Minister for Defence (Mr Killen) on this reference on service salaries, which is now over a year old. The Opposition is concerned with delays in this type of reference and the very long periods which exist between consideration of the overall rates of Defence Force salaries. This is the first report of this nature for several years. Other sections of the community are able to initiate inquiries into their levels of salary-their work related levels-on the basis of an application to a tribunal. Members of the Defence Force are inhibited in this area in that there is no body to which they can make an application nor is there a body which can initiate or agree to hear that application. To that extent, we believe that the mechanism for dealing with Defence Force pay and related matters is unsatisfactory and is less than that which is available to other members of the community.
I have not at this stage had ~he opportunity of seeing the full report. I hope that the Minister will make that available shortly. But the average 7 per cent increase which is mentioned in his statement would seem to be a fairly low level of average increase given the time since the last similar review of work values. It is not an increase which would seem to be comparable with the overall increase in salaries and allowances in the general community which have flowed from the various sources of increase that takes place. I am aware that the national wage case decisions have in fact been automatically passed on to the Services. As indicated in this House on an earlier occasion

when the method of dealing with reports by tribunals on the Services was before this House in the form of a Bill, the Opposition believes that it is necessary that the tribunal should have a capacity to initiate its own hearings into defence force pay and also that there should be a capacity whereby the view of the Services can be put and argued before that tribunal.
The present system may well have the effect of retaining an averaging provision whereby the Services' movements in salaries are somewhat in line with those of the general community. I think that is the result of this report. All other sections of the Australian community have the opportunity to present or have presented on their behalf argument in support of the levels of salaries and the changes in conditions which have taken place. The Defence Force is a substantive section of the Australian community. It is also a section of the Australian community which, in order to obtain that type of employment, has to meet extremely high entry standards. According to the last figure that I have seen, only one in six of those who seek to enter the Defence Force are in fact accepted; yet it is doubtful-in fact I think it is extremely doubtful-that the levels of remuneration in the Defence Force reflect that high level of requirement for entry.
I hope that at some time in the future the Minister, the Government-if not this Government, a Labor Government-will introduce a system whereby an office of defence force advocate is created. We hope that it will operate out of the Defence Force Ombudsman's office and will, on behalf of servicemen, initiate inquiries and seek to have the tribunal examine specific matters. The Minister will b~ well aware of the problems which existed in Bendigo over a long period until this reference. I hope those problems have been resolved by this reference, but until such time as the report is examined this will not be known. Seriously, whilst the members of the Defence Force lack the capacity to have argument presented on their behalf, I believe the best method would be the one I have suggested, and also whilst the Minister or the Government has the power to reject those reports-to my knowledge no such report has been rejected by merely taking no action, the members of the Defence force are disadvantaged in respect of pay and conditions. I believe they have an entitlement to have their pay and conditions dealt with in exactlythe same way as other members of the Public Service and, if I may say so, members of the Parliament. If the reports of the tribunals concerned are unacceptable-to a majority of members of Parliament, disallowance provisions should exist. But the tribunal's report

906 REPRESENTATIVES 27 August 1981

Remuneration and Allowances Bill

should, subject to that, be binding on the Government of the day.
I think that there will be considerable disappointment among members of the Defence Force in the increases which have been granted. As I said, they most likely reflect the average movements which the Austraalian Conciliation and Arbitration Commission has granted in its deliberations, and probably some component for those payments made to especially skilled grades outside. But the Services, like other areas of the Commonwealth Public Service, are losing highly skilled people to outside ind~stry because the conditions of the Services-the pay and other facilities----:are substantially less than those which are available in private employment. The best skills that are available are in fact being bought off those who have done the training-the Services. We cannot afford to allow that sort of thing to continue. I hope that a better method of determining Defence Force salaries and conditions and a more realistic approach to the value of the personnel.can be adopted in the future.
Question resolved in the affirmative.
REMUNERATION AND ALLOWANCES AMENDMENT BILL 1981
Second Reading
Debate resumed.
Mr HAYDEN (Oxley-Leader of the Oppo~ sition) (5.9)-The Opposition does not oppose the Remuneration and Allowances Amendment Bill 1981. It is a Bill which proposes adjustments in salaries and allowances for members of the Parliament, seniQr publip servants, justices and judges. It will be. recalled that, during th~ recess, the Remuneration Tribunal report was released and a pUblic statement was made as to the Government's intention in respect of that report. Subsequently, the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) returned from overseas. Adjustments were made to that initial proposal declared on behalf of the Cabinet. We now have the tJtird episode in this development. I trust that it is the last one. But I think one can observe fairly in passing that there seems to have been pretty fair evidence in this area, as there seems to have been in some other areas from time to time, of the Case oftbe mice playing when the big tom cat was away. I hope that when he comes back they will not go into their holes and try to change this again.
Mr Hawke-Their funkholes.
Mr HAYDEN-Exactly. I am grateful to the honourable member for Wills for his contribution. Some of us are more skilled than others in the use of the English language.

Let me state in summary what is prol"osed in

this Bill. It means that parliamentarians---cthat is,

members of this House and members of the

Senate-will get an.increase in salary of about 10

per cent this year. There will be another increase

of, 1 would expect, a little less than 10 per cent

next-year. That means, in effect, that the basic sal-

ary for members of both Houses of this Parlia-

ment will have increased by about the increase in

the cost of living in the past 12 months. It will be a

little over, certainly, but in the next 12 months, on

the forecast for inflation which has been provided

in the Budget, the increase will be a little less.

Over the two-year period it will just about bal-

ance out.

.

I cite some statistics for the record to reassure those people who may suspect that the movement over time in the basic salaries of members of this Parliament has been too generous. The facts are that the adjustments have lagged well behind wage movements in the community. If we take average weekly earnings for the period from early 1975 to the beginning of this fiscal year, the movement was 93 per cent. It we take the increase in a parliamentarian's salary from th~ beginning' of that period to which I have just referred to the beginning of this fiscal year on the basis of what was originally proposed by the Remuneration Tribunal, the increase would have been 80 per cent behind the increase in average weekly earnings. The. adjusted salary increase for the period stated will beonly 65 per cent. Effectively there is a difference of almost. 30 per cent between movements in average weekly earnings and movements in the basic salary of members of this Parliament.

!tought to be borne in mind, of course, that the determination of partiamentarians' salaries is made by an independent tribunal. It is not declared by self-interested members of parliament
as part of the normal process of the parliament. After careful consideration, recommendations are
made by three people who comprise the Remuneration Tribunal-Mr Justice Campbell, Mr Grimwade and Mr Porter. I recall clearly that
prior to -the setting up of this Tribunal it was always asserted by the media that it was inappropriate for membe~s of this Parliament to be setting
their salaries themselves. within the normal proceduresof the Parliament. It was argued by so many people in the media that what was required
was an independent tribunal to make that deter-
mination. That was done. It has not in any way discouragedthe critical comment of the media-we saw;that a few weeks ago. We are not
the only body of people whose salaries and rates of allowances are determined by separate and independent tribunals. There is a whole range of

Remuneration and Allowances Bill

27 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 907

them. For instance the academics have their tribunal. From recollection, the air pilots have their tribunal. Quite a number of these tribunals are operating in the community quite separately because of the particular nature of the area which is covered by the purview of the tribunaL For instance, the medical practitioners have an independent tribunal .which regularly considers their claims for fee adjustments in relation to health insurance arrangements. This decision was made by an independent tribunal. In spite of that, there was opposition to it in the community.
I put on record again my view in this matter. I did so quite directly' in the immediate aftermath of the release of this report. I restate it: I believe that the salary and allowance proposals which were determined by the Tribunal were justified. I do not accept that members of parliament should be expected to accept salary rates which are inferior to those which are available outside. The corporate sector pays quite generous salaries to people which responsibilities of an equivalent nature to those of members of parliament, to those people in the ministry who are office holders and certainly to the Prime Minister. In all cases the salaries are so much more generous than that provided under present arrangements for those people serving the nation through this Parliament. I have no embarrassment at all in declaring that members of parliament should receive adequate remuneration for the responsibilities that they have, just as I believe that justices and judges of the High Court of Australia and the federal court system in this country deserve adequate recompense to allow them to live with the proper standard of dignity and independence which is appropriate for people in those important positions.
Similarly, if we do not pay decent salaries to our top public servants and to people who are in senior positions in government instrumentalities, semi-government instrumentalities and statutory bodies-however one describes them-they will not stay there. The evidence is that because the rates of remuneration are so much more generous outside we are losing far too many top rate people from bodies such as the Commonwealth Bank and, as I noticed this morning, the Treasury. We will lose them from other Public Service departments. Those people have developed skills, they have abilities which they have refined and they want to make a valuable contribution, but they will not make it at considerable sacrifice to the standard of living and the sence of security that they are capable of achieving, particularly in their retirement, from alternative sources of employment that offer just as much stimulation and fulfilment as is available in the public sector. I

do not believe that service in the public sector shoulq require sacrifice of a degree which far exceeds that which would be expected in any other sector of the community.
I have declared my position quite clearly. I do so without any reservation or any embarrassment and I will continue to do so. If we respect the role of parliament, if we have a commitment to the standard of service which should go into supporting the community through this institution and if the public accepts that it requires the best standards and the best qualities that can be. extracted from the community in service in this Parliament, the public in turn has to accept that the level of remuneration will have to be adequate to attract such people and to retain them. One accepts as a caveat that there is more than just remuneration for service in the Parliament. Overwhelmingly, people here have a sence of mission, they have ideals, they have a commitment about their society. They have their philosophies, they have their idealogies and these things guide them and inspire them. That is why they come into this place to serve. We have our differences on these grounds but collectively, I think we would agree, we are working for what we believe to be a better Australia. We have our philosophical differences in this respect. We have our ideological conflicts. But we do this genuinely and in terms of reflecting substantial proportions of the public's view on this matter. Therefore, I believe that in serving the community we should have no embarrassment, feel no discomfort at all, in accepting a certain level ot' remuneration and certain allowances when an independent tribunal determines after very careful consideration that they are appropriate.

BROADCASTING STATIONS LICENCE FEES AMENDMENT BILL (No.2) 1981
Second Reading
Debate resumed from 18 August, on motion by Mr Sinclair:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
Mr SINCLAIR (New England-Minister for Communications)-Mr Deputy Speaker, may I have your indulgence to suggest that the House has a general debate covering this Bill and the Television Stations Licence Fees Amendment Bill 1981, as they are associated measures. Separate questions will, of course, be put on each of the Bills at the conclusion of the debate.
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Hon. Charles Jones)-Is it the wish of the House to have a general debate covering these two measures. There being no objection, I will allow that course to be followed.
Mr DAWKINS (Fremantle) (S.21)-These ,Bills relate to licence fees for broadcasting and television stations. The proposals in the two Bills will change the formula under which the fees are charged to ensure that those stations with the greatest earnings will pay more under the new arrangements than they would have paid under the old arrangements and that those stations with smaller revenues will pay less under the new arrangements than they would have paid under the old arrangements. As well, the rates at which the fees are charged will be also increased. This will have the effect of increasing the revenue from these fees from about $21.Sm to about $31.5m. About half of that increase is due to the rearrangement of the formula and about half of the increase is due to the increase of the rates at which the fees are changed.
I point out from the outset that the Opposition does not oppose these Bills. I do not intend to speak at great length on these Bills because they are non-contentious. I. expect them to pass through this House and through the Senate without any great opposition. But it is, perhaps, important for me to take a couple of moments to indicate the principles on which the Opposition has approached this question. The first point to be made of course is that radio broadcasters and television stations operate on licences issued by the Government. The number of those licences is limited. They bestow quite substantial. privileges on those people who hold those licences. As well, it must be remembered that this is an industry which is a very highly regulated industry. It is

highly regulated to ensure that there is no abuse by the licence holders of the privileged position that they hold by way of their licences. There is also substantial regulation to ensure that there is a maintenance of high standards of broadcasting in this country. The third objective of the high degree of regulation is to ensure that the broadcasting and television industry acts broadly in the public interest and also there is a need to ensure a high degree of accountability in this indu'stry back to the public.
I have said that the issue of these licences does bestow great advantages on those licence holders. Some.years ago some people said that a television licence was a licence to print money. That is not universally the case but, certainly, in some areas of this industry, very high profits can be made. The only reason those high profits can be made is that the Government has issued licences to a limited number of people in the community in order that they can broadcast on radio or television. I think. there is a general acceptance in an industry which is highly regulated-or regulated at all for that matter-that it should pay for the cost of that regulation. The Government said, in introducing these Bills, that one of the purposes of increasing these fees was to ensure that the industry pays for the cost of regulation. That is something with which we concur.
However there is a second question which I think is equally, if not more important in considering these Bills. The Government in the second reading speeches when introducing these Bills has not spelt out the argument that there should De some financial return to the public in recognition of the privilege which the public has conferred on those people who hold licences. As I have said, this industry, by and large, is a highly profitable industry. I repeat that that is not to say that every radio and television broadcaster necessarily makes a high profit. But certainly this industry is highly profitable and has become even more profitable in recent times. As a result of that, I think the community is entitled to benefit from the high profitability of that industry.
It is likely that the industry will continue to be profitable because we have seen only in recent times that there have been substantial increases in the advertising rates charged particularly by television. Those rates have not been subject to any justification. They have been rates which have been agreed to by the commercial television stations and simply imposed on the advertisers. They are set at a rate which the industry believes the market will stand. Indeed, in the last year, those advertising rates have increased in Sydney by about 25 per cent and in Melbourne by about
