DEFENCE FORCE RETIREMENT AND DEATH BENEFITS AMENDMENT BILL
1981
Bill presented by Mr Killen, and read a first time.
Second Reading
Mr KILLEN (MDretDn-Minister fDr Defence) (5.35)-1 mDve:
That the Bill be now read for a second time.
The main purpDse of this Bill is to. give effect to. the amendments to. the Defence FDrce Retirement and Death Benefits Act fDrecast in the statement. I made to. the Parliament on 14 May this year. It may assist honourable members in their understanding of the prDblems that are to. be resDlved if I were briefly to. explain again the essential background.
FDr many years, cDntributiDns and retirement benefits payable under the Defence FDrce scheme have been calculated by reference to. the rate of salary payable in respect of the substantive rank held by members. AdditiDnal salary paid on the basis of a higher acting Dr tempDrary rank has been disregarded fDr Defence FDrce retirem'ent benefits and Defence FDrce retirement and death benefits purpDses mainly because of the transitDry nature of the acting and tempDrary rank arrangements.
In May 1980, the Federal CDurt of Australia decided, hDwever, that the intended link between 'rank' as defined in the Defence FDrCe Retirement and Death Benefits Act and the annual rates of pay regulatiDns made under that Act had not been properly established. AccDrdingly, as the legislatiDn nDW stands, acting and tempDrary rank rates of pay are to' be taken into. aCCDunt fDr bDth cDntributiDns and benefits purpDses in respect of all service rendered since the CDmmencement of the scheme on 1 OctDber 1972.
I wDuld emphasise that the Defence FDrce Retirement and Death Benefits AuthDrity has no. discretiDn in this matter. It is Dbliged by the law as it nDW stands to. take cDntributiDns on the basis of acting Dr tempDrary rank. There is no. discretiDn available, and I wDuld hDpe that thDse peDple who. are interested in Dr affected by the amendments wDuld understand that. As a cDnsequence, a relatively small number of beneficiaries, estimated to. be of the Drder of 550, are entitled to' higher retirement benefits; and a very large number of bDth pensiDners and serving members are required to' pay additiDnal cDntributiDns withDut any CDrrespDnding increase in benefits. I wish to. emphasise

Defence Force Retirement Benefits Bill

27 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 913

that. They would have to pay their contributions but there would be no corresponding benefit flowing to them.
I turn now, to the practical effects of the Bill before the Parliament. First, it restores to the scheme" with effeCt from 14 May 1981, the original intention that a member's contributions and retirement benefits be calculated on the basis of the annual rate of pay applicable to substantive rank held by the member during service. Second, it makes special provision for all those persons who during the currency of the present scheme have held acting or temporary rank, in the way I go on to explain.
Pensioners or beneficiaries entitled to additional pension benefits will be paid these benefits in full, including any available commutation amounts, from the date of their retirement. As from 14 May 1981 however, their pensions will be adjusted by reverting to what they would have been if calculated according to the substantive rank held on retirement and taking account of any additional commutation lump sums received.
The pensioners to whom I have just referred, that is those in receipt of or entitled to an additiona,l benefit, will be required to pay additional contributions based on their acting or temporary rank rate of pay but only in respect of that period of acting or temporary rank service that immediately preceded their retirement. All additional contributions that would otherwise be payable in respect of acting or temporary rank are waived. These are the beneficiaries of whom I have spoken who would be obliged to pay contributions on their acting or temporary ranks and yet would not receive any corresponding benefit. In other words, only persons who retired or died while holding acting or temporary rank and who qualify for higher levels of pension benefits are liable to pay additional contributions to the limited extent that I have indicated. Currently serving members will not be disadvantaged by having to pay higher contributions in future because of their temporary higher rank status.
The other amendments which I have taken the opportunity to include in the Bill, involve the rationalisation of the provisions relating to pensioners who re-enter the Defence Force. They are designed principally to cut down the high costs of additional pension and commutation benefits that so frequently become payable to pensioners who re-enter the scheme for relatively brief periods. In summary, they provide that only pensioners who re-enter on an appointment or engagement for a period of not less than one year shall again become contributors to the scheme. On their second

or subsequent retirement they will be entitled to appropriate pension increments for their additional service but be excluded from further access to the commutation provisions. They will no longer be required, however, to repay portion of their commutation proceeds during their period of additional service. Pensioners who reenter for brief periods of less than one year will not again become contributing members, nor will their retirement pensions be cancelled on re-entry as is currently the case. I commend the Bill to the House.
Debate (on motion by Mr Scholes) adjourned.
CUSTOMS (VALUATIONS) AMENDMENT BILL 1981
Bill presented by Mr Moore, and read a first time.
Second Reading
Mr MOORE (Ryan-Minister for Business and Consumer Affairs) (5.41)-1 move:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
The main purpose of this Bill is to introduce into the Customs Act 1901 new provisions for the valuation of imported goods for Customs duty purposes. These new provisions are designed to give effect to the agreement on implementation of Article VII of the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade, GATT. The Government decided in May 1980 to adopt this agreement on a free-onboard basis, FOB, so as to exclude the costs of overseas freight and insurance from the Customs value. This decision took into account the views of State governments, industry associations and other interested groups within the community.
Australia's present system of Customs valuation is based upon the Brussels Definition of Value, BDV. This compares each actual transaction with an ideal transaction establishing an entirely open market value in an arm's length situation. The major difference between the GATT valuation and the BDV valuation is that the GATT valuation is a positive valuation system using the actual prices and charges for imported goods.
I will now take a few moments to describe the methods by which a Customs value will be arrived at under the terms of the provisions in clause 8 of the Bill. For the purposes of the Customs Tariff Act 1966, the value of imported goods is defined by section 156 as the Customs value of the goods. Section 157 sets out six methods, that are to be applied in sequence, for arriving at the Customs value. I expect that the first of these methods will

914 REPRESENTATIVES 27 August 1981

Customs (Valuations) Amendment Bill

be the method most commonly used. This involves the ascertainment of the transaction value of the goods which is determined in accordance with section 159.
The starting point for this method. will be the FOB price of goods when sold in a relevant transaction prior to their arrival in Australia. The terms 'price' and 'relevant transaction' are defined in section 154. Before it forms the basis for the assessment of the transaction value, the price may be adjusted, if necessary, in accordance with sub-section 154 (2). Further adjustments may also be necessary under section 159.
The second and third methods are based on the transaction values of goods which are, respectively, identical or similar to the goods to be valued. Section 155 provides the basis for identifying identical or similar goods. Section 160 sets out the adjustments that may, if necessary, be made to the values of the identical or similar goods for the purposes of valuing the relevant imported goods.
The fourth method is based on the deductive value of the goods ascertained in accordance with section 161. This method takes as its starting point the price of the imported goods, or of identical or similar goods, when sold in Australia. Deductions are then made, as necessary, to take account'of elements reflected in the price because of the transportation of the goods to Australia and their marketing in Australia. The fifth method requires the assessment of the computed value of the goods which is to be calculated in accordance with section 161A. This method is based on the costs of production of the goods, adjusted to take account of the costs and profits that usually would have been reflected in sales of those goods when sold FOB. for export to Australia. If all the methods I have referred to fail to establish a value for Customs purposes, sub-section 157 (8) will allow the Comptroller-General to determine the value. In making such a determination the Comptroller General will be limited by the provisions of subsection 157 (9).
Section 158 explains the circumstances in which a method of valuation cannot be used. Importantly,a method cannot be used if there is a lack of sufficient reliable information for its use. The first method cannot be used in certain cases, particularly where the relationship between the parties to the transaction influenced the price of the goods. Sectionl61C confers a right on the owners of imported goods to be informed of the basis on which their goods were valued. Finally, secton 161D provides that the ComptrollerGeneralmay review any valuation made by a

Customs officer and confirm, vary or replace that valuation with another valuation.
The adoption of the GATT valuation will bring Australia into line with the valuation system practised by other major trading partners such as the United States of America, Japan and the European Economic Community. The Government expects' that it will not have widespread consequences for levels of tariff protection now provided to Australian industry. Reference to the Industries Assistance Commission will however be considered during the first year after introduction of the system if any significant variations in customs duty appear to affect adversely Australian industry or importers. The Commission has already been asked to include in all future reports recommendations as to the appropriate levels of assistance uJ;lder the new system. The new system is also expected to make customs valuation more predictable for importers. It has the support of most main organisations involved in the importation of goods as well as organisations representing local manufacturers.
The other amendments contained in this Bill are of a formal nature. In particular, clauses 6 and 7 propose amendments to section 151 of the Customs Act to delete redundant provisions following the elimination of British preferential tariff margins in the Customs Tariff Act 1966. I commend the Bill to honourable members.
Debate (on motion by Mr Young) adjourned.

Mr JULL (Bowman) (5,49)-Before the debate was interrupted we heard a brief outline of the consequences of the Broadcasting Stations Licence Fees Amendment Bill and the Television Stations Fees Licence Bill from the honourable member for Fremantle (Mr Dawkins). As he rightly said, these are non-contentious Bills. He explained how, although there will be an increase in revenue from the changes in the design of collecting revenue for the licensing of radio and television stations, a fairer system will be worked out. It will result ina number of radio and television stations in Australia paying lower licence fees than they presently pay. In fact, fees will actually be reduced for 120 of the 130 radio stations. I understand that for 20 of the 50 commercial television stations in Australia licence fees will also be reduced. I think they are fairly significant figures.

The honourable member for Fremantle pointed out that it was always one of the cries of the industry that having a television station licence or a radio station licence was certainly a licence to print rnoney. If we look at the way radio and television ate going in Australia at the moment we find that in certain'cases, especially in the case of radio broadcasting, the exact opposite is true. I believe that this year, for the first time in Australia, outdoor advertising, billboard advertising, will take a greater percentage of the total amount of money spent on advertising in Australia than will radio.

The new scale of fees will mean that about 120 radio and 20 television stations will pay less and about 30 television stations and 10 radio stations will pay more. The progressive scale means that the more a person earns the more he pays. This promise has not yet been fulfilled in spite of the publicity, prior to the tast election, of the previous Minister and the former member for the area who turned up to be photographed turning the sod for the commencement of construction of the translator. The previous Minister was full of promises and very short on action, It is to be hoped thfJ.t the present Minister for Communications wiII perform in a more positive manner towards these country people now that funds are being made available through the radio and television licence Bills.
In conclusion, I wish to make a few remarks in support of regional television. The fear of losing national advertising revenue is a genuine worry to many provincial television executives. This is not surprising. Station GVL8 in the Latrobe Valley earned 75 per cent of its total advertising revenue from national advertising in the,last year and 25 per cent from local advertising. there has been an increase in local advertising of sOfIle 4 per cent since 1978-79. The last figures available from the Australian Broadcasting Tribunal show that since the change in that area took place local advertising has increased. Television is a capital intensive industry where high returns are necessary to be able to pay taxes and dividends and to have a reasonable amount of mOriey left over to re-invest in the business. However, high returns on revenue and shareholders' funds does not necessarily mean that regional television stations could stand' a change, such as networking, which would destroy thein overnight. I urge the Government to move warily on issues such as the satelIite system, networking, cable television and other new develop~ ments. The Australian Labor Party believes in a strong commercial sector and supports the Bills, particularly as they represent a step in the right direction with a move to a progressive scale of Ii.: cence fees.

Mr ROBERT BROWN (Hunter) (8.19)- Just prior to the adjournment of the House last evening I drew attention to the increasing burden of total individual taxation which was being placed on the shoulders of pay-as-you-earn taxpayers. I mentioned that during the last 16 years, between 1965-66 and 1981-82, the contribution to total individual taxation paid by pay-as-you-earn taxpayers had increased from 67 per cent to 82.7 per cent. At the same time the contribution made by non-pay-as-you-earn individual taxpayers declined from 33 per cent to 17.3 per cent.
This matter,has been of considerable, continuing and increasing concern to the Opposition. Mr BAUME (Macarthur) (8.25)-One point raised by the honourable member for Hunter (Mr Robert Brown) certainly needs to be commented on. I refer to the curious approach by the Australian Labor Party to the question of tax avoidance. I do not want to dwell on it too much except to say that there has never been a government in Australia's history that has done more to prevent tax avoidance, that has brought in more legislation, that has closed off more tax avoidance schemes and that has saved more revenue than

the present Government. It is intriguing that the Labor Government, whose main contribution to
preventing tax avoidance was to say that it
planned to do something-which it found itself
incompetent to do and which eventually was tidied up by this Government-achieved very
little except to introduce a set of schemes which totally inhibited investment in the mining sector
in Australia. Apart from that, it has an abysmal record on tax avoidance. For it to maintain this phoney criticism of this Government, which has
taken so many positive steps to ensure that the tax burden is more equitably shared around

Appropriation Bill (No.1) 1981-82

27 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 921

Austr,alia, seems to me to be a typical piece of hypocrisy along the lines that we have heard so far in this Budget debate.
There is another style of hypocrisy in the criti-
cism of,this Budget. I refer to the persistent allegation that this Government, which is committed to the concept of small government, which has dramatically reduced the level of rise in government spending, is somehow a secret big government. We have heard members of the Opposition
talking about the massive increase in the six Budgets that this Government introduced-an alleged massive increase in Budget revenues. I must admit that I am picking on the Sydney Morning Herald again, but even that newspaper, which I once held in very high esteem in terms of its capacity to editorialise intelligently, is taking a similar line-or perhaps in view of the intellect of members of the Opposition, they got. the. idea from the Sydney Morning Herald. For example, the Sydney Morning Herald in its editorial of Wednesday, 19 August, the day after the Budget was presented, argued that the Government had done very little to cut back the size of the public sector and said that the Government's own spending was going up. To dramatise that, to prove that

this Government is a big government,it then dealt with the question of the receipts of the Government.
This seems to me to underline the massive confusion that exists in the minds of the Opposition and some commentators. Because this Government has certainly increased its revenues over the last six Budgets by a considerable amount, it is accused of being a big Government. What these commentators failed to recognise-I am glad that the honourable member for Hunter lists himself among that group-is that the bulk of the Government's increased revenue has been required to make up the massive gap between expenditure and revenue that was opened up during the Whitlam years. It might sound as if one is harkening back to a long time ago, but the effort has been dramatic. To demonstrate this fact, I shall outline the statistics provided by the Statistics Group of the Commonwealth Parliamentary Library. I seek leave to have incorporated in Hansard various statistics supplied by that body.

Mr BAUME-I thank the House. The statistics I have just incorporated and those that I incorporated in the debate on the sales tax Bills this afternoon show a very significant trend. They show that under the three Whitlam Budgets there was a spending rise in real terms-that is, after taking account of inflation-of $4.7 billion, but an increase in revenue in that time of only $2.6 billion. In other words, there was a $2.1 billion gap between what was raised and what was spent. When the Fraser Government came into power it had to close that gap. That has involved the disastrous situation with which this Government has been lumbered-the cost of closing the gap that was opened up by the previous Government. Let us dramatise the figures. The total increase in spending in six Budgets of the present Government in real terms is $1.5 billion. That is one third of the increased spending of the previous Government in only three Budgets. It is just fascinating, and I am astounded that both the Sydney Morning Herald and the Opposition are incapable of grasping the simple fact' that spending under the Whitlam Government in three Budgets alone increased at a rate that was treble the total rise under six Budgets of the present Government.
Mr Coleman-Treble!
Mr BAUME-Treble! I thank the honourable member for his interjection. It is absolutely unbelievable that the present Opposition which was in government at that time should maintain this nonsense that the present Government is a dreadful, onerous and oppressive government when it has had to pick up that bill which was left forit. I was a massive bill. Let me show the House and the people of Australia how big that bill has been and what the impact has been on this Government. The three Whitlam Budgets increased by only $2.6 billion the total revenues of the Commonwealth, although its spending went up by $4.7

billion. As a result of that the present Government has. had to increase its revenue by $4.5 billion in real terms, whereas it increased its spending by only $l.5 billion. In other words, to close the enormous gap, to fill the dreadful hole into which this economy was quietly sinking, the present Government has had to increase its revenue by three times the amount that it has been able to increase its own spending. In other words, it has had all the criticism for increasing its revenue and none of the credit for increasing its spending from those people who, of course, benefit from the spending.
Let us look at the realities. The Whitlam Government received all the credit for handing out $4.7 billio~ when it raised only $2.6 billion. The Fraser Government has received all the criticism for having to close that gap, having to raise $4.5 billion, while it has increased its spending by only $1.5 billion over six Budgets. It seems astounding that credit is not given where it is certainly due for a magnificent and successful attempt by this Government to close this dreadful gap from which the Australian economy was bleeding to death. When we look at what this Budget has achieved we see that we have been able to have that tight sort of economic control at a time when we have been able, nonetheless, to increase the number of people in jobs by 330,000 in two years. As I said this afternoon, that is the biggest 2-year .increase in the number of people at work in Australia's history. We have been able to do this despite the fact that we have held our taxation rates at levels far below the average in most industrialised countries. I seek leave of the House to incorporate in Hansard tables showing tax revenues as a percentage of gross domestic product in Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development countries.

Mr BAUME-I thank the House.
Mr Robert Brown-Are they more cooked figures?
Mr BAUME-That is the sort of interjection I would expect from the Opposition, which cannot possibly face the reality of what the official statistics show. The only way the Opposition can counter the points I am making is to pretend that the statistics are phoney. They are official OECD &tatistics. The other statistics I have produced are from the Parliamentary Library. If members of the Opposition want to denigrate the Parliamentary Library and accuse it of dishonesty, it is fine by me. If the Opposition is serious in trying to counter what I am trying to say, it might be useful if it did something positive, rather than sneer at official statistics which were not provided by this Government. Let us look at the statistics. If the Opposition can deny the import of these statistics, let it try later. Let us look at the simple question of revenue from taxes as a proportion of gross domestic product for the OECD countries. One will see that Australia's revenue from taxes in 1978 is listed as totalling 28.8 per cent of gross domestic product. Let us compare that with countries such as Canada with 31 per cent, France with 39 per cent,Germany with almost 38 per cent and the Netherlands with 46 per cent.
Mr Cunningbam-What was it under Whitlam?
Mr BAUME-That is typical of the sort of interjection one would expect from the Opposition. The Whitlam Government spent money that it did not have. That it why we have this enormous tax bill and that is why-
Mr Young-That was like you when you were in the stock brokers.
Mr BAUME-I would expect that kind of interjection from the honourable member for Port Adelaide because that is the level of the objection of the Australian Labor Party to the serious points I am making about the economy. It seems to me that the only weapon that the Labor Party has is the weapon of insults, offensive interjections and personal attack: It does not have the capacity to criticise the realities of what the figures show. When the Labor Party can raise its standards, as it attempted unsuccessfully to do at the last election, and debate the issues of this Budget, instead of whingeing about little things, instead of appealing to the base elements, and instead of terrorising the people of Australia by claiming that this is an horrific Budget when it is not, and when the

Labor Party can raise its standards above personal abuse, we might get a decent debate in this House on the BlJdget.
Mr Coleman-They never will.
Mr BAUME-I agree with the honourable member for Wentworth, they never will. Certainly, they never have in the period I have been a member of this House. I was endeavouring to say that the Netherlands has a percentage of total tax to gross domestic product of 47. In Sweden-that socialist heaven-taxes are 53.5 per cent of gross domestic product, as against 28.8 per cent in Australia. Britain's figure is 34.5 per cent and that for the United States is 30.2 per cent. The OECD average is 35.8 per cent. For honourable members opposite to complain about the tax level in Australia is absurd. It seems to me that,.by international comparisons, we have a pretty sound tale to tell. We have a record which, I think, is reasonable.
Let us see whether the Ia.test increase in taxes on goods is something that is improper, indecent, obscene or anything else that the Opposition wants to pretend it is. According to the OECD survey, Australia was one of the lowest countries in the world in terms of the gross domestic product accounted for by taxes on goods and services. The figure was about 9 per cent, compared to 12 per cent for France, 12 per cent for the Netherlands, 12.7 per cent for Sweden and the OECD average of lOt per cent. One can see that so far we have lagged behind the rest of the world in the indirect taxes area. It seems to me that the switch that is now taking place in a small way has a great deal of merit in terms of international comparison and in terms of equity. An interesting point which members of the Opposition do. not seem to recognise is that the average wage earner in Australia spends something like 40 per cent-

Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER-The honourable member knows that that is not a point of order.
Mr BAUME-The Opposition is seeking to waste my time because the points I am making effectively demolish the rubbish that has been maintained in this debate by the Opposition. I turn to the switch to a small degree of indirect taxation revenue, having established quite clearly that indirect taxes as a proportion of total gross domestic product in Australia have been far below the rest of the world. I have said that in terms of equity there is a fair case for maintaining the switch in the way it has been done in Australia. The average wage earner, the average householder, spends something like 40 per cent of his income on items which are subject to these new taxes. In other words, 40 per cent of his income will attract this 2+ per cent increase. But for the lower income earners, something like only 25 per cent of their salary will be subject to taxation of this kind because, as I said this afternoon, they have a higher propensity to consume food.
Mr Mildren-What about the pensioners and the unemployed?
Mr BAUME-The pensioners have a fully indexed pension which takes fully into account the rises in indirect taxes, which basically affect the rich far more than the poor. However, because this Government introduced full indexation of their income, pensioners will receive, in fact, a full indexation benefit although the bulk of their spending, something like 75 per cent of it, will not be directed towards items on which indirect taxes will be increased in this Budget. So let us nail this question of equity. The fact is that pensioners, who will not have to pay any indirect tax on their food, will receive the benefit of full indexation on all the items on which they do not spend anything more than approximately 25 per cent of their incomes.
Mr Barry Jones-You said that this afternoon. That is exactly what you said this afternoon.
Mr BAUME-Exactly. I said it this afternoon, and I am endeavouring to reinforce it. Let us look and see where the Government is spending this money. Where have the big increases been? The pensioners are the people who have received the biggest increase in-government spending since this Government has been in office. The biggest rise in our spending has been in the area of social security-in real terms an increase of $1.3 billion or 30 per cent since this Government has been in office. rise, and a rise which I am certain has been appreciated by the members of the defence forces in my electorate, for example. It seems to me that the only inhibiting factors in maintaining this Budget, in selling this Budget to the people and in getting rid of the deliberate confusion being created by people opposite that there will be a disaster in this Budget, are some little items which are very important to the people involved but which are not significant in terms of total revenues. Mr LIONEL BOWEN (Kingsford-Smith) (8.45)-This is an atrocious Budget, and the honourable member for Macarthur (Mr Baume) knows it. The only thing we can say about his speech is that we will give him marks for loyalty but nothing for matter. Let us put it very clearly: If there were an election tomorrow he would be annihilated in the seat of Macarthur, because the Government has done nothing but redistribute the resources of the people it represents to organisations usually belonging to foreigners. It is significant that a fair segment of the honourable member's speech related to countries other than Australia. Let us look at the facts where this Government is concerned. At page 87 of the Budget Papers the Government clearly admits that at least 2.3 million people are in poverty, because it refers to this estimated number of holders of health cards. The Government admits that itself. This is the sixth Fraser Budget. We will say it is the sixth poverty Budget. It is a Budget that has budgeted for a $1,500m surplus-a surplus which will be extracted from the people's pockets and which will not be given back to them. I will table figures later which clearly will show that in the six Budgets the Government has done nothing but redistribute money from the poor to those who do not need it. That applies right across the board, whether one is talking about social services, health, education, housing or whatever. Every State Premier is against the Government. We in the Opposition have been inundated with telexes from the Government's own 'supporters~ the employers-wondering what in the name of fortune the Government will do about their industry, which the Government has referred back to the Industries Assistance Commission to consider the question of protection. We have been inundated with telexes from those who are interested in the newspaper or publishing industry, saying that this is the only Western democracy to tax information. Where is the Government's support? Is it not about time we tested the Government by asking: 'Where is your mandate?'
Let us look at this matter in detail. The poverty line, as far as the Government is concerned for a family with two children is $200. At least three million Australians fall below that line. So what sort of an economy has-the Government created in its six Budgets? Why make excuses all the time, saying that there is a reason for your doing this. Let us look at the Government's history ove-r the

period since 1975, when all those promises, whether they related to health schemes, indexation, pensions or whatever, were never kept. These people we are talking about are disadvantaged. The Henderson Commission of Inquiry into Poverty clearly showed that for an Australian family of four to survive it would need $227 a week, and most Australians agree with that. When the McMahon Government commissioned the first inquiry into poverty in Australia, 1.3 million Australians were living below the poverty line. There are now 3 million Australians living below that poverty line-an increase of 1.7 million. In regard to the Government's cover up, Professor Henderson said:
This Government is guilty of trying to suppress the evidence. Obviously it doesn't want the real facts to come out.

If one is a child of a poor family one is not as healthy and does not get the same care. It is more likely that one will be hospitalised and suffer from infectious diseases, malnutrition or whatever. The opportunity of having an upper occupation in life is denied one, Statistics clearly show that a poor child has oneseventh of a chance of attending university, There is plenty of evidence of thilt. If honourable members look at the report of the Karmel Interim Committee for the Australian Schools Commission, which we commissioned when we were in government they will see that it clearly showed that two-thirds of positions in universities were given to the'top elite because of the occupational back-up of the parents. All these disadvantaged persons' problems are permanent and are increasing in Australia, whether they be in regard to health, education or housing, or the problems of just being poor. These facts can be verified by any medical authority, any university or any commission of inquiry. What is it that the Government can saY is fair and reasonable? In our view, it has no right to penalise the poor parents but in the mind of any Australian it is not justified in penalising the children of the poor, which is what this Government is doing.

Mr LIONEL BOWEN-I thank the House. In six Budgets over six years this Government has extracted over $162 billion in taxes and $90 billion in income tax-most of it from those who pay as they earn, as has been said before by my colleagues. In 1975-76 PAYE tax was 41.7 per cent of total tax collections. This year it has climbed to 45 per cent. What is the converse situation? Company tax was 15 per cent in 1975-76. It has now fallen to 13.7 per cent and profits are rising much faster than wages. This year alone PAYE tax is up 22 per cent while wages have risen only 13t per cent and company tax has risen only 10.1 per cent. Last year profits rose by 18.2 percent.
In six Budgets this Government has redistributed more income than any Government in Australian history. The direction of this redistribution has been towards increasing inequality, not just for the poor but for most Australians whether they be in the lower or middle income groups. It should come as no surprise to anyone that this Government holds the record for the greatest budgetary contribution to increasing inequality of any government since the Bruce-Page Government. Yet the Government has not rested on that record. It will now force a similar policy on all State governments. The much vaunted new federalism was supposed to give greater autonomy to the States but it is has done nothing of the sort. New federalism has involved a combination of untruths about what funds are available to the States and then the cutting of those funds.
At the same time the Government is cutting not only its social wage contributions but also the social wage in respect of State Governments. Let me give an example. On 4 May at the Premiers Conference the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) announced the cancellation of the existing tax sharing formula under which the States got 39.87 per cent of income tax collections. Instead the Government did away with the formula and the Statt"~ got a 9 per cent increase in the tax sharing funds. They should have got a 15 per cent increase to match the provisions of the previous formula. What does this mean, for example, to New South Wales? In that instance it means that

New South Wales gets $143m less from taxes, the taxes which its own people pay. We should bear in mind also the increased sales taxes which will be imposed and which will net over $800m. An amount of $300m will be extracted from the people of New South Wales in sales tax. Is it any wonder that the Government parties will be annihilated in the forthcoming State election? The Commonwealth Government has robbed all State Governments but it has been severely savage oil New South Wales.
Let us have a look at some other factors as they apply to New South Wales. The Government in New South Wales was encouraged as a result of a letter from the Prime Minister to accelerate electricitydevelopment. So the New South Wales Government applied to get $171m from the Loan Council. That was refused. Again we see a savage attack on New South Wales after it was asked to do something about development in relation to electricity. I understand that the Leader of the Opposition in New South Wales was down here urging the Fraser Oovernment to carry out this sort of policy. I understand that he wants the Prime Minister to campaign for him in the forthcoming election campaign. I suggest that they both remain on sick leave; it is the only chance they have of even getting 10 per cent of the votes. Would anyone in New South Wales want to vote for a Government that does this to the people in that State?
We should look at some other matters in respect of the Federal Government. In the last six Budgets the Federal Government has cut real net payments to New South Wales for housing from $177m to $13,5m. Why has it done that? It is only shifting the responsibility and the burden on to State governments. Let us look at health. I ask the Minister for Health who is at the table whether it is a fact that he has done away with the health agreement. The Budget says so. The Government has a new commitment but the commitment is nowhere near what it was before. I have advice from the New South Wales Government setting out details in the health area. The cuts amount to $1 OOm because the Government will not :share the cost burden any more. The Government has said also

Appropriation Bill (No.1) 1981-82

27 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 931

From the little we heard, the Opposition's policies don't add up to much.
Since Tuesday night we have heard a succession of Australian Labor Party speakers pour forth so much doom and despair that I wonder if the chief architect of the Australian Labor Party's current arguments is not Samuel Beckett himself. I make no apology for linking the Opposition's arguments with the theatre of the absurd. The two fit together well. The reason is simple. The Australian Labor Party's despair about the economy is so far removed from reality that it is laughable.
Quite. the contrary to what the Australian Labor Party would have us believe, the economy is looking good. Fact one is that inflation is down to 8.8 per cent. Fact two is that unemployment is at its lowest level for four years. Fact three is that private investment is up by 20 per cent. This Budget is aimed at improving this pattern of economic success achieved by the Government. It is a Budget that points to continued economic growth in the forthcoming year.
The reduction of the Budget deficit by almost $1,OOOm, to $146m, should ease the pressures on interest rates and make more funds available to home buyers and others in the community without, at the same time, fuelling inflation. The sensible approach of this Budget will produce lasting success in the economy. On this side of the House we are intelligent enough to realise that one cannot legislate improvements in the economy. One cannot legislate against unemployment or inflation, or legislate for economic growth. But one can create the right conditions in which such improvements can take place. One can create a stable economy for development to take place.
In my electorate, the Northern Territory, the prospect of future development is particularly welcome. Since self-government in 1978the Budget of the Northern Territory Government has grown from $350m to $756m this year. This reflects not only the transfer of State-like functions from the Commonwealth to ihe. Northern Territory, but also the rapid economic growth in the Territory which has taken place under the Everingham Country-Liberal Party Government. This Federal Budget contains a continuing high level of commitment to the Northern Territory. Members will be aware that the basic framework for financial arrangements between the Commonwealth and the Northern Territory is set out in a memorandum of understanding agreed to between the two governments in 1978. Under the terms of that memorandum, the Commonwealth will provide the Northern Territory with $618.9m this year. This is a generous contribution

representing $4,765 per capita in the Northern Territory for State-type services.
It is worthwhile pointing out that over the period 1978 to 1981 the revenue raised by the Northern Territory Government has increased from $60m per annum to $130m per annum. This doubling of the local revenue base has occurred without imposing burdens on local taxpayers except in this year's Northern Territory Budget, which levied a tobacco tax and made other minor adjustments. In recent times and since the memorandum of understanding was negotiated, the Northern Territory Government has had extra functions transferred to it such as regulating uranium mining for which additional funding is necessary and may require close attention to detailed settlement of adequate funding.
Significantly, additional Commonwealth expenditure on its own department such as the Department of Aboriginal Affairs, the Department of Social Security, the Department of Defence, the Department of Transport and Telecom Australia and on capital works projects also provide an important boost to the economy. Furthermore, these specific Budget items will enhance the development of the Northern Territory and alleviate some of the disabilities of living in an isolated area. For all the Labor Party's screaming one would think-that the Government is doing nothing about helping Australia's needy through social security. This Federal Budget will increase social welfare spending by 14.7per cent or $1,265m.
Mr Birney-By how much?
Mr TAMBLING-By $1,265m. This means that for every dollar of tax collected 47c goes towards social security payments. In the Northern Territory, some 5,948 families will take advantage of the new provisions for family allowances. Throughout the year I have received a number of complaints about unemployment benefit being paid to the spouse of a person on strike. I am pleased the Government is taking action to halt this situation. I am concerned, though, about the problems of those receiving income security payments in the Northern Territory. Additional assistance in the form of zone allowance does exist for ordinary taxpayers. However, people receiving unemployment benefit; pensions and other social security payments receive no such entitlements which recognise the factors of isolation. I ask that the Government consider some extra form of relief for those on social security payments to counterbalance this discrimination. It is a sad fact of the Northern Territory lifestyle that often aged people are forced to leave for

932 REPRESENTATIVES 27 August 1981

Appropriation Bill (No.1) 1981-82

southern States for no other reason than the expense of living in the Northern Territory.
I mentioned before that this is a Budget of development with major implications in the Northern Territory. As honourable members are aware, the most exciting new development in the Northern Territory is the emergence and maturity of the uranium industry. It is pleasing to note that $16m has been allocated for the Alligator Rivers region. With the Australian Labor Party opposed to uranium mining on the one hand and continually apologising to the Northern Territory electorate on the other for its anti-uranium stance, it could not hope to match the high priority which the Fraser Government gives the uranium industry. Of the $16m set aside for the uranium province, $9.9mhas been made as a contribution towards labiru township, specifically for a hospital, police station and housing. In addition, the Office of the Supervising Scientist has been given $4.2m to monitor uranium mining operations. The National Parks and Wildlife Service has $2.4m to run Kakadu National Park. Coupled with the 2S per cent increase in the Northern Territory Government's Budget allocation for the Territory Parks and Wildlife Commission which has a budget of $lS.9m, I hope that the Commission will assume a greater role in the administration and management of Kakadu National Park and, in fact, take over functions from the Australian National Parks and Wildlife Service.
Tourism in the Territory is rapidly expanding. It is the second largest industry after mining. The scope for further investment in tourism is unlimited. Finance for Uluru National Park and the provision of $13.4m for national roads, some of which will go towards the first tourist developmental road at Ayers Rock, will provide an important impetus for this industry. Similarly, the massive increase in funding for employment programs comes at an opportune moment in Northern Territory development. Because of a small population base and its isolation, the Northern Territory has had considerable difficulty in attracting skilled workers to the major. resource projects. The 31 per cent rise in the allocation for national manpower training programs should go part of the way to providing skilled labour for development. I point out however that the success of these resource projects and, inevitably, the development of the Territory depends on a stable community being created with the development of a stable work force. It is no secret that an effective way of doing this without imposing undue costs on the private sector and eventually on the consumer is by providing some form of tax concession. At the moment this is done by offering a

zone allowance tax rebate. With the view to providing consistent and stable development, the level of the zone allowance should. be substantial throughout the Territory, including the service port of Darwin.

As we have all experienced, growth is fraught with pains. In the case of the Northern Territory this is housing. The Northern Territory Government in its own Budget this year has been innovative in trying to encourage home ownership and
housing development but it is neither possible nor proper for government to meet all housing needs. I challenge the traditional sources of housing finance, banks and building societies to make more of their funds available for home finance in the Northern Territory. The security and the need are both obvious.

Mr TAMBLING-It is a matter of getting money, not interest rates. The Labor Party policy would certainly mean that the money would dry up. I turn briefly to communications. With the generous Budget allocation for the Australian Broadcasting Commission, Territorians are
expecting an improvement in services. The Australian Broadcasting Commission has provided an excellent service to isolated areas over the years which commercial enterprises have not found viable. However, I indicate to this House the concern of many people in remote parts of Australia, most of whom cannot listen to this debate tonight, who are worried that the additional services will be diverted to where they are least needed, that is, to the metropolitan areas at the expense of rural areas. The report of the Dix Committee into the' ABC placed high priority on the provision of a second network for ABC radio in regional and outback areas. It argued that 'it offers the best means of redressing the imbalance of ABC radio
services between metropolitan and outback areas'.

One of the most pleasing aspects of this Budget is generous funding for Aboriginals and Aboriginal organisations. The Department of Aboriginal Affairs has received substantial increases as has the Aboriginal Development Commission. Funds have been made available for Aboriginal employment, housing and health schemes. Not only is the Government trying to alleviate the problems which are affecting Aboriginals but also, through such organisations as the Aboriginal Development Commission, it is promoting Aboriginal advancement, self-reliance and cultural and commercial freedom. per cent of the funds at the disposal of the Department of Aboriginal Affairs for advancement programs will go to Aboriginal managed organisations. In the Northern Territory the Department is allocating no fewer than 42 grants to organisations for Aboriginal improvement.

Mr YOUNG (Port Adelaide) (9.17)-The wife of the honourable member for the Northern Territory (Mr Tambling) must be .very loyal. Now that the Australian Democrats have made up their minds-it has caused them great consternation-this will be the Government's third last Budget. If everybody on this side of the House shared my view it might be the Government's last Budget. I believe that the Australian Labor Party should play politics in Australia in the same rotten fashion in which they are played by the Government. The Senate has the power, as the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) told us last Tuesday week, to block the Budget. This Budget being as reprehensible as it is, to use the Prime Minister's words of 1975, the Senate should use its powers to block it. The people should be asked to vote on whether they want a Budget of this nature.
Earlier in the debate someone said that it is not relevant to talk about whether matters related to the Budget were raised in the Prime Minister's policy speech of 1980. He said that this is the 1981 Budget and We should forget all about the 1980 policy speech. This is the third time that the Prime Minister has pulled the stunt of saying one thing at the election and doing another straight after. I believe that the people of Australia should adopt the term used by the Returned Services League 'Lest we forget'. If the Democrats are now going to water on their earlier statement that they would knock back the sales tax measures this Government can live at ease for another three years. All its measures will be passed because of the weak kneed messages that will be sent back from the Senate as a result of the sta,nd of the Democrats. The Government can go on shaping its Budgets as it haS done this time and force its policies on the Australian people.
What the Government will not do is present a Budget of this nature in 1983. This Budget is designed to allow the Government to give back to the voters in 1983 some of the taxes it is grabbing

in 1981 and 1982. Nothing could be clearer than that. I-do not believe the Government is doing anything at all to try to hide the motivation of this Budget. It will take money from people in 1981 and 1982, give a little back in 1983 in order to survive and take it again in 1984. That is the way this Government has operated since it was elected in 1975 and obviously it is the way it will continue after this year.
Under this Government the problems that have been caused and the injustices that have been created are as much acts of deliberate policy as they are the result of incompetence. It has hidden behind the excuse that its policies and the burdens that have gone with them are economic necessities. The Prime Minister's assumption that what he wanted was what the rest of Australia wanted evaporated with the reaction to his comment that 'life was not meant to be easy'. Since then he has adopted the false rhetoric of fighting inflation first and promising that economic recovery is on its way. We have been told of the prospect of a consumer-led recovery, an investment-led recovery and an overseas-led recovery. All of these have amounted to nothing and now we are supposed to sit and waitfor a resources-led recovery. If the Government is allowed to continue with its present foreign investment and development policies, most people will not enjoy many of the benefits of this either.
Now the long lead times of resources projects are being used by the Government as a further excuse for not coming to grips with unemployment. The Government has dumped the unemployed. It has not just walked away from them; it has hounded and stigmatised them in a sickening media campaign to neutralise unemployment as an election issue. As time has worn on, the ranking of Government Ministers for employment has _ slid about as far as it can go, as has the Govern~ ment's financial commitment to the unemployed. In this Budget the Community Youth Support Scheme has been abolished. This action has ignored the fact that the average period ofunemployment for 15 to 19 year-year-olds in this country which is going through a so-called boom is 26 weeks. .
The Government's policies are often contradictory. It has said that fighting inflation is its first priority but it has fuelled inflation with its oil pricing policy and its sales tax increases. We have heard about the benefits of small government,.but taxes have not been reduced. The burdens must be spread across a smaller tax base.. Under the Prime Minister that .burden is increasingly put on the wage earner. The estimate of average weekly earnings, to which the Government prefers to

934 REPRESENTATIVES 27 August 1981

Appropriation Bill (No.1) 1981-82

refer, is the seasonally adjusted estimate of average earnings before tax of all male civilian employees in Australia, including the highest paid as well as the lowest paid. For the March quarter of 1981 that estimate is $283.90 a week or about $14,700 a year. A similarly calculated estimate of the average weekly earnings of adult male full time employees, excluding managerial and executive staff, is $245.50 a week or about $12,700 a year. According to the. latest Budget figures, 75 per cent of individuals have annual incomes of under $15,000. But about half earn less than $11,000 a year and almost 40 per cent of wage earners receive less thlln $10,000 a year. The biggest group earns between $9,000 and $10,000 a year. These are the people the Prime Minister is putting the screws on. The 0.26 per cent of the individuals who earn more than $50,000 a year are the ones he is looking after.
It is sickening to the people of Australia to witness Government members like the Treasurer (Mr Howard) of this country being interviewed on television telling the people of Australia that he can understand the problems of the average person who earns $300 a week. Let me. repeat: Seventy-five per cent of wage earners receive less than $1 5,000 a year or $300 a week. About half of the wage earners receive less than $11,000 a year and 40 per cent receive less than $10,000 a year. The Treasurer does not know what people earn. How can he understand the hardships that are being forced on the people through measures like this Budget?
The Government's double standards for the community are nowhere more evident than they are in the field of taxation. Despite the Prime Minister's mention of the benefits of tax indexation in his policy speech last year, only seven months later the Government announced that it had made its last indexation adjustment and that any future adjustments would be made on a discretionary basis having regard to economic conditions. This means that from now on the Government can rely on inflation to make its tax increases. The real value of. deductions for dependants will decline and increases in wages will .mean higher average tax rates. These burdens will fall disproportionately heavily upon those on lower incomes. For example, if we use the Government's prediction of a 13.5 per cent increase in the average wage across the board, a person currently earning $8,000 a year would have an increase in his tax bill this year of 57 per cent. If a person on $8,000 a year receives a 13 per cent wage increase, as predicted by the Treasurer in his Budget, his tax bill in the next financial year will

go up by 57 per cent. The tax bill of a person earning $14,500 a year will go up by 21 t per cent, and a person currently earning $50,000 will have an increase in his tax bill of 17.8 per cent. That is the way in which the Government intends to raise the additional revenue it requires this financial year.
In recent years company taxation revenue increases have barely kept pace with inflation, while increases in revenue from personal income tax have far exceeded inflation. The Government's failure to redress the growing imbalance of the burden between corporate taxes and personal income taxes is nowhere more evident than in the area of resources. Honourable members on this side of the House have frequently argued for a resource rental tax. We are not talking about punitive taxes to discourage investment. We are talking about ensuring that an adequate share of the benefits derived from the nation's mineral wealth is distributed amongst the people. The day that Australia considered itself a nation poorly endowed with mineral resources ended in the early 1960s with the lifting of the iron ore export embargo.
To go on talking for another 20 years about the need to ensure that companies should be able to extract every ounce of profit from every tonne of ore because of the risks of exploration is to divorce oneself from sOme modern realities. There are enough examples of mining companies having been given leases over ore bodies which they took no part at all in finding. In many cases the exploration was carried out by the Government-owned Bureau of Mineral Resources, Geology and Geophysics. In other cases there has been a substantial body of geological knowledge about an area and the ore body has already been well defined. Examples can be found at Weipa and on the New South Wales coal fields. Should the companies given the right to exploit such resources also be given the right to extract super-normal profits? In our opinion they should not. I think most people in the community are sceptical that capital intensive resource-based industries will provide the number of jobs we require directly or indirectly to alleviate the present social and economic situation. If the Government maintains its present position we will continue to see a group of foreign companies extracting super-normIII profits from our resources while the Government continues to tax the wage earner beyond his capacity to pay.
Tax is not the only issue in relation to the mining industry coming out of this year's Budget. Last year the Government set a target range for the growth in the money supply of between 9 per cent and II per cent. The flow of foreign capital into this country was so great that towards the end

Appropriation Bill (No.1) 1981-82

27 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 935

of the financial year it was realised that the growth in the money supply would reach almost 13 percent. The Government for the last six years has believed that the remedy for inflation is a harsh monetary policy~in plain English, to impose credit squeezes. The more' foreign investment, the greaterthe growth in the money supply, the more pressure the Government has brought to bear on interest rates. The fact that the Government's policies are brutal, wasteful, inefficient and wrong does not alter the fact~the Government's approach to monetary policy and foreign investment has meant that Australians have had .to bear all the burdens of the credit squeeze. This has affected households, small businesses, rural industry and manufacturing. Foreign investment has, therefore, received preferential treatment at the expense of the Australian community.
We have seen another example of the Government's attitude toward foreign investment in the last month with its decision to ask Shell to offer 25 per cent of its local interest to Australian investors. The Government has not made clear what it has in mind for Shell. Adequate Australian equity is 51 per cent so that there can be effective local control, not silent partner status, as well as a better participation in earnings. Without the Government's issuing a timetable by which this should be achieved, it appears that the request to Shell is inadequate and probably motivated by a desire to solve what the Government might see as a serious public relations problem, Alternatively it could be a shameless exploitation of nationalising status which will allow Shell to take out 100 per cent equity in local projects without our knowing when it is ever going to achieve 50 per cent local equity.
I turn to the Treasurer's $892m bonanza. The Treasurer's promise Of 12 March of this year in announcing the Government's decision not to proceed with the introduction of anew broadly based tax has now been broken. He sa.idthat the Government was anxious to minimise theinflationilfY consequences of collecting additional revenue and that it did not want to move towards greater reliance on indirect tax without providing a noticeable offset in personal tax reductions. The Prime Minister also must have forgotten'to mention these new sales taxes to the Australian electors before the last election. This means that in a full year $892m in additional sales tax. will come out of the pockets of Australians on virtually every item that they buy. The Government admits that this will add 0.75 percent to inflation. This should be contrasted with the $12m in. a full year which will be taken from our coal exports and which will add nothing to inflation.

This sales tax increase, combined with the Government's utilisation of fiscal drag to maximise its take of personal income tax, probably makes this year's Budget the most regressive in living memory in terms of the distribution of income. It is obvious that people on or below the average wage will spend a greater proportion of their income on items that attract sales tax. The blow is even greater for homemakers. Sales tax on furniture, floor coverings and whitegoods has doubled, going from 2.5 per cent to 5 per cent. They are all major expense items and they are necessities in setting up a home. This is another unnecessary burden which this Government has heaped on top of rising interest rates.
The Prime Minister and the Treasurer think it is politically more palatable to justify the recent interest rate increases by referring to the inflationary potential of wage increases rather than admit that it is a result of their own reaction to the blowout in the money supply caused by the high inflow of foreign capital which they have sought. The continuing issue is whether the latest round of interest rate increases will prove to be high enough. If the finance market can only see higher rates ahead, there will be pressure on the Government to raise interest rates still further so that it can maintain its present monetary policies. The impact of high interest rates on manufacturing is serious enough. It has pushed up the costs of overdrafts and new capital and has worsened the cost sfructure of companies as they require refinancing. This will obviously flow through to the. consumer price index and affect the competitiveness of local industry. But its impact on home owners is devastating.
The people worst affected are those who purchased their homes three to five years ago when interest rates were substantially lower than they are now. Steeply rising housing costs had forced horne buyers to the limit of their borrowing capacity, usually on second but sometimes third mortgages at punitive interest rates. They consideredthemselves lucky if they could even bridge the deposit gap. First it was unemployment that oaused people to lose their homes; now people with jobs just cannot keep up with the rising interest rates. If honourable members take a drive around any.relatively new suburb they will find mortgage sale signs on well kept properties that are three to five years old. These increasing interest burdens have coincided with a more expensive ,phase of life when these people have started families. This is a time when their reasonable expectation would have been that the proportion of their income devoted to mortgage repayments would have started to decline. It is not

936 REPRESENTATIVES 27 August 1981

Appropriation Bill (No. 1) 1981-82

the 1960s; these people cannot find a second job or work overtime to make up the difference.
In the last couple, of years interest rates have increased by 2 percentage points. This is the exact opposite of the Prime Minister's promise in 1977
that interest rates would drop by 2 percentage points. The conclusion that the Government's policies give priority to foreign investment over home ownership is inescapable. In the present cirsumstances, with skyrocketing housing and rental costs in our largest capital cities, there is a backlog of persons who might have been expected to purchase homes over the last few years. They have suffered a diminished standard of living because of the present economic climate and are forced to continue to seek rental accommodation. In this environment we find that lengthy waiting lists exist for public rental housing and low income home ownership loans. Waiting periods are generally expressed as a number of years. Worse than this, there has been a dramatic increase in homelessness, including young p~ople, much of which involves a need for permanent low price accommodation.
In response, the Government has chosen to cut allocations for welfare housing by more than $20m. Welfare housing advances to the States and Territories have been cut from $285m to $262m. However, this includes $12m for Aboriginal housing, which was previously administered by the Department of Aboriginal Affairs. This means that the allocation has been cut to $250m. This represents a decrease of more than 20 per cent in real terms. Instead, a $2m allocation for the start of a program of crisis accommodation for families in distress has been announced. It is obvious that it is the Government that is creating the crisis in housing.
To complete the picture of the Prime Minister's policy of creeping misery, I will make a few observations about the Government's record on social security. While pensions and adult unemployment benefits have been indexed, a vast proportion of people seeking emergency' relief from various agencies are recipients of social security payments. This is because their level of payment is inadequate to meet even basic needs. Many families are having to use emergency relief as an ongoing way of life to supplement pensions and benefits. It should be noted that pensions are diminishing in relation to community income movements. Last year the pension was 23 per cent of average weekly earnings. In 1981-82, after this Budget, it will fall to 22 per cent. The fact that the unemployment benefit is $11.60 a week less than the pension must be seen in the light that 78,000 people in this country have been unemployed for

more than a year and that an additional 55,000 people have been unemployed for between six months and a year.
Unemployment benefit for 18-year-olds again has not been indexed. Many young people who left home in the country to come to the cities to look for work are now living well below the poverty line. There can be no doubt that this Budget would have been rejected by the people if the Prime Minister had gone ahead with his threat to take it to the people. I wish that the Australian Democrats had stuck with us on this issue. They promised, according to Senator Chipp, that they would 'keep the bastards honest'. The bastards in this place happen to be the Government and the Democrats should have voted with the Australian Labor Party so that we would have an election in 1981 when the Prime Minister was doing the things that he intends to do all the time, not making the false problems that he makes at election time.

Mr TUCKEY (O'Connor) (9.37)-Budgets, as the Opposition has been at pains to tell us, are about people. They are about the welfare of people, and the welfare of people is strongly influenced by Budget decisions. The choice that exists for a Government as to how it influences that welfare is,to me, the dilemma of today's politics. This Budget and the speeches about it have demonstrated the vast divergencerin viewpoint between the Government, the deliverer of the Budget, and the Opposition with its alternative strategy. Both claim to have the welfare of the population at heart. The Budget before us, at least to a degree, accepts as a major strategy that Australians are resourceful, hard-working and willing to make their own way in the world. It creates conditions for people to succeed by their personal enterprise. It seeks in many directions to remove government from the lives of Australians and to leave them to their own decisions.
The Opposition's alternative, as presented by its leader and re-rendered by its supporters with practically no variation, offers us Government at every turn. When one summarises the Opposition's package it might appear that this is not so. It has pitched its bid particularly high. to an electorate blinded by handouts. How would the Opposition do this? I will refer to what it has said. It would abandon the proposed sales tax so that the money could go to consumer spending.

The message of this Budget is that workers and manufacturers should get together so that they can make more money, sell goods more cheaply and give other people more money to spend. That is true consumer spending-a dollar that goes further.
We will not legislate our way out of industrial strife. make its taxes obvious and answer for them. But at least we call it a tax. We call it sales tax. The Leader of the Opposition called for a mild deficit. Of course, when the Labor Party was last the government of Australia it operated almost eternally in deficit-in fact, in a very large deficit. What name can we give a deficit other than a tax by stealth? We can call it an expenditure and a commitment for future generations. The deficits of the Whitlam years have measured heavily on the tax burden of present day Australians. If I can expand on that statement: We need only a simple analogy of someone getting a $20,000 personal loan with no repayments for 12 months. That person will live very well for that 12 months and then the trouble will start. Anybody who is fortunate enough, as a government is, to borrow money at one per cent and to tell somebody else how much he will borrow, can very easily become the irresponsible individual in the community and spend beyond the community's ability.
Much has been said of the plight of the home owner and his mortgage interest rates. The institution of home ownership within Australia is, I think, one of our great pillars. I hope that all steps will be taken to maintain it. Nevertheless, to hear some of the more colorful members of the Opposition bemoan the home owner's fate raises some humour with me when I consider the basic attitude of the Opposition to the individual owning anything. I wonder why it is suddenly so sympathetic to home owners. This Budget not only removes the Federal Government from the domestic loan market but also creates a situation whereby, as the predicted surplus materialises, the Government can, if it chooses, go back into the market and buy back its securities, thus introducing the funds it believes the economy can handle and further reducing interest rate pressure.
My previous political experience was in local government. I know well the burden that the present day wealth tax called rates-household rates-imposes on home owners. I imagine that Australian home owners today pay in excess of $1 billion in rates and charges of that nature on their land. The funds allocated in this Budget for a direct expenditure on local government are a direct subsidy to ratepayers. Local government has only the rate as a flexible source of income. In my experience Federal Government grants represented from 20 per cent to 300 per cent of the rate revenue of various local authorities in Western Australia. This grant could otherwise be recouped only by increased rates. I .congratulate' the Government for continuing its policy of supporting local government by allowing it a direct proportion of income tax.

Mr Campbell-Labor started thilt.
Mr TUCKEY-Do not tell me who started it. The Labor Government started it with a lousy $40m.
Mr Campbell-That was Labor policy.
Mr TUCKEY-The Labor Government's policy was all right, provided that one went cap in hand to a mob of people in Canberra and begged for one's money. There were no untied grants from the opposite lot. They lose control. The result is double edged. The money goes to grass roots government, to the people who know best where to spend it and who ensure that the funds go as far as possible. The grant guarantees delivery of grass roots services which could otherwise be provided only by land owners.
Finally, let me refer to overseas investment. The Opposition still lives with the belief that Australia is an economic island. It chooses to blame capital inflow for all our economic ills. It ignores entirely the prosperity that such investment can bring. It proposes controls, similar no doubt to those it proposed--
Mr Campbell-Reagan has put capital inflows on.
Mr TUCKEY-Do not tell me about Reagan. We are in Australia. The Labor Party would impose controls similar, no doubt, to those it imposed previously-controls which, if associated with the other economic measures it espouses, would guarantee the same result as the last one when there was not a slowing down of capital inflow, but a reversal of capital inflow. To use the words of the honourable member for Wills when he wore another hat: 'Capital will go on strike'. No one knows better than he the effect of a strike. Capital needs labour but labour cannot survive without capital. Labour cannot survive on money created by government because only money created by resources and hard work has any value.
Debate (on motion by Mr Duffy) adjourned.

If vehicle manufacturing ceases, it is argued that the unemployed would find work in the mining and service industries.
