That is sheer nonsense. No economic analyst and no observer of the industry is saying that in Australia today. This advertisement is totally and utterly misleading and it is downright' fraudulent. It attempts to reduce a sensible debate to' a nonsense. Mr Deputy Speaker, I wish to protest to the House in the strongest possible terms again&t debasing an important national argument by this sort of absolute bilge.
Mr ROBERT BROWN (Hunter) (10.8) -Two claims made by the coalition parties prior to the 1975,dection and prior to elections subsequently have been that they would form a

government which was committed to small government and that they would form a government which was committed to low tax. The claim has been repeated and apparently the assumption is that if the claim is repeated often enough a sufficiently large proportion of the Australian people will come to accept it.
I will deal very briefly with the two claims mentioned. In relation to small government, probably the most appropriate measure to determine the extent to which any government becomes involved in the economic affairs of the nation is the proportion of the total gross domestic product which is absorbed by total Budget receipts. During the last Budget of the Labor Governmentthat was the Budget for the year 1975-76-total Budget receipts absorbed 25.1 per cent of the gross domestic product. In this current Budget this Government has arranged for the absorption of 27.5 per cent of the gross domestic product as total Budget receipts, the highest percentage ever recorded if the history of this Commonwealtho
If the proportion of gross domestic product going to Budget receipts in 1981-82 had remained equal to the proportion that applied in 1975-76, for this current financial year the private sector in Australia would have been left with an additional $3,556m. As a result of this Budget that will be taken from them. I repeat: The best single measure of the extent to which the Government participates in the economic affairs of the nation is the proportion of the total gross domestic product which is absorbed by Budget receipts, and those figures indicate not only that it has grown since the Government came to office but also that the percentage is the highest it has ever been.
The other claim that has been made is that this is a low tax government. Surely the figures which would justify or refute a claim of this kind would be the proportion of the total gross domestic product which is absorbed by total taxation revenue. Despite the claim that has been made that the Labor Government was a high tax government, the fact remains that in 1975-76, the year of the last Budget for which we were responsible, the proportion of total tax revenue which was absorbed by the Federal Government was 23.2 per cent. For the current financial year the percentage has increased to 25.5 per cent. The Treasurer (Mr Howard) attempted earlier today to manipulate these figures by referring to the Budget deficit in 1975-76 and suggesting that the proportion of total gross domestic product in that year was greater than it was. I remind honourable members of the fact that in 1975-76, although 23.2 per cent of the gross domestic product was absorbed by total tax revenue, that included a national health

942 REPRESENTATIVES 27 August 1981

Adjournment

scheme which meant that every family in Australia, in terms of today's values, was better off to the extent of about $12 a week. If the same proportion of gross domestic product going to taxes in 1975-76 had remained applicable to 1981-82, for the current financial year income earners throughout the whole of Australia would have been left with an additional $3,420m in their pockets. This is the government which suggests that people should be given the opportunity to determine the way in which they will spend their income. This is precisely the government which has denied them that opportunity.
Mr BRAITHWAITE (Dawson) (10.13)-1 speak tonight in full support of the proposition put during the adjournment debate last night by the honourable member for Kennedy (Mr Katter) on the subject of zone allowances. I speak for the same reasons as the honourable member for Kennedy and for the reason that I have made representations over many years to the treasurers of this Government. These representations were to ensure that on any change of the zone. allowance, equity was returned to those living in the inhospitable climatic parts of our continent where, besides the droughts, dust, floods and flies, costs of production and costs of living have escalated, and living standards have diminished in .comparison with those in the city.
This is why the .majority report is so disappointing. After a year of anticipation, since the report on zone allowances was commissioned, the expectations of those who have been responsible for the devolution of our industries and who develop our basic resources, have been crumpled and completely disregarded. Their report is basically worthless. It is not worthless because their conclusions and recommendations do not agree with what 1 felt would have been the result, but worthless because, upon the collection of so much evidence and in the face of so many obvious facts in the majority report, this evidence and these facts were not used. For instance, in acknowledging that zone allowances were introducedin 1945 and that geographical boundaries were then set, one might have inquired as to whether they were politically or practically set to achieve a purpose.
Paragraphs 2.1 0 and 2.11 on page 3 of the report make assumptions and are unable to determine how boundaries were arrived at at the time of introduction. If the boundaries were practically set the development of industries and the spread of. population since 1945 must, of necessity, have called for some alteration to boundaries one way or the other. But the majority found that there should be no re-alignment of existing boundaries. 1 find this utterly incredible. The fact that a third

category has now been included, disregarding boundaries of any type whatsoever, and setting the criterion of a distance of 250 kilometres from a town with a population of 2,500 or more, is in conflict with the geographic boundaries concept that originally was introduced, So the majority report recommendation is neither one thing nor the other.
The base values retained in zones A and B have remained unchanged since 1958-59 except for the rebate conversion in 1975. There was no effort whatsoever to quantify the real value of the allowance or rebate. It is incomprehensible to suggest that, in an economy thrashed by inflation in the 1970s, with intervening booms and busts, those values have not been affected in any way except for the suggestion that urbanised areas with a population in excess of 25,000 should be downgraded. Obviously, the majority report is superficial and shallow. On the other hand, Mr Slater has tried to do something with the evidence and facts, and in what would not have been an exercise in ease he made a determined effort to bring forward constructive recommendations. 1 congratulate him on his effort. I note that at page 47 of the report Mr Kerr lends weight to the arguments of Mr Slater to an extent that one might consider their findings a majority report.
As member for Dawson 1 understand better than any other representative the conditions of my own electorate,the proper feelings of the people and the necessity' for zone allowances to restore some of the financial disadvantages of a taxation rebate. 1 refer to the new mining developments of the Bowen Basin. The arbitrary boundaries set in 1945 are not realistic for 1981. The unease of the people working in these areas found its outlet in the miners tax strike of 1980. The majority report neglects them in every way. The earners of Australia's primary wealth deserve some consideration.
1speak of an area where there are difficulties of distance, road construction, road maintenance and weather. The cost of telephone rentals in outback Australia is now at a premium of $500 per year. Television .is not bad; it is just non-existent. Insurance policies taken out by people living above the Tropic of Capricorn incur a surcharge because of storm and tempest and matters such as that. Some people in Australiacomplain about water salinity. We just do not have the resources to overcome the problems.
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Millar)~ Order! The honourable member's time has expired.

time for the English language to undergo some of the reforms that have been achieved in other languages is long overdue. Again, I seek leave to incorporate in Hansard a document prepared as a Press release, in which the spelling reform one spellings are used.

Leave granted.

The document read as follows-

On Tuesday, September I, The Spelling Action Society

(S.A.S.) will be 10 years old. In this short lifetime this move-

ment has hundreds of members around the world, more than

eny previous movement to reform spelling. It has as patrons

two Nobel Prize winners who are also Fellows of the Royal

Society, Sir Mark Oliphant the former South Australian

Governor and Sir Macfarlane Burnet, the 'father of

immunology'.

.

SAS is also unique in that the oldest-established spelling reform movement, patronized by the Duke of Edinburgh, has recognized and promoted SR I as part of its program. SRI or spelling reform one in the SAS program may be defined in the quatrain:
Eny clear short vowel sed as in 'hemorrhage' and 'led' may be red as 'e' insted of a spelling more widespred.

The dozen alternative spellings now current for this simple sound are illustrated in the 12 words of a less rational quatrain:
Grenwich frend ses Thems gest sed hemorrhaging lepard beried meny hefers ded.
Some 10 years ago I asked the then Minister for Education on notice if he would investigate the possibility of approving the use of SR I as an alternative to the standards of such . august authorities as the Oxford or Webster's dictionaries or the Public Service Style Manual. He got opinions from several literati. A few were in favour. Most tcnded to produce the usual superficial objections, such as the need to preserve fossilized spellings to help students of etymology rather than make spelling easier for beginners. Harry Lindgrcn had demolished these specious points in his 1969 Alpha paperback: Spelling Reform: A New Approach. A few language professionals suggested, obviously without having contemplated Lindgren's reasons for choosing SR I, that a better first step in spelling reform would be to replace ph by f, ignoring the fact that this would require exceptions to the rule or a second change away from f in several words including nephew, uphold, sapphire, off, rime and an extra rule for rough, cough etc. The future changes so necessitated would cause difficulties unless first a rule similar to SR I had become well established to distinguish the vowel sounds of rille and rime, and the vowel sounds of Navy and Navvy, and a similar rule was adopted for the v sounds of nephew and of. In other words, the expert writers were not expert in step-by-step reform based on English speech sound units insted of on existing spelling complexities.
In its first decade SR I has been used in publiShed anthologies, a novelette and a government discussion booklet which I issued on community helth policies for the A.C.T. SRI has been adopted as standard by editors of journals, including one on educationalresearch and one organ of a teachers' union. The Sydney Morning Herald's Education Supplement has advocated SR I, and it is used on request by virtually every major daily paper in Australia for letterS to the editor concerning spelling reform.
Governments might well consider authorizing their employees, including teachers, to use SR I as an alternative to more widespred standard spellings. This is not a radical idea since leading dictionaries commonly show alternative spellings for one wordLespectally a word for which Webster, and Americans generally prefer a more logical spelling than one

first standardized by Dr Johnson, the 'father' of dictionary makers. No-one now spells 'debt' the way the French root of the word is spelt: 'dette'. The reason is that the dominant writer of his age, Dr Johnson, decided we ought to put in a 'b' to remind us of the earlier Latin root 'debit-'. Such spelling fossils are as useless as a whipholder on the dashboard of a 1981 model horseless carriage.
Interested Members and Senators should send $5 to join SAS, 40 McKinlay Street, Narrabundah, A.C.T. They will get Lindgren's book, a rubber stamp with the SR 1 rule for use on documents where they use SRI, and 12 issues of the monthly newsletter Spelling Action.

May I acknowledge the remarks of the honourable' member for Perth (Mr McLean) with respect to the Community Youth Support Scheme and make a few remarks of my own in reply. The first point to be made is that this was a decision by the Government. I think the honourable member said that it was a decision by the Department of Employment and Youth Affairs. Of course, all decisions are decisions of the Government, and this was one such decision. Secondly, the honourable member maintained that no thanks had been ex" pressed in the communication to which he referred to those associated with and working for some CYSS projects. I want to place on record that the Government acknowledges the work done by people who have worked in the CYSS area. We have made it plain on a number of occasions--

Answers to Questions

The following answers to questions were circulated:

Uranium
(Question No. 401)
Mr Hurford asked the Minister for Trade and Resources, upon notice, on 24 February 1981:
(I) Is the government able to terminate all mining and sales of uranium, permanenty, indefinitely or for a specific period, as recommended by the Fox Report; if so, would the Government be liable to compensation claims for the action.
(2) What was the specific nature of the doubts concerning the transfer of the Government's interest in the Ranger project to Energy Resources of Australia that necessitated the insertion of sub-section 41 (2AA) in the Atomic Energy Act 1953.
(3) Could the Government be implicated, in any way, as party to commercial contracts for sale of uranium arising from the sub-section 41 (2AA) amendment to the Atomic Energy Act 1953.
Mr Anthony-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Under Standing Orders, questions should not ask Ministers for legal opinion. I am advised that any answer to this question would require expression of opinion on a matter of law.
(2) In the course of finalising some aspects of the commercial documentation relating to the formation of Energy Resources of Australia Ltd (ERA), legal counsel, acting for some of the Australian commercial parties involved, queried whether the arrangements for the continuing operation of the Ranger project envisaged between the Commonwealth and

ERA as reflected in the divestment documentation would be 'on behalf of the Commonwealth' within the meaning 6f section 41 of the Atomic Energy Act and the Authority which was to be assigned to ERA. The Government examined the matter and decided that the Atomic Energy Act should be amended to put the matter beyond doubt, so that operations at Ranger clearly will continue to be 'on behalf of the Commonwealth'. The amendment was inserted in the Act as sub-section 41 (2AA).
(3) No.

Exports of Coal and Aluminium
(Question No. 403)
Mr Uren asked the Minister for Trade and Re. sources, upon notice, on 24 February 1981:

(I) For the commodities of (a) coal and (b) aluminium what were the (i) volumes and (ii) values of exports from each State and from Australia as a whole (A) in total and (B) to each country or region of destination in each year from 1960-61 to 1979-80.
(2) What are the projected (a) volumes and (b) values for each year to 1989-90.
Mr Anthony-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) The Australian Statistician has supplied the following information:
Table I-Exports of coal by State for the years 1960-61 to 1979-80 inclusive.
Table 2-Exports of coal by country of consignment for the years 1960-61 to 1979-80 inclusive.
Table 3-Exports of aluminium and alloys, unworked, for the years 1960-61 to 1979-80 inclusive (by State for the years 1960-61 to 1968-69 thereafter not available for publication) .
Table 4-Exports of aluminium and alloys, unworked, by country of consignment for the years 1960-61 to 1979-80 inclusive.
Figures in the tables have been rounded and discrepancies may therefore occur between sums of component items and totals.
Statistics on coal, and aluminium and alloys unworked, on a country by State basis have not been provided because:
(i) records containing data for years prior to 1966-67 on a . State \>y country basis are no longer available;
(ii) from 1969-70 State details of the export of aluminium and alloys, unworked, are subject to confidentiality provisions and are therefore unavailable; and
(iii) data for the years 1966-67 to 1979-80 on a State by country basis, other than those outlined in (ii) above, are not readily available.

Attorney-General's Department: Special Arrangements for Disabled Persons

(Question No, 470)

Mr Holding asked the Minister representing the Attorney-General, upon notice, on 24 February 1981:

( I) [n view of the fact that 1981 is designated' as the Inter-
national Year of Disabled Persons, what special arrangements has the Attorney-General's Department made to assist disabled persons. '

(2) Do any special arrangements made include (a)

increased employment of disabled pel'sonsand (b) improved

access for disabled persons to, any' building or other facility

occupied by the Attorney-General's Department; if so, what

are the details.

(3) What budgetary allocations were mad~ in 1980/81 to

facilitate any special arrangements for disabled persons in the

Attorney-General's Department.

'

Mr Viner-The Attorney-General has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:

Answers to Questions

27 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 959

(I) to (3) I refer the honourable member to the answer given by the Prime Minister to Parliamentary Question No. 458 (Hansard,IOJune 1981).
Funding of Private Schools
(Question No. 700)
Mr Dawkins asked the Minister for Education, upon notice, on 4 March 1981:
(I) Is it a fact that, in 1976 (a) 6% of private schools received more finance from State and Federal Government grants than they spent on education and (b) 56% of private schools received more than 80% of their expenditure from State and Federal grants.
(2) How many private schools receive more than (a) 80% and (b) 100% of their expenditure from State and Federal Government sources in each State since 1976.
Mr Fife-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) and (2) The figures quoted in part I (a) and (b) appear in the April 1978 Schools Commission Report for the triennium 1979-81 and were based on 1976 data. In assessing school expenditure for these purposes, no allowance has been made for the payment of interest or repayment of principal in respect of loans for capital purposes, nor for the costs of making provision for future commitments or for expenditure on such capital items as furniture and equipment. All of these costs must be met, at least in part, from a school's cash income.
Surveys conducted by the Commission indicated that, in 1977, some 7% of non-government schools spent less on the provision of recurrent resources than they received from State and Commonwealth Government recurrent grants, while 55% of non-government schools received recurrent grants from Government sources totalling more than 80% of their recurrent expenditure. The equivalent proportions in 1979 were 6% and 58% respectively. Comparable figures are not available for other years.
If debt servicing is taken into account, the great majority of non-government schools (that is those in the Catholic sector) provide on average more than one-third of total expenditure from their own efforts, including the estimated value of contributed services; the remaining two-thirds being provided in the form of grants from Commonwealth and State Governments. These schools have on average recurrent resource levels about 30 per cent below those of the average governmentschool.
Other non-government schools receive on average less than one-third of their recurrent expenditure in the form of Government grants.
After taking into account debt servicing and the other factors mentioned above, the majority of the remaining schools which appear to spend less on the provision of recurrent resources than they receive from State and Commonwealth recurrent grants are systemic schools. School systems receive block payments of grants in respect of all the schools in the system and every effort is made to ensure that system aggregate data reported to the Commonwealth Schools Commission are accurate.
However, the nature of systemic arrangements is such as to preclude exact allocations to each school. Services are often provided on a system-wide basis (e.g. remedial and advisory teachers) and systems often consolidate school fees, cope with bad debts, and pay salaries and associated costs, at a central level. In some instances, this has led to inconsistencies between income and expenditure figures as reported by individual schools. These inconsistencies balance out over the system

and no system has received in any year more Government grants than it spent in providing recurrent resources.
There remains a very small number of instances since 1976 in which a school has actually received more recurrent funds from Government sources than it spent in a particular year. Such instances have been confined to aboriginal community schools in which substantial enrolment variations from year to year may create surpluses through unexpected increases in enrolments and hence in grants paid on a per capita basis. Adjustments are made in subsequent years to take account of such surpluses.
In my statement to Parliament on 4 June I announced that the Government has accepted the Commonwealth Schools Commission's recommendation that, from 1982 onwards, the total disposable income available to a school from private sources will be considered by the Commission in its assessment of schools or systems for subsidy proposes. By relating income to expenditure, the Commission believes that a better appreciation of a school's overall financial situation can be obtained.

Non-Systemic Schools
(Question No, 2612)
Mr Dawkins asked the Minister for Education, upon notice, on 10 June 1981 :

960 REPRESENTATIVES 27 August 1981

Answers to Questions

( I) Did the Schools Commission's Report for 1982-84 state, at paragraph 12.30, that 38 per cent of non-systemic schools spent less than 75 per cent of their available income, after they had serviced all debts, on the provision of recurrent resources for students; if so, how was the remaining income used and was any of it derived from public sources.
(2) Was any of the financial assistance provided from public sources spent on purposes other than the purposes for which it was granted.
Mr Fife-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) The figures quoted in Part (I) appear in the March 1981 Commonwealth Schools Commission 'Report for the Triennium 1982-84,' at paragraph 12.30.
The Commission indicated earlier in the same paragraph that in using the term 'available income' it was referring to income from private sources only. Such income comprises tuitiQn fees, other receipts from students, church or parish grants, and other cash income from private sources for recurrent purposes; grants or donations for capital purposes from parish, parent organisations, building funds or bequests; proceeds from the sale of fixed assets; other capital cash receipts from private sources; and the value of contributed services of teachers and other professional staff.
In addition to meeting the cost of providing recurrent resources over and above those financed from Government grants, and meeting the cost of servicing all of the school debt, the income available to a school from private sources may be used in making provision for future commitments (for example, provision for future building projects), in meeting the cost of capital items not funded from government sources, in the reduction of working overdrafts, and, if the school is a boarding school, in financing any net deficit on the boarding operation.
I would point out that non-government schools are free to apply their income from private sources as they see fit. The Government has, however, accepted the Commonwealth Schools Commission's recommendation that, in future, the total disposable income available to a school from private sources should be considered by the Commission in making assessments for subsidy purposes. It is expected that this will help ensure that decisions regarding the level of Commonwealth funding to be granted to schools and systems will accurately rellect their overall financial situation. However, no non-government school will receive less than the basic Commonwealth grant of 20 per cent of average government schools standard costs.
(2) The accountability requirements attached to grants made by the Commonwealth Government to non-government schools and systems under States Grants (Schools Assistance) Acts have been satisfied in all cases; the funds were applied to the purpose for which they were granted. Accountability for funds granted by State Governments is the responsibility of the State Government concerned.

Mr Thomson-The Minister for Veterans' Affairs has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(1) The Repatriation Artificial Limb and Appliance Centres (R.A.L.A.Cs) manufacture a full range of artificial limbs and appliances. No other Government organisation manufactures or issues on behalf of the Department of Veterans' Affairs. The commercial limb contractors, which tend to service specific geographic regions, are expected to manufacture any limb for Which they receive a prescription. Appliance manufacture is not currently open to contract.

Greenslopes Repatriation Hospital, Queensland
(Question No. 2374)
Mr Humphreys asked the Minister representing the Minister for Veterans' Affairs, upon notice, on 28 May) 981:
(1) Is it a fact that staff employed in the pathology laboratory of the Greenslopes Repatriation Hospital, Queensland, are, re~uired to work permanent overtime.
(2) If so, (a) is the necessity for permanent overtime a cause of recent staff resignations in that laboratory, (b) in what other sections of the hospital is permanent overtime worked, (c) was permanent overtime introduced to combat the imposition of staff ceilings and (d) will the transfer of staff following the closure of the Rose!l1ount Repatriation Hospital, Queensland, ameliorate the overtime situation at Greenslopes.
Mr Thomson-The Minister for Veterans' Affairs has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:'
(I) No.
(2) (a) Refer to (1).
(b) Overtime is worked on a regular basis at the Repatriation General Hospital, Greenslopeo, in the following areas:
A regular rostered overtime shift is worked by the Transport Officers (Clerical Assistants, Grade 3) each Saturday morning between 8.00 'a.m. and noon to arrange transport for hospital patients.
In the Accounts Section, two clerks (a paying officer and a witnessing officer) are required to work on an overtime basis each payday to pay staff completing early shifts.
Overtime is worked every four weeks by two clerks, a clerical assistant and an accounting machinist, to process the payment of fees to visiting specialists. Payments are made to 120 specialists involved in servicing all the clinics at the Repatriation General Hospital, Greenslopes.
(c) No.
(d) The transfer of staff following the closure of Rosemount Repatriation Hospital will have no effect on overtime worked at the Repatriation General Hospital, Greenslopes.
Veterans' Affairs: Pensions
(Question No, 2407)
Mrs Darling asked the Minister representing the Minister for Veterans' Affairs, upon notice, on 3 June 1981:
(I) In assessing a'Yeteran's claim for pension, is a distinction made between (a) assessment and (b) entitlement claims.
(2) What ,is the stafldard procedure adopted in respect of both categories.
Mr Thomson-The Minister, for Veterans' Affairs has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(1) Yes.

(2) When a claim for acceptance of a disability as servicerelated is received, arrangements are made for the medical investigation of the condition claimed and all available relevant medical evidence and service records are obUlined.
When investigation of the claim is complete,' all the evidence is submitted in the first instance to a Repa\riation Board for determination. If the claim is rejected by the Board there exists a right of appeal to the Repatriation Commission. If the appeal is unsuccessful, the applicant may then apply to the Repatriation Review Tribunal for a revieYl of the Commission's decision.
If the applicant elects to appeal or apply for a review he or she may submit additional evidence which, together with all the other evidence, is made available to the determining authority. The applicant may also attend a Tribunal hearing in person and be assisted by an advocate.
Where a claim for acceptance of a disability is successful at Board level, the Board may make a determination on assessment at the same time. If the claim is successful at Commission or Tribunal hivel, further medical opinion is normally obtained on assessment and the case referred to a Board for determi'riation. If the claimant is dissatisfied with the decision on assessment he or she may apply to the Repatriation Review Tribunal for a review of that decision on assessment within three months of receipt of notification of that decision.
Where a veteran has an accepted'disability(ies) and considers that the incapacity has deteriorated since last assessed, an application may be made at any time for increase in the rate of pension. In these circumstances, further medical evidence is sought and the assessment of incapacity reviewed by a Repatriation Board. A right to apply to the Repatriation Review Tribunal for review of a Board determination as above also exists in these circumstances. The applicant may also submit further evidence in the same way as for an entitlement case, and attend a Tribunal hearing in person.
In all cases the applicant is advised of the decision and the reasons for it. .

Australian National Railways
(Question No. 2607)
Mr Morris asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 10 June 1981:
(1) What were the (a) labour operating costs, (b) nonlabour operating costs, excluding capital servicing costs, (c) capital servicing costs and (d) capital expenditure of the Australian National Railways Commission for each year from 1972-73 and what are the estimated figures for 1980-81.

(2) Of the total operating costs, excluding capital servicing costs, what percentage was paid to the private sector.
(3) What percentage of capital expenditure was paid to the private sector.
(4) -What was the import component of the (a) noncapital servicing operating costs and (b) capital expenditure.

(1) The Australian National Railways Commission has provided the information in the following table:

As 1977-78 was the first year of consolidated accounts of the former Commonwealth Railways, South Australian Railways (country lines) and the Tasmanian Government Railways, information for the years 1972-73 to 1976-77 is not available. In addition, the ratio of labour and non-labour for 1977-78 is estimated and is based on the ratio for 1978-79 as the precise figures are not known for that year, as the Commission did not assume full control of the transferred systems until I March 1978.
(2) The percentage of total operating costs, excluding capital servicing costs, paid to the private sector is not readily identifiable. However the Commission has advised that 100 per cent of fuel costs and an estimated 98 per cent of materials costs and 44 per cent of incidental expenditure are paid to the private sector.
(3) The percentage of capital expenditure paid to the private sector is as follows:
1977-78-89 per cent 1978-79-90 per cent 1979-80-91 per cent 1980-81-88 per cent
(4) There has been no import component of (a) noncapital servicing operating costs, and (b) capital expenditure for the years 1977-78 to 1980-81. However, while there have been no significant individual items imported, various purchases of equipment and stores from Australian companies would contain some components that would have been, of necessity, imported. This proportion is not known.
Consultancy Services
(Question No. 2628)
Mr Morris asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 10 June 1981:

' ( 1) What was the precise nature of the consultancy services being provided by Beca Carter, Hollings and Ferfler Pty Ltd at a cost of $126,250 under contract reference G FD 127 referred to on page 44 of the Commonwealth of Australia Gazette No. G 16 of 21 April 1981.
(2) Why was it found necessary to employ outside consultants rather than Departmental officers for the tasks involved.
Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) The decisions by the domestic carriers to introduce wide body jets on their routes brought forward the need to examine the broad options available for the development of Melbourne Airport. To facilitate this, the consultant was commissioned to study and report on the current and long term requirements for terminal facilities, car parking and associated road systems as well as long-term runway development strategy.
(2) The collection of data for this study, and the analysis involved, required the short-term services of a large number of specialists who are not available within my Department.
Allied War Veterans' Pensions
(Question No. 2644)
Mr Young asked the Minister representing the Minister for Veterans' Affairs, upon notice, onlO June1981:
(I) How, many applications for Allied War,veterans' pensions were (a) received and (b) granted in 1980 and during the period 1January to 9 June '!981.

968 REPRESENTATIVES 27 August 1981

Answers to Questions

(2) What was the country of birth and nationality of these applicants or in what armed forces have they served in each war.
(3) How many of the applicants served with (a) regular, (b) irregular or (c) partisan forces, by nationality in each case.
(4) How many partisans, and from what countries, were granted the Allied War veterans' pension during the same period as that referred to in part (I).
(5) How many persons are presently waiting for their pensions to be granted, by nationality, and what is the average waiting period between application and the granting of the pension.
(6) What sum was spent during the period referred to in part (I) on the Allied War veterans' pensions.
(7) What is the situation in relation to the payment of this pension to those who served with (a) the Greek partisans, (b) the Italian army, (c) Yugoslav'partisans, (d) Russian soldiers and partisans, (e) Vietnamese, (f) Kampucheans under the Pol Pot regime and (g) Afghanistan freedom fighters.
Mr Thomson-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) (a) The number of applications in 1980 from Allied veterans for service pension was 1,200. A further 338 applications were received from I January to 9 June 1981.
(b) In 1980,920 Allied veterans were granted service pensions and from I January to 9 June 1981 the number was 262.
(2) Statistics are not compiled on service pension applications on the basis of country of birth of applicant. Figures on applications, according to the nationality of the forces with which the applicant served, are provided in the following table for the period 7 February 1980 to 9 June 1981. All appl~ cations were in respect of World War" service, except for one Russian World War 1case.

(3) Statistics are not readily available on the nature of the service in the Allied Forces with which applicants served. The Repatriation Act 1920 requires that, to qualify for service pension, Allied veterans must have served in forces which were not 'irregular forces'.
(4) It is not known how many former partisans have been granted service pension.
(5) As at 9 June 1981 the number of applications undetermined was 97. The following table gives the distribution of outstanding applications according to the nationality of the forces with which the applicant served.

The average time between application and dispatch of notification for all service pension applications, regardless of whether or not pension is granted, is 49 days. A sepa{ate figure for Allied veterans' pensions is not available.
(6) A precise figure for the amount spent on service pensions to Allied veterans is not available but it is estimated that, for the period 7 February 1980 to 9 June 1981, an amount in the vicinity of $2.7m was paid in service pensions to Allied veterans and $1.6m in service pensions to their wives and widows.
(7) The general position with regard to the service pension eligibility of the forces in question is given below. Caution is needed in applying the information in particular cases as eligibility also depends on other factors.
(a) Former Greek partisans are ineligible for pension as they are considered to have comprised 'irregular' Allied Forces. However, Greek partisans who had prior service with the country's regular armed forces between the date of invasion, 28 October 1940, and the date of capitulation, 31 May 1941, may qualify for pension.
(b) Former members of the Italian Army in World War II would be eligible for pension if they served only in the period 8 September 1943 to the end of the European war on 8 May 1945. The Italian armistice with the Allies was signed on 8 September 1943 and from thatdate some Italian Forces fought with the Allies. Service with the Italian Army prior to 8 September 1943 would disqualify a veteran from pension on the grounds of service with a force which was at war with Australia at the time. Soldiers of the Italian Army in World War I would be eligible for pension, provided they did not also serve in the Italian Forces in World War II prior to 8 September 1943.
(c) Former Yugoslav partisans under Marshal Tito's command are eligible if they served on or after 22 December 1941, the date on which the Ist Proletarian Shock Brigade was proclaimed.
(d) Russian soldiers of World War II are eligible for pension if they served in one of the following periods:
(i) 21 June 1941, the start of the German offensive, to 8 May 1945; and
(ii) 8 August 1945, the date the USSR declared war on Japan, to 14 August 1945, the date the Japanese accepted surrender terms.
Russian soldiers of World War 1 are also eligible for pension.
The partisan movement in the USSR comprised 'irregular' Allied Forces and, thus, its followers do not qualify for pension.
(e) South Vietnamese servicemen who served during the Vietnam conflict would be eligible, but it is emphasised that, like other Allied veterans, they would have to meet the qualification often years Australian residency.

(f)-(g) Applicants for service pension on the basis indicated in part (7) (f)-(g) of the question would be ineligible.
The comments about eligibility in part (7) (b) to (e) are made subject to the veterans meeting all the other legisl\ltive criteria.

Ansett l,'ransport Industries Ltd

Mr Hunt-On 27 May 1981 (Hansard, page

2663), Mr Campbell asked me a question without

notice cencerning money alleged to be owing tp

the Department of Transport by Ansett

Transport Industries.

.

The following is the answer to the honourable member's question:
F~lIowing a review of rentals of domestic and international
terminals, Ansett Transport Industries in 1975-76 took writs out of the High Court and the Supreme 'Court of Victoria challenging the validity of the resulting increases. '

The Company has since made regular but partial payments 'without prejudice'to its legal position. Rental arrears did not at any stage approach $8m.

The matter has now been resolved and Ansett's rentals for terminals throughout Australia are fully paid at the rlltes'currently fixed by my Department. These, of course, reflect the Government's policy offull cost recovery of airport terminals.

While in the past lease agreements did not provide for payment of interest on arrears of rentals, this matter has now been rectified following consultations with the industry in the Aviation Industry Advisory Council.

Taxation Deductions
Mr Howard-On 26 February 1981 (Hansard, page 233), the honourable member for Barton asked me a question without notice about the effect on Commonwealth taxation reveQue of State and local government charges and imposts. The question was raised in the context of reports that the Sydney City Council was contemplating some form of levy, to be borne by developers, the proceeds of which would go to welfare housing. I undertook to see if I could obtain an assessment of the revenue effect.
As a matter of strict nomenclature, I note that where State and local government charges and imposts are properly paid by a business as business outgoings, and so are deductible under Section 51 (I) of the Income Tax Assessment Act, the payments are costs which are netted off gross income to arrive at assessable income. It is only in respect of concessions, such as the investment allowance or the 20 per cent loading on depreciation rates, that in the strict sense revenue costs or losses on any level of profits can be said to occur.
Nevertheless, State charges and imposts can and do affect Commonwealth revenue which would, Qbviously, be much higher, other things being equal, if those charges and imposts did not have to be paid and charged as business costs. I
However to measure the effect is not a simple matter because the available data on State and local government charges and imposts are not readily dissectable between payments by businesses and payments by private persons or exempt bodies. As well, some charges and imposts borne by private persons (private rates for example) can attract an income tax rebate where the taxpayer's total concessional expenditure exceeds $1590.
Against that background, and as a broad indicator only, I note that, in 1978-79, State and local governments collected $425 million by way of 'business' taxes (liquor taxes, road taxes, fire brigade contributions and business franchises); $1,529 million by way of pay-roll tax; and $3,296 million by way of other taxes borne by households and by businesses. The whole of the 'business' taxes, most of the pay-roll tax and a substantial proportion of the other taxes would be tax deductible. The additional revenue that would have resulted if those amounts had not been allowed as outgoings properly deductible under Section 51 (I) would probably have been in excess of $1 ,0OOm. I note also that State governments receive certain royalty payments from businesses. Along with land rent, these amounted to about $305m in 1978-79. There would be some further impact on Commonwealth revenues from that.
